# Secondhand Thoughts

# Chapter 1

The man at the turnip stall was going to leave his wife.

Not today. Probably not this year. But Anya Forger could hear him building up to it while she stood three stalls away pretending to examine pears, and the level of rehearsal was impressive in a miserable kind of way. He'd run through the speech four times since she'd been within range. Each version was worse. The current draft included the phrase "I deserve to feel alive," which — no. Nobody delivered that line out loud and left the conversation with any dignity. She almost wanted to help him edit.

*—and I'll say, listen, Gerda, we've had a good run but I think we both know that—*

She didn't help. She was working.

Berlint Central Market spread through three city blocks between the diplomatic quarter and the government district, a maze of canvas canopies and wooden stalls that thinned in autumn but never fully closed. The afternoon crowd was thick enough to be useful — tourists consulting pocket maps, pensioners comparing vegetable prices with the exhaustive seriousness of arms negotiators, mothers with children who ran at shin height and thought about nothing except wanting things. Anya held her position by the pear display and waited for her contact.

Turnip Man's rehearsal continued in the background, layered under a woman in a brown coat who was composing an indignant letter to her landlord about the mold in the bathroom. *It's been four months, Herr Dietrich, and I don't think it's unreasonable to—* Two stalls over, a teenager was replaying an argument with his mother and losing, even inside his own head. Behind Anya, a man was thinking about bratwurst with a focus that bordered on worship.

This was the noise. It was always the noise. Sixty, maybe seventy minds within range — roughly twenty meters in every direction, give or take — and every one of them talking at once, none of them aware anyone could hear. Anya couldn't turn it off. Hadn't been able to since she was four, when the first thought she'd ever registered was a nursery teacher's exhausted prayer for nap time. Whatever anyone within range was actively thinking arrived in her head, in their voice, at their volume, whether she wanted it or not.

She mostly didn't want it.

The contact was late. His codename was Kinglet — a mid-level courier, nothing remarkable, just a man who picked things up and left things behind and worried about it the entire time. Anya had been watching the dead-drop bench near the fountain for seventeen minutes, and in that time she'd learned that the pretzel vendor across the way had a sciatica problem he was managing with anger and a philosophy about the proper ratio of coarse salt to fine salt that she would never fully forget.

Somewhere in the crowd, faint and easily missed, a woman was thinking about her son — *If his unit gets redeployed to the eastern sector again, I swear—* The thought trailed into worry too diffuse to follow. Anya let it pass. Background static. Everyone was worrying about something.

Then Kinglet's thoughts entered range from the south. *Should've worn the grey jacket. The grey doesn't wrinkle. Why do I never — okay. Focus. The newspaper is on the bench. Walk naturally. Am I walking naturally? This doesn't feel natural.*

Standard courier anxiety. He was a nervous man doing his nervous job. Anya had worked with worse.

She scanned outward. The crowd noise was a wall of competing voices, but she'd been doing this long enough to know what she was listening for. Trained minds sounded different from the usual churn. They were organized, repetitive, and they stayed on target.

There. East side of the market, near the cheese stall.

*Subject approaching the bench. Hold position. Wait for visual confirmation of the pickup.*

The thought was crisp and male and utterly wrong for the setting. Nobody in a market thought in tactical present tense unless they were working.

She found the second one nine seconds later, threading through the crowd from the south. *East approach set. I'll take the exit route once he commits west. Awaiting signal.*

Two tails. Professional. They were positioned around the dead-drop bench, waiting for Kinglet to pick up the newspaper — the newspaper with WISE microfilm — and then they'd follow him back to wherever he was going, which was the diplomatic quarter, which was WISE, which was everything.

The pears blurred in front of her. Okay. Okay, think.

Kinglet was ten meters from the bench. He was still thinking about the grey jacket. He had absolutely no idea he was walking into a trap. He was going to sit down, pick up a newspaper full of intelligence, and lead two hostile surveillance professionals straight back to the heart of Westalian intelligence operations in Berlint.

Anya put the pear down. She'd been holding it for seventeen minutes and hadn't paid for it, but she also hadn't bitten it, so that was probably — it didn't matter. She started walking.

The first problem was communication. She had no earpiece to Kinglet. This was a passive surveillance assignment — she was there to observe the dead-drop and confirm the exchange, not to manage the handoff. She didn't have a signal protocol with him because she wasn't supposed to need one. The second problem was that she couldn't explain to anyone how she knew the contact was tailed, because the answer was *I read their minds* and that was a sentence she had never said out loud to anyone in her life.

So she'd improvise. She was bad at plans. She was genuinely good at the part where the plan was already ruined.

She cut between stalls, moving fast, angling toward the fountain. Behind her, Turnip Man's resolve was collapsing. *Maybe I won't mention the car. The car is a whole thing. If I bring up the car she'll—*

*Subject reaching for the newspaper.* The first tail, repositioning.

Anya moved faster.

She reached Kinglet just as he was bending toward the bench. She caught his elbow, laughed too loudly, and said, "Oh my God, I'm so sorry, I thought you were someone — you look exactly like my friend's — wow, that's embarrassing." She gripped his arm hard enough to make the point.

His thoughts were a siren of confusion: *What — who is — her hand is on my arm, this is not protocol, there is no contact protocol for—*

"Don't sit down," she said through her smile. "Walk with me right now. You've got two on you."

He went white. Not a little pale — actually white, the blood dropping out of his face like water through a drain. He was a courier. He filed paperwork and left newspapers on benches and occasionally met someone in a parking structure. This was not his job. His thoughts collapsed into a high, thin whine of panic that Anya had to push through like wading in mud.

She kept the smile, kept the grip, and steered him north into the densest part of the market, where the stalls pressed close and the crowd moved slow.

*She's intercepting the subject.* The first tail, sharp with alarm. *Is she an asset? Does she — move to secondary approach. North side.*

*I've lost visual. The crowd is — damn it.* The second tail, already behind.

Anya read them both and steered. The first was going to loop around the spice stalls to cut them off from the north. She turned left, pulling Kinglet into a narrow gap between a bakery stand and a man selling secondhand books who was thinking, with great bitterness, about a woman named Elke.

"Keep up," she murmured.

"What is happening?" His voice shook. He was twenty years older than her and his hands were trembling and hers were steady and she didn't have time to feel anything about that asymmetry right now.

*There — north side, between the canopies. I've got a partial—* The first tail had caught a glimpse. His mind was clicking through options with professional speed: *Pursue through the stalls? Too narrow. Circle to the west exit and intercept—*

West exit. Anya went east.

"Why are we—"

"Because they're expecting west."

"How do you—"

"I'm good at reading situations."

She pulled him through a row of vegetable crates. Behind them, the first tail was committing to the west exit, his thoughts already there — calculating arrival time, line of sight, where to position for interception. He was good. He was also going to be standing at the wrong exit in about forty seconds.

The second tail was trying to close from the south, but the crowd had thickened — a knot of tourists had stopped to photograph a flower stall, blocking the aisle like a clot in an artery. Anya read his frustration as he shouldered through and used the gap. She steered Kinglet past a display of jarred honey, through a service lane between stalls that smelled like overripe apples and cardboard, and out through a gap she'd noticed on her way in.

A fruit cart. Wooden, heavy, loaded with late-season plums. A boy of about sixteen was leaning against it, thinking about a girl named Sofie with a level of romantic anguish that was almost operationally distracting.

Anya bumped the cart with her hip as she passed. Not hard — just enough to shift it, to send a cascade of plums tumbling across the cobblestones behind them and create a mess that anyone following would have to navigate.

The boy's thought exploded: *The plums — oh shit, oh shit, the plums, my uncle is going to—*

The second tail: *Obstruction. I've lost — completely lost. They could be anywhere in—*

The first tail, arriving at the west exit and finding nobody: *She took him east. She read us. How did she—*

That last thought landed in Anya's range as she and Kinglet emerged on the western edge of the market, cutting north toward the street that fed into the diplomatic quarter. Once they crossed into the Westalian mission's orbit, pursuit became a political problem that no professional tail wanted to create. The tails would peel off. They always did, at that line.

Anya let go of Kinglet's arm.

Her head was throbbing. She'd been reading forty-plus minds at full intensity for almost twenty minutes, and the noise had been stacking up behind her eyes like pressure from a fever. She could feel the fatigue in her arms and legs — a specific heaviness that had nothing to do with running and everything to do with the fact that her brain had been doing something it was never designed to do at a scale that ground her down every time. She'd need to stop soon. Sit somewhere quiet. Let the noise thin.

But not yet.

Kinglet was leaning against a lamppost, breathing through his mouth. His jacket was wrinkled — the wrong jacket — and his forehead was slick. His thoughts were a wreckage of relief and leftover terror and a vivid, specific desire to never do this again.

"You're fine," Anya told him. She pushed her hair back from her face — a gesture she'd had since childhood, hands near her ears, unconscious — and repeated it. "You're fine. We're clear."

The microfilm was still on the bench. The newspaper, unrecovered. That was a loss, but a recoverable one — the dead-drop site was burned now, but the intelligence could be rerouted through another channel.

Kinglet looked at her with an expression she'd seen before on people she'd pulled out of bad situations — gratitude layered over something less comfortable, the awareness that a woman twelve years his junior had just handled a crisis he'd frozen inside.

"Thank you," he said.

"Don't use that dead-drop site again."

"I won't."

"And next time, wear the grey jacket. It doesn't wrinkle."

His thought: *How does she—*

But she was already walking away, and he was already out of range, and she was very, very tired.

---

Forty minutes later, in a room that smelled like institutional carpet and coffee that had outlived any reasonable definition of freshness, Anya sat in the third row of a WISE briefing and tried not to read anyone's mind for five consecutive minutes.

She failed before she finished the thought.

*—if this runs past six I'll miss the opening number. She's been practicing the Chopin for three weeks, she's got the dynamics down now, and if I'm not there in the front row she'll—* That was Reinhardt, two seats to her left. Analyst, mid-forties, reliable, entirely consumed with the fact that his seven-year-old daughter Marta had a piano recital tonight and he had promised, with the specific gravity that promises to seven-year-olds carry, that he would be there.

*Eggs. Bread. Something for dinner. Did she say laundry soap? I don't think we need — actually, do we have soap?* Analyst Brewer, assembling a grocery list with the uncertainty of someone who lived alone and had not opened her own cupboards with conviction in some time.

*—the line of her jaw when she's making a point. She doesn't know she does that thing with her glasses, does she? When she pushes them up and then—* Berg, who was sitting directly behind Anya and who was, for the sixth consecutive month, failing to conceal a crush on their handler that radiated from his mind like a space heater. Anya had endured this since spring. She knew more about what Berg found attractive about Sylvia Sherwood than any person alive, including Berg, and she would have paid real money to stop knowing.

The briefing room was on the third floor of a building in the Westalian diplomatic quarter that, from the outside, looked like a modest commercial property. Inside, it was fluorescent lights and ventilation hum and the particular carpet that government offices shared across every nation and era — beige, resilient, hostile to personality. The coffee on the credenza had been brewed that morning by Sylvia, who did not replace a pot before 1600 hours because wasting coffee offended a principle she had never articulated and that no one in the department had ever been brave enough to question.

Sylvia stood at the front of the room in a grey blazer and a collared shirt that together said *I am here to work and you should be too*. Reading glasses on the chain around her neck. Short grey-blonde hair that she cut, Anya suspected, at the same place on the same schedule every six weeks, because Sylvia did not adjust things that were functioning. Her face was sharp in the way of someone who had been making difficult decisions in rooms like this one for thirty years and had stopped apologizing for any of them.

"The pattern is consistent with covert operational activity, not standard political maneuvering," Sylvia said. Her diction was precise — she did not use contractions when she briefed, and her sentences arrived pre-structured, situation-assessment-directive, as if she had formatted them before opening her mouth. "We are looking at a structured campaign inside Ostania's security apparatus. The scope suggests an organized effort with specific objectives and an active timeline."

She touched the map pinned to the briefing wall. Red markers clustered near the Ostanian government district, east of center.

"The operative or operatives are unknown. The purpose is unknown. What we do know is this: the activity pattern is consistent with pre-conflict positioning. Weapons procurement channels that had been dormant for several years have been reactivated. Military intelligence sharing between Ostanian departments has been restricted in a manner that suggests compartmentalization for a specific project." She paused, and the pause was calibrated — she let it breathe for exactly long enough to register as serious without tipping into theatrical. "We do not yet have a clear picture of the objective. But the indicators are serious enough to warrant dedicated investigation."

*I wonder if there'll be parking near the recital hall.* Reinhardt, already drifting.

*—and the way she holds a pen, just the way she—* Berg. Hopeless.

From the back of the room, quieter, in the measured cadence of a mind that stayed organized even when it was cynical: Gerald Falk was thinking.

*Threat assessments calibrated to budget cycles. Same as last quarter, same as the one before. Inflate the numbers to justify the operational allocation, deflate when the appropriations review calls for discipline. Sherwood presents it as intelligence and it is intelligence, but the framing — the framing is always set to whatever posture keeps the lights on. Everyone in this room knows this. Nobody says it.*

Anya's attention snagged on Falk for half a second. He sat near the back row — senior analyst, mid-fifties, the kind of man who blended into any institutional setting like he'd been issued with the furniture. Thinning hair, reading glasses, a cardigan that was either brown or grey depending on the light. He'd been at WISE longer than most of the people in this room had been alive. He'd attended Anya's Eden Academy graduation when she was twelve, standing near the back with her father, and afterward had given her a fountain pen — silver, heavy, with her initials. She'd kept it. She wasn't sure where it was now, but she'd kept it.

His thought was cynical, but it was the specific cynicism of a man who'd watched institutional machinery from the inside for three decades and could hear the gears grinding. Anya had caught similar sentiments from half the senior staff at one point or another — the low-grade skepticism of people who believed in the work but had lost patience with the theater around it. She let the thought pass like weather and turned back to Sylvia.

"Forger." Sylvia's gaze found her with the directness of someone who did not scan a room but targeted a specific person. "You will take point on identification and assessment. Your diplomatic cover provides access to Ostanian state functions. Use it. I want a preliminary assessment within ten days."

*She always picks Forger for the exposed ones.* That was Berg, with a thought that was equal parts admiration and resentment and entirely not Anya's concern.

"Understood," she said.

*Should I bring flowers to the recital? Is that too much for a seven-year-old? No — she'd love it. Marta would love flowers.* Reinhardt, who had already checked out of the briefing emotionally and was now fully inhabiting the evening ahead of him.

Anya bit the inside of her cheek and did not smile.

The briefing ended with Sylvia collecting her notes in the precise order she always collected them — map last, folded into thirds. People stood, gathered their files, began the small post-meeting exchanges that were half professional and half procrastination. Sylvia left the room without lingering, because Sylvia did not linger.

Anya had caught Sylvia's thoughts about her during the briefing, as she always did: *Solid under pressure. Her field judgment has been reliable for two years. She reminds me of Loid at his sharpest — the instinct for the right read in the wrong moment.* The assessment was warm in a contained, institutional way that was as close to affection as Sylvia's professional register permitted.

Anya stood and felt the headache from the market settle more firmly behind her right eye. The fluorescent lights were needles. She wanted, suddenly and specifically, to be in a room with no one else in it. But her father was waiting for her at Cafe Linden, and there was nowhere within twenty meters of anywhere in Berlint that didn't have someone thinking in it.

---

Cafe Linden was a narrow room with dark wood tables and a window that hadn't been cleaned since the previous season. It occupied a side street between the diplomatic quarter and the residential neighborhoods south of center — close enough to WISE to be convenient, far enough to feel like somewhere else. Anya and Loid had been meeting here for two years. The waiter knew their order and had long ago stopped pretending he hadn't noticed they were always slightly too alert for a father and daughter having coffee.

Loid was already seated when she arrived. Coffee untouched, a cherry strudel on the plate across from him — her favorite since she was eight. He never ordered one for himself. She'd asked about this once and he'd said he wasn't hungry, which had been a lie, because his thought at the time was *She runs too hard and she doesn't eat enough*, and the lie was so small and so fatherly that she'd let it sit.

He looked up when she came in. Forty-four. Blond hair with grey at the temples that had accelerated over the past two years in a way neither of them mentioned. Reading glasses folded beside his coffee cup — he needed them for menus now, and for small print, and the first time she'd seen him put them on something had shifted in her understanding of time that she hadn't been ready for. His face was kind in a way that was both genuine and the residue of a lifetime of cover identities, though by now the distinction between the real warmth and the performed warmth had dissolved so thoroughly that even Anya, reading him, couldn't always find the seam.

"How was the market?" he asked.

"Eventful." She sat down and pulled the strudel toward her. "Dead-drop was burned. Two tails on Kinglet, coordinated, somebody professional. I pulled him out through the east side and looped west to the diplomatic zone."

His face didn't change. His thoughts did.

*Two tails. She was alone. She handled two professional tails in a crowd-density environment without backup or—* The analytical thread caught on something beneath it, and the assessment wavered. *She was alone. Twenty meters of range and forty civilians between her and hostile surveillance and she—* He caught himself. Redirected. *East side was the right play. The crowd density in the market's interior gave her natural cover that the tails couldn't overcome without closing distance, and she used the diplomatic quarter as an extraction boundary. Sound operational logic.* The spy's evaluation, clean and precise, laid over the father's alarm like a fresh coat of paint.

"Smart read on the exit route," he said. "The diplomatic zone boundary was the right lever to pull. They'd have had to risk an incident to follow."

"That was the idea." She took a bite of strudel. It was good. It was always good here.

"We'll reroute the recovery through an alternate channel. The microfilm isn't time-sensitive." He took a sip of coffee with the unhurried precision of a man who measured his gestures the way other people measured their words. "What about the new assignment?"

"Sylvia gave me something this afternoon. Unauthorized operation inside Ostania's government — somebody running a structured covert campaign, purpose unknown. The pattern looks like pre-conflict positioning. Weapons procurement channels coming back online, compartmentalized intelligence sharing."

He set his coffee down. The gesture was careful, which meant he was interested.

*Pre-conflict positioning. That's Sherwood's framework for—* His thought went fast, mapping Ostanian political factions, military procurement networks, historical precedents from the last border escalation. The operational mind that had made him the best field agent WISE had ever run was engaged now, building a tactical picture with the speed of someone who had been doing this longer than Anya had been alive. But threaded through the analysis, pushing up between the clean professional assessments: *She'll be at state functions. SSS provides security at every major Ostanian government event. If Yuri Briar's detail is working those rooms and she's—*

He stopped the thought. His eyes stayed on his coffee.

"You'll need sustained access to Ostanian government functions," he said. "Your attaché cover is solid, but repeated presence at high-level events draws pattern recognition. You'll want to vary your attendance — some cultural, some diplomatic, some social. Give them a profile that looks enthusiastic rather than strategic."

"Boring but visible."

"Exactly." A small smile. Anya read the thought behind it — *She's sharp. She's sharp and she's young and she's going into places I can't follow her and—* and then, abruptly, the thought cut off. Not trailed away. Cut. He'd caught himself going somewhere he didn't want to go, somewhere about her age and what he'd lost at twenty and what this work took from people, and he'd killed the thought like closing a door.

She looked at the strudel on the plate in front of her and busied herself with her fork. This was the arrangement, though only one of them knew it existed. He worried about her in the privacy of his own mind, and she let him believe it was private, because telling him otherwise would mean explaining everything, and explaining everything was the one thing she had never been able to do.

"I'll be careful," she said. She was answering a thought he hadn't spoken, and she'd gotten very good at making that look like normal conversation.

They talked for another twenty minutes. He suggested specific diplomatic events that would give her access to the Ostanian apparatus without overexposing her. He mentioned security protocols at state functions. He said that Yor was trying a new stew recipe and that she should come home for dinner soon, and the sentence was simultaneously a father's invitation and an operative's wellness check and she couldn't untangle the two, and she didn't want to, because the tangled version was the true one.

They sat for a while after that. The operational conversation wound down and something quieter replaced it — the version of them that existed in the gaps between work, where he was just her father and she was just his daughter and neither of them was particularly good at knowing what to do with that.

"How's the apartment?" he asked.

"Fine. The radiator makes a noise."

"What kind of noise?"

"A sort of... ticking? When it comes on."

"That's the pipe expanding. It's normal." He said this with the absolute certainty of a man who had once maintained six separate cover identities across two countries and who now, in semi-retirement, channeled the same operational precision into home repair. "If it starts banging, that's a different issue. Call the building manager."

"Okay."

"Or call me. I can look at it."

"You'd drive forty minutes to look at my radiator."

"I'd drive forty minutes to make sure your radiator isn't going to rupture a pipe in the middle of the night, yes." His face was straight. His thought was not: *She's living alone. She insists on living alone. This is the correct developmental step for a twenty-year-old, I know this, I have read the—* and then the thought dissolved into something less structured, less analytical, just a father's formless awareness that his daughter was out in the world and he couldn't follow her there.

"I'll call if it bangs," she said.

"Good." He picked up his coffee cup, found it empty, and set it down again. "Your mother's making stew tonight. New recipe. You should come by sometime this week."

The way he said *new recipe* carried a warning she didn't need telepathy to decode. Yor's culinary experiments were acts of love that frequently crossed the line into acts of violence against ingredients. But the stew was real and the invitation was real and the kitchen would smell like burning and hope, and she missed it.

"Maybe Thursday," she said.

She paid for the coffee because he always forgot to bring cash. He didn't forget. It was part of the routine — one of a hundred small rituals that had accumulated over fourteen years of a cover family that stopped being cover somewhere along the way.

Outside, Berlint was going amber. Early autumn evening, the sun low enough to turn the west-facing windows into sheets of dull gold. The air had a crisp edge — summer's warmth gone, winter still distant, the city held in the kind of cool that made you walk faster without deciding to. Leaves on the cobblestones. A tram rattling down Gartenstraße, its bell sounding twice as it rounded the corner.

Anya walked south toward home.

The thoughts came without effort. She was tired, and tired meant the filter she'd spent twenty years building was thinner — the range didn't shrink but the noise got louder, less organized, a layered hum of voices she hadn't asked to hear.

A woman closing her shop, pulling down the metal shutters with practiced rhythm. Her thoughts matched the repetition: *Elsa hasn't called. She always calls on Tuesdays and it's Wednesday now and she hasn't called and the news said there were delays at the eastern checkpoints and if something happened they'd tell me, wouldn't they? Someone would call?* The sister lived near the border. Anya walked past and the thought faded behind her.

A student coming the other direction, headphones in but the music wasn't working — his thoughts were louder than whatever he was listening to. *If tensions get worse and they reinstate conscription — do university exemptions still hold? They used to. Lehmann said they did but Lehmann failed civics—* He was nineteen, maybe twenty. He was thinking about a draft that didn't exist yet with the specific dread of someone who'd grown up hearing about the last one.

A shopkeeper locking up early — his thoughts had the tired, calculating quality of someone running numbers that wouldn't add up. *If the eastern trade routes stay restricted another month, the cost on imported stock alone — and Frau Dittmar wants her order by Friday and I don't have — maybe I call Korsch, see if he can source from the southern—* He was solving a logistics problem that was really a political problem that was really a fear he couldn't name. The border wasn't just an anxiety for Berlint's citizens. It was an accounting entry.

An old man on a bench. His mind was moving slowly, settling, the way water calmed after a stone dropped through it. He was thinking about pigeons and the cold and a woman named Ilse who had been dead for a long time. The grief was so worn it was almost texture — not sharp anymore, just there, like a knee that ached in weather. Anya didn't want to hear it. She heard it.

A couple walking ahead of her, close together. He was thinking about whether the restaurant on Müllerstraße would be crowded. She was thinking about her father's medication and the pharmacy closing early this week. Underneath both thoughts, unspoken between them but legible to Anya in tandem: the border. The news about the border. The delays. The rumors about troop movements that might be rumors or might not be.

Neither of them said any of it aloud. They talked about dinner and parking and whether Thursday worked for the dentist, and below the ordinary logistics of a shared life they were both quietly afraid in a way they hadn't been six months ago.

Anya read it all. She'd been reading all of it, all day, since she'd woken up in her apartment and caught her neighbor's thoughts about a leaking pipe before she'd finished brushing her teeth. The city's anxiety was a pressure system she could feel — diffuse, distributed, impossible for any single citizen to recognize as a pattern because each person only carried their own piece of it. But Anya heard them all, and the pattern was clear. More people thinking about the border this month than last. More people with a cousin in the military, a sister near the checkpoints, a news report sitting in the back of their minds like something they couldn't put down.

Berlint was getting nervous. The city didn't know it yet.

She turned onto her own street. Quieter here — a residential block, the crowd thinning to three or four minds within range. A neighbor watching television, his thoughts a muddy echo of whatever the program was saying. A child on an upper floor, drawing, thinking the colors: *Green. No, the darker green. The one that looks like trees.*

Anya slowed.

She stood on the sidewalk in the thinning autumn light and let the thoughts settle around her. The neighbor's drowsy attention. The child's greens. Somewhere near, a dog dreaming in short sensory pulses — warmth, a hand, food.

Every one of them believed their thoughts were their own. The inside of their heads was the one place in the world where no one could follow — the man on the bench with his dead Ilse, the student dreading a draft, the woman whose sister hadn't called. They all trusted, had to trust because the alternative was unbearable, that the privacy of their own minds was absolute and permanent and inviolable.

Anya had been inside them all. Every person she'd passed today. Every person she'd passed every day for twenty years. She'd heard the turnip vendor's sad rehearsal and the pretzel seller's sciatica complaints and Reinhardt's love for his daughter's piano playing and Falk's tired institutional cynicism and Loid's fear for her that he thought he'd hidden and every other unguarded, involuntary, unspeakable thing that the people around her carried in the one place they believed was safe.

She hadn't asked. She'd never asked. She couldn't ask, because asking meant telling, and telling meant — she didn't know what telling meant. She'd never done it. Not once, not ever, not to anyone. Not to her father, who thought his worry for her was private. Not to her mother, whose warmth and whose secrets Anya had been reading since childhood with the same undiscriminating clarity. Not to Sylvia, whose trust Anya heard every day and used every day and hadn't earned, not honestly, not the way Sylvia believed she had.

She was the most connected person in any room she entered and the most alone.

The child upstairs had settled on a green. The dog had shifted in its sleep. The street was quiet in the way that streets were quiet when you could hear what was underneath.

Anya went inside.


# Chapter 2

The car dropped her two blocks from the Foreign Affairs Ministry, which was standard practice — arriving by private car at an Ostanian state dinner drew less attention than arriving by diplomatic vehicle with Westalian plates. Anya paid the driver, stepped out onto the sidewalk, and stood for a moment in the autumn evening.

Berlint at dusk. The streetlights were coming on, warm against a grey sky that had been threatening rain since noon and hadn't delivered. Leaves skittered along the pavement in front of the Ministry's iron gates, and the air smelled like wet cobblestone and the faint diesel sweetness of the government car park beyond. Two blocks back, in the direction she'd come from, a woman was arguing with a child about whether they could get ice cream in October. The woman's thoughts — exasperated, guilty, already capitulating — reached Anya in the brief quiet before the gates.

She walked toward the entrance. Her credentials were in a slim wallet in her clutch: Marta Kramer, junior cultural attaché, Westalian diplomatic mission. The invitation had been arranged through legitimate channels — WISE had embedded her cover deep enough that an actual cultural attaché position existed in the mission's records, complete with a desk she occasionally sat at and a supervisor who thought she was competent but disorganized. The desk had a plant on it. She kept forgetting to water it.

Two SSS officers flanked the Ministry's main entrance. The taller one checked her credentials against a printed guest list while the shorter one watched her face with the specific blankness of someone trained to observe without appearing to. She read the taller one without trying: his thoughts were about his daughter. Math homework. Division. She kept getting the remainders wrong, and he was going to miss dinner again because of this posting, and his wife would be the one to explain it, and he resented that more than the cold.

"Attaché Kramer." He returned her papers. "Welcome. Through the main gallery, second doors on your left."

"Thanks so much."

She stepped through the entrance into the heated foyer, and the noise hit.

Not the audible kind — that was still muffled behind the gallery doors, a low hum of conversation and string music. The other kind. Two hundred minds, give or take, pressing against the limits of her skull like a headache that hadn't decided where to settle. Surface thoughts: fragments, loops, half-formed sentences. The quality of crowd telepathy was less like eavesdropping on individual conversations and more like standing in a train station where every announcement played simultaneously in a different language. You could tune toward one signal, but the background never disappeared. It just became a texture — a constant neural static that she'd been living with since she was old enough to understand that other people didn't hear it.

She adjusted her posture, smoothed the front of her dress — dark blue, appropriate for a junior cultural attaché who wanted to look serious but not ambitious — and walked through the gallery doors into the ballroom.

The Ostanian Foreign Affairs Ministry did state dinners the way it did everything else: heavy curtains, too many chandeliers, and an atmosphere that suggested you should be grateful to be here. The room was long and high-ceilinged, lit in that particular warm gold that made marble floors look like honey. Round tables with white linens. A string quartet near the windows playing something vaguely Baroque that nobody was listening to. Waitstaff circulating with silver trays — champagne, wine, something non-alcoholic in a frosted pitcher that no one was touching. The Ministry's own catering, which meant the food would be technically correct and entirely forgettable.

The heat was welcome after the autumn air outside, but it did something unpleasant to the telepathic noise — warmth and alcohol and proximity made people's thoughts looser, louder, less contained. She could feel the room as a pressure against the back of her skull, a low-grade throb that would become a proper headache within two hours if she didn't manage it. She'd learned management. You couldn't stop the noise, but you could change your relationship to it — let it become ambient, like traffic sounds, filtering for anomalies rather than trying to process every signal. The alternative was drowning, and she'd drowned in crowds before, in her first year at WISE, standing in a Berlint market with her hands over her ears and tears running down her face because she couldn't make it stop. Loid had found her in a doorway. He'd thought she'd had a panic attack. She'd let him think that.

She took a champagne glass from a passing tray and began to work.

Working a room, for Anya, meant running two operations simultaneously in the same body. Track one: social navigation. Move through clusters, introduce yourself, smile, ask questions about cultural programming and bilateral arts funding that were boring enough to be invisible but specific enough to seem informed. Track two: listening. Not to what people said. To what they thought.

She approached a circle of mid-level officials near the bar, and the deputy minister who'd met her at a reception two weeks ago — Werfel, a soft-faced man with kind eyes and a permanent crease of worry between his brows — recognized her and turned.

"Attaché Kramer, isn't it? Good to see you again."

"Deputy Minister Werfel. How's your daughter? The cello recital was this week, wasn't it?"

His face opened. He was pleased. His thoughts confirmed it — *she remembered, that's — Elena would love that someone asked* — and for a moment he was just a father at a work event, happy that a near-stranger had bothered to recall a detail about his child.

"She was wonderful. Well, she missed a note in the Handel, but she covered it beautifully. She's got her mother's composure." He paused, swirling the wine in his glass. "Are you finding the cultural attaché role interesting? I know Berlint's offerings can be... idiosyncratic."

"I'm learning," she said, which was true in ways he couldn't imagine. "The National Gallery exhibition last month was actually fascinating. I didn't expect the Ostanian modernists to be that political."

"Art is always political here," he said, and his thought underneath — *everything is political here, that's the whole problem* — carried a weariness she recognized from dozens of officials she'd read over the past two years. People who served the state because they believed in something and stayed because the alternative felt worse.

She lingered with his group for a few minutes, letting the conversation carry her while she scanned the surrounding minds. Most of what she caught was dinner-party noise:

*—told him the budget was finalized but Schiller's office hasn't signed off and if he thinks I'm going to beg—*

*—she's wearing the same dress as the Trade Minister's wife, someone should tell her, except absolutely nobody is going to be the one to—*

*—pork's undercooked. I can tell from here. The caterer cut corners again—*

*—flight leaves at six, which means the airport by four, which means leaving by three, which means this dinner's already too long and we haven't even sat down—*

She let the noise wash past her without grabbing. The trick — something Loid had taught her before she'd understood she needed it — was not to hunt. You didn't chase a signal through a crowd. You let the crowd talk, and you waited for a signal to distinguish itself. Operational thoughts had a different texture than social ones. Tighter. More controlled. People thinking about dinner plans or marital anxiety radiated outward, undisciplined; people thinking about covert timelines or unauthorized meetings held their thoughts closer, which paradoxically made them louder once you caught the edge, because the effort of containment produced a specific cognitive pressure she'd learned to recognize.

She moved to a new cluster. A woman in a charcoal suit, mid-fifties, standing with her back to the window. Her thoughts ran along institutional lines:

*—the initiative won't survive the next review cycle unless someone from Defense makes a case directly to the Committee. Three weeks. Maybe four. The southern checkpoint has to clear before—*

A waiter refilled the woman's glass and the thought fractured. *Oh. The white, not the — well, fine. Thank you.*

Gone. But Anya had tagged it. "The initiative." A timeline measured in weeks. A checkpoint. She filed the woman's face and position and moved on.

Near the center of the room, a general she recognized from WISE files — Brennan, border infrastructure command — was thinking in numbers. Procurement figures. Unit costs for something she couldn't quite identify, except the numbers were large and the anxiety attached to them was the kind that came from moving money through channels that wouldn't survive scrutiny. He was thinking about arms shipments in aggregate — quantities, routing, a southern depot that needed to be ready before a date he kept circling back to — and also, simultaneously, about his sciatica and whether the hotel bed tonight would be any better than last week's.

*—four hundred thousand, plus the logistics surcharge, which Müller is going to flag unless I can route it through the cultural liaison budget where nobody looks—*

She caught that and let it go. Not the operative. A person connected to something, maybe. But not the center.

The headache was settling in behind her left eye. She shifted her champagne glass to the other hand and pressed her thumb briefly against her temple — a gesture that might have looked like tiredness to anyone watching, which it was. Sustained scanning in a room this size was like reading a book printed in six languages simultaneously: you could do it, but the processing cost accumulated, and by the end your brain felt like it had been wrung out. She had maybe another hour of sharp reception before the resolution started to degrade.

An elderly official passed close enough that his thoughts grazed hers — a vivid, specific worry about his grandson's military service placement and whether he could pull strings without it looking like he was pulling strings. She caught the grandson's name, his age, his posting. She hadn't wanted any of it. That was the thing about telepathy that the operational briefings never covered: you didn't get to choose what you learned. You heard what people thought, and people thought about everything, and most of it was none of your business and all of it was in your head now, permanent, alongside every other private truth you'd ever accidentally consumed.

A younger diplomat — male, early thirties, handsome in the way that was useful in his profession — was composing a message to someone he'd rather be with tonight. The message was detailed and intimate and Anya shifted her attention away, studying the string quartet instead. It didn't fully help. You couldn't close your ears to thoughts the way you could close your eyes to a page.

The fragments accumulated across the first hour. "The initiative." Timelines measured in weeks. Arms procurement with numbers too large for routine budgets. A southern checkpoint. Money being routed through back channels. Anxiety about exposure. None of it coming from a single source — multiple people carrying pieces of something larger, the way workers on a construction site each hauled their own materials without seeing the building's shape. The thoughts were second-order: echoes, not origins. People who'd been told about a plan, not the person making it.

She was looking for the person who could see the whole thing.

***

An hour in, and the champagne in her glass had gone flat and warm. She hadn't drunk any of it. The glass was a prop, and props were useful — a woman holding an empty hand at a dinner party looked like she was looking for something, while a woman holding a glass looked like she'd found it.

She'd worked two-thirds of the room. Most of the thoughts she'd caught were institutional white noise: career anxiety, personal resentment, schedule management, the one diplomat still composing his love letter in his head. The operational fragments — the initiative, the timeline, the procurement — were all reflections. People thinking about something they'd heard or been briefed on, not something they were driving. She needed the source, not the echo.

The string quartet shifted to something faster, and a few couples moved toward the cleared space near the windows. Anya repositioned toward the far end of the room, where the crowd thinned near the terrace doors. Fewer people meant less noise and sharper individual signals, though it also meant less cover — you couldn't disappear into a crowd of six.

She paused at a display case of framed photographs documenting forty years of Ostanian cultural policy — handshakes, ribbon cuttings, a prime minister squinting into stage lights — and pretended to study them while she scanned.

An SSS officer by the terrace door was thinking about shift changes. A woman in a green dress was worrying about a spot of sauce on her sleeve. A minister's aide was mentally composing a resignation letter he'd been writing in his head for three years and would never send.

And then she caught the thread.

It was different from the others. Not anxious, not fragmented, not the loose and leaking quality of dinner-party cognition. Someone was thinking with focus — sustained, organized, moving from consideration to consideration with a discipline that most people in a social setting couldn't maintain. Like a musician tuning an instrument while a party played around them. The thoughts had structure: a sequence of names considered and evaluated, each one filed with a note about reliability, risk, timing. A meeting that needed to happen before a specific event, through a specific channel, with a person whose cooperation was uncertain.

She turned slowly, scanning. Telepathy wasn't directional the way hearing was — she couldn't point at a signal like a compass needle. But proximity sharpened reception, and as she moved, the signal either gained or lost resolution, and the difference told her roughly where to go.

Toward the terrace.

She moved through the thinning crowd. A brief detour around a conversation she didn't want to get caught in — the Trade Minister's wife, whose distress about the dress situation had curdled into something genuinely miserable — and she angled toward the French doors.

An official she didn't know stepped into her path. Tall, grey-haired, the alert bearing of someone from military intelligence. "Excuse me — you're with the Westalian mission?"

"Cultural affairs," Anya said, keeping her voice light. "Marta Kramer."

"Colonel Dreher. Retired." He didn't look retired. His thoughts were sharp and appraising: *Westalian. Young. Alone. Interesting.* "Enjoying the evening?"

"Very much. The Ministry always puts on a beautiful event."

"Mm." His thoughts shifted to something about attendance patterns, and she smiled and excused herself before he could decide she was worth a longer conversation.

Through the French doors, the terrace was lit by iron sconces and the reflected glow from the ballroom behind. The autumn cold was immediate — it pressed against her bare arms and the back of her neck. A few guests had stepped out: a couple arguing in low voices near the balustrade, a woman smoking a cigarette with the furtive speed of someone breaking a rule she didn't respect, an older man checking his watch with the expression of a person who'd already decided to leave.

And apart from all of them, near the far railing, a man standing alone.

His posture was formal without being rigid — the learned ease of someone who'd grown up in rooms like this and knew how to occupy them without appearing to try. Dark suit, well-fitted. Dark hair, kept short. His hands rested on the stone railing, not gripping, just resting — a studied casualness that was so practiced it might have actually become natural. She couldn't see his face clearly from this angle, but she didn't need to. The signal was unmistakable. He was the source.

She stepped onto the terrace, moving along the railing to a position about fifteen feet away. Not close enough to seem deliberate. Close enough for clear reception. The cold bit at her arms and she crossed them, which looked like a woman who was chilly and was not an operative taking position.

His thoughts came into focus like a radio dial clicking into station.

*—Griesler won't hold past the end of the month. If he goes to the Committee before I can establish an alternative channel through Kleist, the whole sequence falls apart. I need to talk to the old man's people. Tuesday. Maybe Wednesday if Kleist's office confirms.*

Names she didn't recognize. But the operational grammar was clear — this was someone managing a network, maintaining contacts, running parallel timelines. Not a person who'd heard about the initiative. The person running his own.

*—the Front's meeting on the fourteenth. If I can get attendance confirmed by Thursday, we'll have enough to map the full civilian side. The military connections are already documented through Father's files—*

Father's files.

The man turned slightly, reaching for a glass he'd set on the railing, and she saw his face.

She knew it.

Not from WISE briefing files, though he was in those too — a low-priority line item in the Desmond family monitoring file that listed him as a junior diplomat of moderate potential and uncertain loyalties. She knew the face the way you knew a face you'd seen every day for years and then not at all — changed, older, the soft lines of childhood carved into someone adult, but the same underneath. The jaw was sharper. The mouth more set. The expression of someone carrying something heavier than anyone around him guessed.

Damian Desmond.

Twenty years old. Her age. Son of Donovan Desmond, whose political machine had driven two nations to the brink of war fourteen years ago, whose shadow still shaped everything in this city. She'd known Damian was in Ostania's diplomatic corps — WISE tracked the Desmond family as a matter of routine. But the monitoring file said junior diplomat, factional player, possible asset for the national unity caucus. Not this. Not what she was reading in his head right now.

His thoughts continued, and she listened, standing in the cold, her hands going numb against her own arms. She should have gone back inside. A professional would have tagged him, noted his identity and position, and returned to work the room for corroboration. Instead she stood on the terrace and listened to Damian Desmond think, because his thoughts had the quality of something she recognized — not the content but the shape. A person carrying too much alone. She knew what that felt like. She'd known what it felt like since she was four years old and realized she could hear things nobody else could hear and had nobody to tell.

That wasn't a useful thought. She filed it away with the other ones she didn't examine.

***

He was thinking about the Renewal Front.

The name surfaced in his mind with the familiarity of something he'd been carrying for months — not new information but a constant, the way a person with chronic pain thinks about their body. The Renewal Front: a coalition of military hardliners and civilian nationalists organized around the conviction that the peace with Westalis was a controlled surrender, a slow erosion of everything Ostania had been. They wanted an incident. Something that would make diplomacy untenable. Something that would justify mobilization, force the engine of conflict back to life, finish what the last generation started and this generation was supposed to have buried.

Damian's thoughts laid the Front out the way someone maps a problem they've been living inside alone for too long. Not organized for an audience. No neat categories. Just the constant background processing of a mind that had been tracing a maze in the dark — each new corridor confirmed, the exits still unclear. He thought about funding channels: defense procurement contracts, money routed through legitimate military budgets into operations that had nothing to do with defense. He thought about personnel: officers attending unauthorized meetings, civilians whose businesses existed to clean political violence into economic activity. He thought about the person at the center, whose name he knew but whom Anya hadn't caught yet — someone he associated with grief that had hardened into conviction, loss that had calcified into ideology.

There was more. Specific worries, cycling. A contact in the military — Griesler, the name she'd caught earlier — was getting nervous and might pull out. A retired aide from his father's era who had information about the Front's civilian funding but was afraid to share it. A meeting at a policy forum next week where a Renewal Front operative would be present and Damian planned to observe without being observed, which he knew was riskier than it sounded because his last name made him visible in any political room. And underneath all of this, a thought he kept returning to without finishing, the way you keep touching a bruise: *If they find out what I'm doing before I'm ready —* The sentence never completed. It didn't need to. The consequences were clear enough in the way his thoughts tightened around the possibility and then deliberately released it, like someone practicing not flinching.

And he was trying to take it apart.

She stood on the terrace and felt the understanding arrive the way weather arrives — not all at once but as a change in pressure, a shift in what the air could hold.

His father's network. The intelligence contacts, the retired operatives, the institutional relationships that Donovan Desmond had built over decades of political maneuvering — Damian was using all of it. Not continuing the work. Inverting it. The same relationships that had constructed Ostanian nationalism's infrastructure were being repurposed to map and dismantle the people who wanted to finish what Donovan started. He'd been identifying Renewal Front members, documenting connections, tracing funding. Running a covert counter-operation against extremists inside his own government, using the only tools available to a twenty-year-old diplomat with a famous last name and no institutional backing: his father's leftovers and his own position in the Ministry.

Anya read this and understood, with the particular clarity that came from hearing someone's unfiltered truth, that Sylvia's briefing had been right about the unauthorized operation and wrong about everything else. The activity pattern wasn't pre-conflict positioning. It was pre-conflict prevention. One person, working alone, trying to stop a war before it started.

He was doing what his father never would have.

She should have felt relief. The mysterious operative wasn't a threat — he was an ally she didn't know she had, and his intelligence on the Renewal Front was exactly what WISE needed.

She didn't feel relief. What she felt was closer to the sensation of missing a step on a staircase — the lurch, the recalibration, the ground suddenly a different distance than your body expected. She felt the specific vertigo of realizing that every assumption she'd built this evening on had just inverted, and that what she'd learned — all of it, the Front's structure, the counter-operation, the names and timelines — she'd acquired through an ability she could never disclose. She couldn't walk into Sylvia's office tomorrow and say: *I read his mind and he's on our side.* She'd have to construct a version of tonight that made sense without telepathy. Observation. Social engineering. Pattern analysis. A plausible intelligence pathway built on methods that would survive institutional scrutiny.

She'd have to lie.

But that was the next problem. That was tomorrow. Right now she was standing on a cold terrace reading Damian Desmond's unguarded mind, and his thoughts were shifting.

It happened the way memories happen — not as narrative, not as story, but as sensory association. Something about the evening — the cooling air, the faint sound of the quartet through the glass doors, some neural pathway she couldn't trace and he probably couldn't either — pulled him away from operational thinking and into something older.

*Eden.*

The word surfaced like a coin turning over in shallow water. Eden Academy. He didn't think the full name. Just the shape of it — the way a place you'd lived in for years compressed into a single image. For him it was the courtyard. The specific green of the grass in spring. The bell that always rang two seconds late.

*The girl with the pink hair.*

Anya's fingers pressed harder against her own arms where they were crossed.

His memory wasn't a story he was telling himself. It was fragments, arriving in the order that association dictated. A color first — her hair, that specific pink that nobody else had. Not dyed. Just hers. He'd thought it was strange, and then he'd stopped thinking it was strange, and then he'd stopped thinking about it at all because it was just her, just what she looked like. A sound next — laughing. She was always laughing, or about to laugh, or still recovering from the last one. She'd hit him during a game — dodgeball, he was almost sure — and his nose had bled, and he'd been furious, standing there with blood on his shirt and his pride hurting worse than his face, and she'd come back with a wet paper towel from the bathroom and she'd looked sorry. Genuinely sorry. But also a little like she was trying not to smile, because the hit had been an accident but it had also been, objectively, impressive, and she knew it, and he'd known she knew it.

She tilted her head. In the memory, the girl tilted her head the way she always did — to the left, chin down, deciding whether to say the honest thing or the careful thing. And her name was—

*An—*

The terrace door opened behind him. Warm air and noise flooded out. A man in a grey suit leaned through the doorway.

"Desmond, the deputy minister's asking for you."

Damian straightened. The memory dissolved — not gradually, but like a hand wiping condensation off glass. His face composed itself. The shift from private to public was so fast that if Anya hadn't been reading his thoughts she'd have missed it entirely. One moment he was a boy remembering a girl from a schoolyard; the next he was a diplomat being summoned.

"Of course. Tell him I'll be right there."

He picked up his glass and turned. His stride was measured, unhurried. As he passed within six feet of Anya, his thoughts were already reorganizing around the deputy minister's known positions on border-security appropriations — a series of arguments mentally rehearsed, concessions calculated, the smooth machinery of a mind trained to operate in political rooms. He glanced at her as he passed. A brief, automatic social acknowledgment — the kind you gave someone sharing a terrace at a party.

*Westalian, probably. Cold without a coat.*

Then he was through the doors and his thoughts folded into the noise of two hundred other minds and she couldn't distinguish his signal anymore.

He hadn't recognized her.

Anya stayed on the terrace. The cold had gotten into her hands and the exposed skin above her neckline, and she didn't move. Inside, the dinner continued under its warm light. The chandeliers. The white tablecloths. The whole careful theater of two nations performing cordiality while their intelligence services worked the margins. Somewhere in there, Damian Desmond was shaking the deputy minister's hand, wearing the diplomatic composure that was both a performance and a genuine part of who he was, carrying a counter-operation that nobody in that room knew about.

Nobody except her.

She knew his operation. His network. His timeline. His fear that the Front would move before he was ready to expose them. She knew names he hadn't shared with a single other person. She knew the structure of a conspiracy that WISE had barely begun to sketch. She knew all of this because she had stood fifteen feet away and entered his mind without his knowledge or consent, the same way she'd entered every mind she'd ever been near, since she was small, since before she understood what she was doing or what it cost anyone.

And she knew he'd almost remembered her.

Pink hair. A punch during a game. A wet paper towel. A name that started to form and dissolved when someone opened a door.

He'd been carrying that memory for fourteen years. Not prominently — it lived in the place where old, unresolved things settle, surfacing when something triggered it and submerging when the present took over. But it was there. She had mattered to him in some way he'd never examined and never quite released, and she was standing right here on this terrace, with a different name and a different life and the same face he'd looked at without placing.

The distance between what he almost knew and what she completely knew was going to shape everything that came next. She was certain of this the way she was certain of the cold against her skin — as a fact, physical and present and not going anywhere.

She was going to have to go back inside. She was going to have to file a report. She was going to have to sit across from Sylvia and construct a version of tonight that would satisfy WISE — careful, plausible, stripped of telepathy and childhood memory and the ache of hearing someone almost say your name.

She set the warm champagne on the stone railing. The glass made a small sound in the quiet. Her breath was visible. Below, the Ministry gardens were dark shapes — hedgerows, gravel paths, the outline of a fountain that wasn't running. The remaining leaves on the trees moved in a wind she could feel on her arms.

Then she smoothed her dress, uncrossed her arms, and walked back into the warm noise of the party.


# Chapter 3

The debriefing room at WISE headquarters smelled like burned coffee and carpet glue. The fluorescent tubes overhead hummed at a frequency that settled behind Anya's teeth, a vibration she'd stopped noticing consciously three years ago but which was there now, in her jaw, because she was noticing everything. The water pitcher on the table had a chip in the handle that maintenance had never replaced. The chairs were the same beige plastic as every other room in the building. Someone had left a pen cap on the floor under the far chair, blue, the kind that came in packs of twelve from the supply closet.

Sylvia sat across from her. Reading glasses on the chain around her neck, not on her face — which meant receiving mode, not review mode. Her hands were folded on the table, her posture the precise uprightness of a woman who had spent thirty years in rooms like this and had long ago stopped distinguishing between comfort and duty. She looked at Anya the way she always looked at agents during debrief: attentive, evaluative, and underneath both of those, something that wasn't quite warmth but that Anya could hear if she listened.

*Good. She is punctual. Loid's influence, or her own — hard to say now. She has become her own operative.*

The thought arrived in Sylvia's voice: clipped, structured, the mental equivalent of a briefing memo. Sylvia's surface thoughts ran like institutional prose — orderly, sequential, formatted. Even her private impressions came with headings.

"Your initial assessment of the Ostanian operative." Sylvia uncapped a pen. "I want the full picture. Walk me through what you observed."

Anya opened the folder she'd prepared. She'd typed the report at her apartment the night before, sitting on the kitchen counter with her shoes off because the desk chair squeaked and the squeak was annoying at two in the morning. She had written three drafts. The first was true: Damian Desmond, son of Donovan, running a counter-operation against the Renewal Front from inside the Foreign Affairs Ministry, using his father's old intelligence network against the people who wanted to finish what his father had started. She'd read his sincerity. She'd read his isolation. She'd read the memories of a girl with pink hair that kept surfacing in his unguarded thoughts. She'd deleted that draft because filing it would trigger a full WISE operational response — analysts swarming the sector, surveillance assets redirected, Damian identified and assessed and ultimately burned or turned or used until the mission consumed him the way missions consumed people.

The second draft was close to true, with the telepathic intelligence stripped out. It still identified Damian too clearly. It still named the Renewal Front as the primary threat in the sector. Filing it would have been honest. Filing it would have been correct. She'd deleted it too.

The third draft was a lie.

"The operative is a mid-level diplomat. Damian Desmond — yes, son of Donovan, but he's kept visible distance from the family's political infrastructure." Anya kept her voice level. Conversational. The register you used to report something that was worth mentioning but not worth prioritizing. She'd learned that register from Loid, who'd taught her how to modulate information delivery without ever calling it that. "His activity profile is consistent with internal Ostanian factional positioning. Party maneuvering. I don't think he represents a direct security concern for Westalian interests."

*Consistent with the eastern sector pattern we have been tracking. Her assessment aligns with the secondary indicators.*

Sylvia's thought was approving. Procedural. Not a flicker of suspicion. Two years of reliable field work were a bank account, and Anya was emptying it in a single transaction.

"The Renewal Front intelligence," Sylvia said. Not a question. A prompt.

"Fragments." Anya turned a page in the folder — a prop, something to do with her hands. "References to 'the initiative,' some anxiety about a timeline. It could be the standard nationalist rhetoric we've been hearing since spring. I'd classify it as elevated chatter, not operational activity."

The fluorescent hum was louder now, or she was more aware of it. She was minimizing intelligence about a conspiracy that was engineering a diplomatic incident designed to push two nations toward war. She was doing it in a building full of people whose careers existed to prevent exactly that. She was doing it while the woman across from her thought —

*Her field judgment has been consistently sound. Two years without a significant analytical error. She has Loid's instinct for threat assessment — the ability to distinguish signal from noise under pressure. That is not a common quality.*

Anya's hand rested on the table. She focused on the grain of the laminate under her fingertips. Fake wood. Fake assessment. Fake everything in this room except Sylvia's trust, which was the realest thing Anya had ever felt someone direct at her, and which she was currently using as the surface her lie slid across.

"The Desmond name alone would normally trigger an escalation protocol," Sylvia said. Institutional process, not challenge. She was making a note in the margin of the printed report — small, precise handwriting that Anya couldn't read upside down.

"I know. But the son isn't the father." True. "What I observed at the dinner didn't suggest operational sophistication or hostile intent." False. "He struck me as someone navigating his family's political inheritance, not weaponizing it." True and false simultaneously, which was the worst kind of lie because the truth in it made the falseness almost invisible.

*She is correct. We have been over-indexing on the Desmond name for a decade. It is time to recalibrate.*

Sylvia was already recalibrating. Anya could hear it happening — the institutional machinery shifting, resource calculations adjusting, two analysts who would have been assigned to Damian's sector now redirected to the border situation because Anya Forger had told her handler that the threat was manageable.

"I am classifying this as watch-and-report." Sylvia capped her pen. "You will maintain passive observation on Desmond through your cover function. Monthly reporting unless something changes."

"Understood."

"Anya." Sylvia looked at her properly — without the glasses, without the pen, with the full weight of her attention, which was considerable even without telepathy to verify it. "Your work at the dinner was clean. The approach, the positioning, the extraction of observations under crowd pressure. I noticed, and I want you to know that."

*She does not hear this enough. Loid is the same way — he assumes competence is its own reward. But she is young, and the work is isolating, and it costs nothing to tell someone they did well when they did well.*

The trust was not generalized institutional confidence. It was specific. It was aimed at Anya by name, informed by specific field reports and specific conversations with Loid and a specific memory of an extraction six months ago that Anya had handled under pressure. Sylvia was thinking about that extraction now — about how Anya had improvised a route change when the original plan fell apart, and how she'd remained calm, and how the contact had gotten out clean.

Anya sat there and received all of it. The specific, professional, earned trust of a woman who had given her career to this organization. The trust that made the lie possible. Without it — without the two years of clean work, without the accumulated credibility, without Sylvia's assessment that Anya's judgment could be relied on — the false report would have been questioned. Scrutinized. Flagged. The trust was the infrastructure her betrayal rode on, and she was reading it in real time, consuming it like oxygen.

"Thanks," Anya said. Her voice held. Her father had taught her how to maintain composure under operational stress, and she was grateful for the training in the specific, nauseating way you could be grateful for something that was currently enabling you to do something terrible.

She gathered the folder. Stood. The chair scraped against the carpet — the same institutional scrape it always made, the sound of every debriefing she'd ever been in, except this was the one where she'd lied.

"Anya."

She turned at the door.

"Coffee is fresh. Left pot. Do not drink from the right pot — Henderson left it there on Wednesday and I believe it has become a research project."

*I should throw it out. I keep meaning to throw it out. Tomorrow.*

Anya almost smiled, and the almost was where the real pain lived. Sylvia was a person. Not a system or an institution or an abstract concept Anya was betraying — a fifty-eight-year-old woman who made dry jokes about old coffee and remembered which of her agents needed encouragement and wore her glasses on a chain because she kept losing them on her desk. Anya had just lied to her face. And Sylvia's surface thoughts had contained nothing but Monday-morning logistics and a genuine, unsolicited thought about making sure a young operative heard she was doing good work.

"I'll grab some. Thanks, Handler."

She walked out. The corridor was empty — WISE at eight in the morning, most analysts not yet in, the overnight team cycling out, the building hollow and echoing the way schools echo before first period. Anya's shoes were quiet on the institutional carpet. She passed two closed doors, a water fountain with a persistent drip, and a notice board where someone had taped a cartoon of a cat wearing a security badge.

She went into the women's bathroom. Locked the stall. Sat on the toilet lid with the folder in her lap.

Her hands were shaking. Not much. Enough that the folder's pages rustled against each other, a tiny paper sound in the tiled room. The fluorescent hum was different in here — higher, or maybe she was just more aware of everything now that she didn't have Sylvia's thoughts to focus on.

The false assessment was in the system. A traceable document with her name and her classification and her professional judgment committed to WISE's institutional memory. If someone cross-referenced her report with other intelligence — with whatever the Renewal Front was actually doing in the sectors she'd downgraded — the discrepancy would be there. Not today. Maybe not next month. But the lie had a half-life she couldn't calculate, and somewhere in the building's analytical machinery it was already being absorbed as reliable baseline data, shaping resource allocation and threat models and the operational picture that other agents would trust with their decisions.

She pressed her palms against her knees and breathed.

She'd done it. She'd actually done it. Not the dramatic kind of betrayal — no stolen files, no dead drops, no midnight meetings in parking garages. The bureaucratic kind. The kind that looked like routine assessment and slid into the system and sat there like a slow-release poison because nobody suspected it was anything other than a competent young agent's honest evaluation.

For Damian Desmond. For a man whose thoughts she'd read without his knowledge at a state dinner, whose counter-operation she believed in because she'd seen his sincerity from the inside, whose sincerity she'd accessed through a power he didn't know existed.

She'd lied to her handler. Using information stolen from her target. Using an ability no one knew she had.

Two deceptions now. The telepathy — lifelong, involuntary, the condition of her existence. And the false report — chosen, deliberate, four minutes old.

Anya flushed the toilet she hadn't used, washed her hands she didn't need to wash, and went to get coffee from the left pot.


The Ostanian Academy of Arts occupied a limestone building in the government district, its columns either original eighteenth-century or very good fakes — Anya couldn't tell and didn't much care. The cultural exchange reception filled the main hall: eighty people, wine and finger food, a string quartet in the corner playing something she was fairly sure was Schumann. Diplomatic staff from both delegations, some academics, a few journalists, and the particular species of government-adjacent socialite who appeared at every event with wine and a guest list.

The telepathic noise was manageable. Not the overwhelming density of the state dinner, where hundreds of overlapping thoughts had battered her like standing in a wind tunnel. This was more like a cocktail party playing on multiple radios at moderate volume — she could let the murmur wash over her or tune toward individual signals with a focused effort.

*—absolute waste of funding, this exhibition, I could have—*
*—need to confirm the flight before—*
*—are those the salmon ones? Where are the salmon—*

She stood near the refreshments table with a glass of Ostanian white — a little sweet, not her preference, but it was the prop that made her look like a person at a party instead of an intelligence operative surveilling the room. The cover was easy tonight: a young Westalian cultural attaché attending an official East-West exchange event. She'd worn her hair down, the pink falling past her shoulders, and a dress that was professional without trying hard enough to look like it was trying. Loid had once told her that the best cover identity was one where you didn't have to remember what you were pretending to be, because it was close enough to what you were. Good advice from a man who had built an entire real life on that principle.

Damian was near the far wall, talking to a woman in a grey suit. Anya picked up the woman's surface thoughts — flight schedules, a mild resentment about the catering, a vague thought about whether the cellist was attractive — and dismissed them. She focused on Damian.

He looked different here than at the state dinner. The diplomatic armor was lighter. His posture was still trained — the Desmond spine, the product of a childhood where every room was a performance space — but his face was less composed, and when the woman in grey said something mildly funny, his laugh was a real one.

*—her hair. The attaché from the dinner. She's here. Don't — you're mid-conversation. Focus. Ms. Jansen is talking about the trade delegation. The trade delegation. Focus on trade.*

He was trying not to look at her. He was failing. His thought had the quality of someone slamming a drawer shut on their own fingers — the discipline and the impulse and the pain of catching himself all happening at once.

Anya took a sip of wine and began to move.

Three intermediate stops. A brief exchange with a Westalian press officer about the new cultural exhibition schedule — thirty seconds, enough to be seen, not enough to anchor. A polite nod to an Ostanian academic she'd met at a previous function. A pause at the hors d'oeuvres table where she selected something involving goat cheese that she had no intention of eating. Each stop moved her closer to Damian. When the woman in grey excused herself to greet a colleague, Anya stepped into the gap with the timing of someone who'd been trained to enter rooms at exactly the right moment, which she had.

"The quartet's ambitious tonight," she said. "I think that's supposed to be Brahms."

He turned. Up close his face was sharper than she remembered from school — the boyish softness gone, the jaw angular, the eyes the same dark brown but guarded now in a way they hadn't been when he was six years old and furious that a girl with pink hair had punched him during a dodgeball game. He was taller than her by enough that she had to angle her head slightly to meet his eyes.

"It's Schumann, actually," he said. Polite. Measuring. The diplomatic register engaged. "The Piano Quintet in E-flat."

*Westalian delegation. She was at the state dinner. Something about her face. Her — what is it about her face?*

"Oh. Well, now I'm embarrassed." She wasn't, but the admission of error was operational — Loid again, always Loid: the fastest way to make someone comfortable is to be slightly less competent than they expect. "I'm Anya. Cultural attaché with the Westalian mission."
