"Damian Desmond. Foreign Affairs." He shook her hand. Firm grip, warm palm, a little damp in a way that told her he was more nervous than his face suggested. "I thought I noticed you at the state dinner last week."

"Probably. I was the one trying to find the bar without looking like I was trying to find the bar."

A small sound that was almost a laugh. His thought: *She's funny. That's — stop cataloguing her personality. You met her literally ten seconds ago. This is professional contact. Professional.*

"The bar was well-hidden," he said. "I think they do that deliberately. Keep the diplomats sober."

"Hasn't worked in my experience."

This time the laugh was real, and Anya felt it arrive with a small, warm concussion in her chest that had nothing to do with her telepathy and everything to do with the specific sound of Damian Desmond laughing without performing.

*Her face when she said that. She scrunches — her nose. Someone used to — not the dinner. Before. Years. Why does she—*

The recognition was there at the edges. Not a clear signal — more like the peripheral awareness of something moving just outside your line of sight. He was looking at her hair, at her face, at the way she tilted her head when she waited for him to speak, and his brain was trying to match the pattern to a memory filed away in whatever storage system twenty-year-old Ostanian diplomats kept their childhoods in.

"So you're in Foreign Affairs," Anya said, steering onto safe ground. She knew exactly where he worked, what he did there, what he was secretly trying to accomplish, and the name of his childhood dog. "Is that as exciting as it sounds?"

"It sounds exciting?"

"To someone who reads about cultural funding all day, yeah."

He relaxed a degree. His shoulders dropped maybe a centimeter — invisible to anyone not trained by Loid Forger to read posture as language. "Mostly it's paperwork. Position papers. Meetings about meetings. The exciting parts are someone else's pay grade." A beat. "For now."

*The 'for now' was too much. That sounded ambitious. She's going to think you're one of those Desmond people who talks about destiny. Pull it back.*

"Although pay grade implies there's a ladder, and I'm not sure there is." The correction was clumsy — he'd heard himself sound too eager and was trying to sand it down into something modest, but the sanding was visible and the modesty was worse than the ambition because it was so obviously performed. His thought while he said it: *That was worse. That was definitely worse. Stop talking about your career. Ask her something. Anything.*

"What about the cultural attaché work? Is it—" He searched for the right question and she watched him search, his diplomatic training offering several options and his actual interest discarding all of them. "Is it what you expected?"

"Nobody expects cultural policy," Anya said. "You sort of fall into it and then realize you care about exhibition catalogues."

"That's — yeah. That's how it works in Foreign Affairs too. You arrive thinking you'll change the world and then you spend three years learning the filing system."

He meant it. His thought didn't contradict the words — it reinforced them, which was rare for Damian, whose private and public registers usually ran on separate tracks. He was being honest, and the honesty made his voice sound younger, less curated, closer to the boy he'd been before the training and the name and the weight of his father's legacy taught him to sound like something else.

Anya noticed she was staring at him and picked up her wine glass.

*She's looking at — was she looking at me? She picked up her glass very fast. Either she's nervous or she doesn't like the wine. The wine is pretty bad. It's probably the wine.*

"The wine's not great," she said, because she couldn't help herself, and his expression of surprised agreement was so transparent that she had to press the glass against her lips to hide whatever her face was doing.

They talked. The conversation moved through the safe corridors of diplomatic small talk — the exhibition, East-West cultural funding, a translated speech that had gone wrong in a way that was funny in retrospect and apparently a minor international incident at the time. Damian was good at this. His public register was smooth, each sentence doing social work while appearing casual, the product of a lifetime of rooms like this one.

But his thoughts ran alongside his words like a second track.

When she mentioned growing up in Berlint: *Berlint. Eden Academy was in Berlint. A girl — there was a girl with — no. Has to be a different person. Berlint is a big city. Lots of people grew up here.*

When she gestured with her hand to make a point about funding: *Her hands. She uses them when she talks. She — someone used to — stop it. You're projecting. You met her at a reception and now you're inventing connections because she's pretty and you're lonely. That's not a connection. That's a Tuesday.*

When she tilted her head to listen to his explanation of the eastern cultural exchange program: *There. That exact tilt. Who tilts their head like that? I've seen — when I was—*

She was managing two conversations and the gap between them was getting harder to hold. Every time his thoughts edged toward recognition, she had to steer the spoken exchange away from anything that might deepen the association — away from schools, from childhood, from anything that might give the half-formed memory enough material to crystallize. Every time his thoughts edged toward attraction, she had to not react. This was the high-wire act of being Anya Forger: the person most qualified to have this conversation and the person least able to have it honestly.

He was thinking about her smile. Specifically, he was thinking about the slight asymmetry — the left side of her mouth pulling up first — and about how he found it—

She turned to look at a painting on the wall. Abruptly. Transparently. He noticed.

"Sorry," she said. "Thought I recognized someone over there."

*She keeps looking away when I'm talking. Am I boring her? I might be boring her. I was about to talk about the funding reconciliation framework. That would bore anyone. Change the subject. Something interesting. Something—*

"Have you seen the exhibition in the next gallery?" he asked. The question came out slightly too fast, the pitch slightly too high, and his internal reaction was immediate: *That sounded eager. That sounded like I'm trying to get her alone in a gallery. I'm not trying to — I just think the art is — it doesn't matter what I think the art is. She's going to say no. She should say no. This is a professional reception and I—*

"I haven't," Anya said. "Show me?"

Protocol said: maintain a light touch at first contact. Don't extend the encounter past twenty minutes. Don't let the target set the location or pace. She was already forty minutes in and now she was following him into a quieter room.

His thought as she said yes: a small, startled burst of pleasure that he immediately tried to discipline into professional composure, like a man trying to stuff a firework back into its tube.


The gallery was cooler than the main hall, and quieter. Maybe fifteen people drifting between paintings. The crowd's telepathic noise dropped to a murmur — distant enough that individual thoughts surfaced cleanly, like voices carrying across water. Damian's thoughts were easier to isolate here, and she was aware that this was part of why she'd said yes. More access. Better signal. She was an intelligence operative doing intelligence work, and the fact that she was also a person who wanted to keep talking to him was a complication she wasn't ready to name.

They stopped in front of an abstract piece — large, aggressive, the canvas worked over in layers of dark paint that looked like someone had fought with the material and neither side had won.

"This one's interesting," Anya said, because it was there and she needed to say something.

Damian studied it. His public face arranged itself into the expression of a man considering art.

His thought: *I don't understand abstract art. I've never understood abstract art. I nodded through six years of Eden Academy art history and I still don't understand it. If she asks me what I think, I'm going to say something about the use of negative space because that's what Demetrius always said and it worked for him. Negative space. The interplay of form and—*

"What do you think?" Anya asked. She was, at her core, a person who couldn't resist watching someone try to bluff when she could see their cards.

"The artist's use of negative space is—" He stopped. Something in his face shifted — the diplomatic polish cracking, a flash of the stubbornness underneath. He started over. "Honestly? I don't get it. It looks like someone had a bad day and took it out on a canvas."

Anya laughed. Not the professional laugh — the one she used at receptions, controlled and warm and calibrated for social function. The real one. It came from her chest and carried the slightly too-loud quality that Loid had spent years trying to train out of her, the laugh she'd had since she was a child, the one that echoed.

It echoed in the gallery.

*That laugh.*

The thought was louder than anything she'd read from him all night. Not analytical. Not self-correcting. A raw, unfiltered sensory response — the sound of her laughter hitting something deep and unexamined in him, a memory he couldn't locate, a recognition that was almost but not quite—

*A girl at school. She laughed like that. Exactly like that. In a courtyard — no, a hallway — no, outside somewhere. After something happened. Something funny. She was laughing and I was standing there and I thought—*

The memory reached for completion. Anya could feel it stretching, trying to close the circuit — pink hair, a courtyard, a specific afternoon — and she watched it not quite get there. The sediment of a childhood stored away and rarely disturbed shifted but didn't fully surface. He blinked. Twice. His hands went into his pockets — the stress tell, the one that meant he'd felt something he couldn't categorize.

"That funny?" he said. His voice was lighter than his thoughts.

"The bad-day-canvas thing." She was still smiling and couldn't entirely stop. "Sorry. I just — you're right, though. It does look like that."

"You know, most of what I say at these events is rehearsed." He was half-turning toward her, the gallery light catching the angle of his jaw. "The honest answers always come out worse."

*I had a line prepared. About the interplay of form and intention. It was actually a decent line. I rehearsed it in the car. And then she looked at me and I panicked and said the real thing and the real thing made her laugh and now I don't know if that's good or—*

"It came out better than rehearsed," Anya said. "Honest stuff usually does."

He looked at her. Not the diplomatic look. The other one — the one where the polish pulled back and the earnestness underneath was visible, stubborn and slightly bewildered, the expression of someone unaccustomed to being seen clearly and not sure whether to lean into it or away.

"I — there's a lecture series next month," he said. He pulled one hand from his pocket, then put it back. "Comparative cultural policy. It sounds dry. It is dry. But the last one was actually not bad, and if you're interested in the East-West exchange space—"

*Don't ask her. You met her ninety minutes ago. This is not professional networking. You're asking a woman you find attractive to attend a lecture about cultural policy because you can't think of a normal way to see her again. That's — that is the most Damian Desmond thing you have ever done.*

"—if that's the kind of thing that falls under your work, it might be useful. No pressure. I can send you the details."

Anya should have said she'd think about it. She should have maintained the light touch. She should have been the cover identity — the pleasant Westalian attaché who took a polite interest and promised nothing.

"I'd like that," she said. "Send me the details?"

She gave him her official contact information — the cultural attaché email that routed through WISE-monitored channels. He saved it in his phone. His thoughts while he typed were a collision of professional justification and something warmer that he was working very hard to label as networking.

*This is standard diplomatic contact. Completely standard. I am networking with a cultural attaché from the Westalian mission. The fact that she has — that I find her — this is networking. I'll send the details tomorrow. Not tonight. Tonight looks eager. Tomorrow morning. No, mid-morning. Mid-morning is professional. Mid-morning says 'I had this on my calendar and happened to remember.'*

He was going to send it at six in the morning. Anya was certain.

They walked back through the main hall toward the exit. The reception was winding down — the quartet packing up, the catering staff clearing plates, the room's telepathic noise thinning as guests left in twos and threes. At the door he shook her hand again. Same firm grip. Drier now.

"It was nice to meet you, Anya."

"You too, Damian."

*She said my name like she's said it before. Like it was already — no. That's the thing your therapist talks about. You assign significance to—*

He had a therapist. The information arrived and she absorbed it and immediately wished she hadn't, because it was another piece of his private life stolen without his permission, another item in a growing inventory of things she knew about Damian Desmond that he'd never chosen to share with her.

"Have a good night," he said. Formal. The diplomatic register resettling, like a coat being pulled back on against the cold.

"Yeah. You too."

She watched him go. He walked well — straight-backed, unhurried, the trained gait of someone who'd been taught that exits matter. But his thoughts as he crossed the lobby were not about the exit.

*The laugh. Something about her laugh. And the way she tilted her head when she listened. I've — someone — a specific girl. Pink hair. At a school. She used to—*

*What was her name?*

He pushed through the doors into the autumn night. His thoughts faded past the edge of her range, dissolving like a radio signal losing its station, the half-formed recognition thinning to static and then to silence.

Anya stood in the lobby of the Ostanian Academy of Arts with her coat over her arm. Outside, the government district was going dark. The streetlights had come on along the boulevard, and the trees lining the pavement were turning — orange and brown, the leaves curling at the edges, autumn moving through Berlint the way autumn moved through any city, indifferent to the people worrying under it.

She'd pushed past protocol. Forty minutes past the recommended first-contact window. She'd let him choose the gallery. She'd laughed like herself instead of her cover. She'd agreed to see him again. Every operational guideline she'd been trained to follow was screaming at her, and the screaming sounded a lot like her father's voice, measured and precise, explaining the principles of contact management while she sat at the kitchen table as a teenager eating cereal and absorbing lessons she'd never expected to use like this.

But she'd read his thoughts all evening. She knew what he carried — the Desmond name and the counter-operation and the loneliness of a life built on a performance he couldn't drop. She knew his sincerity was real because she'd been inside his head while he produced it, and that was the problem, wasn't it. She knew things about Damian that Damian had never chosen to tell her. His nervousness. His stubborn decency. His memory of a girl he couldn't quite name. His therapist. His rehearsed line about negative space. The specific quality of his attraction to her, which she'd consumed without asking, the way she'd consumed Sylvia's trust that morning — taking what wasn't offered and using it to make her own choices easier.

She put on her coat. Her hands were steady now. The shaking from this morning had passed, replaced by something quieter and worse: the recognition that she'd done two things today, and she'd chosen both of them, and both of them had worked exactly as she'd intended.

The false report was in WISE's system. The contact was established. Two deceptions — one aimed at the institution that trusted her, one woven through every word she'd spoken to the man she was supposed to be assessing — and both of them were hers. Not compelled by the mission. Not forced by circumstance. Chosen, because Anya Forger had decided she knew better than everyone around her. Better than WISE's analytical machinery. Better than Loid's training. Better than Sylvia's thirty years of experience. Better than Damian's right to meet someone without that someone already knowing his thoughts.

She walked out into Berlint. The evening air was cold enough to feel on her face, the first real bite of autumn, and the city's thoughts washed over her as she headed toward the diplomatic quarter. Commuters thinking about dinner. A couple arguing about whose turn it was to cook. A child thinking about a cartoon. Someone rehearsing what they'd say to their boss tomorrow. Someone else not thinking about anything at all, just walking, just tired, their mind a low hum of fatigue and the particular emptiness that came at the end of a long day.

None of them knew she was there. Inside their heads, taking what they hadn't offered. She'd been doing this her entire life. Every person she'd ever passed on a street, every classmate, every neighbor, every stranger on a tram — she'd been inside them, uninvited, for as long as she could remember.

Today that lifelong fact had become a choice. Two choices. And both of them had cost something that she could feel in her body — not in her throat or her chest, not the stock vocabulary of guilt, but in her hands, which had held the folder steady while she lied to Sylvia, and which had shaken afterward in a bathroom stall, and which had been steady again by the time she shook Damian's hand in a gallery. The steadiness was the worst part. It meant she was getting used to it.

Anya walked home through the autumn dark, reading the city all the way, carrying the specific weight of the day she'd become a person who betrayed her handler's trust and used a man's unguarded thoughts to build a connection he believed was honest. Two deceptions. One she'd been born with. One she'd made this morning at a table in a room that smelled like burned coffee.

She was good at this. That was the thing she couldn't stop thinking about. She was really, really good at this.


# Chapter 4

The Ostanian Forum on Economic Realignment was held in a conference room on the third floor of the Foreign Affairs Ministry, and it was exactly as tedious as its name suggested. Anya stood near the back wall with a glass of sparkling water she hadn't touched, wearing her cultural attaché smile, while a deputy minister explained his vision for bilateral tariff reform to an audience that was mostly thinking about lunch.

*...the salmon looked good at the buffet earlier, wonder if there's any left...*

*...if Dreher mentions the import quota one more time I'm going to lose my mind...*

*...call Marta, tell her I'll be late, no, text her, if I call she'll want to talk about the bathroom renovation again...*

The usual hum. Thirty-odd bureaucrats and party functionaries in a room meant for twenty, the radiators working too hard, condensation on the windows turning the grey October sky into a smear. Anya let the thoughts wash through her and tried to pick out operational texture from the noise. Mostly there was none. These forums existed so mid-level officials could be seen attending them, and the thoughts in the room reflected that: career anxiety, social positioning, one woman silently ranking the men in the room by attractiveness with a specificity that made Anya want to take notes.

Then she found Damian.

He was standing near the front, to the left of the podium, in conversation with two men she didn't recognize. Dark suit, the posture he wore in government spaces — squared shoulders, the practiced ease of someone who'd grown up performing comfort in rooms like this. His surface thoughts were professional, half-listening to whoever was speaking to him while composing a mental draft of talking points for a meeting later that afternoon.

*...need to frame the border checkpoint issue as procedural, not political, Grosz was right about the framing...*

Normal. Controlled. Damian's mind in public settings was a disciplined thing — not blank, but organized, the thoughts of someone who'd learned to manage his own interior. Anya had noticed this at the cultural exchange event. He wasn't naturally closed; he just didn't let himself wander. Even now, the only stray thought she caught was a brief flicker about whether his dry cleaning would be ready before Friday.

She was about to let her attention drift back to the broader room when the man on Damian's left thought something that made her fingers tighten around her glass.

*...brought up the security meeting again, deflected, but Dreher told me he wasn't on the original invite list. Someone got him in. Why would a mid-level diplomat need access to defense procurement briefings?*

The man was tall, mid-forties, thinning blond hair combed carefully over a scalp that was losing the battle. His suit was better than his rank warranted — Anya could tell because it fit properly, and government-salary suits in Ostania never fit properly. He had a name tag she couldn't read from this distance, but his thoughts were loud and structured, the kind of organized suspicion that came from training rather than paranoia.

*...the Desmond name opens doors, always has, but this one's been opening different doors lately. The meeting with the naval attaché. The questions about the supply depot audit. Pattern.*

Anya set her glass down on the windowsill behind her. Her hand was steady. She'd been trained for this. She'd been trained by the best spy in the world, and the best assassin, and she could handle a policy forum with one suspicious functionary.

But the man's next thought scraped through her with the particular nausea of watching something dangerous unfold in real time:

*...report to Gessler tonight. Either Desmond's angling for a committee seat, which is fine, or he's running something off-book. If it's the second one, Gessler will want to know. Gessler always wants to know about the Desmond boys.*

Gessler. She didn't know the name. But the man's mental association was clear: Gessler was above him, connected, someone whose attention you directed toward people you wanted watched. The Renewal Front's internal structure was still opaque to Anya, but this had the shape of a chain — an informant flagging a suspected dissident for someone higher up.

She needed the man's name. She needed to see his badge.

Anya picked up her glass again and began a slow circuit of the room, the kind of aimless drift that cultural attachés were expected to perform at these events. Smile at a cluster of trade officials. Nod at a panel moderator she'd been introduced to once. Accept a canape from a tray and not eat it. Each step brought her closer to Damian's group, and with proximity the man's thoughts sharpened.

*...Desmond's doing the thing his father used to do — the charm offensive, the one where he agrees with everyone and commits to nothing. But Donovan was better at it. This one's got tells. He gets too specific when he's lying. Mentioned the Velden procurement audit by name, and that audit isn't public knowledge. Who told him about Velden?*

Close enough now. The badge read: BRANDT, K. — Ministry of the Interior, Policy Liaison.

Brandt, K., with his ill-hidden scalp and his well-fitted suit, was methodically building a case in his own head that Damian Desmond was a security risk. And he was going to report it tonight.

Anya's stomach turned. Not the dramatic plunge of a crisis, but the specific low-grade nausea of watching a trap close in real time while the person inside it smiled and shook hands and thought about dry cleaning. She had maybe hours before Brandt's report reached this Gessler. Maybe less, if Brandt decided to call instead of meeting in person.

She looked at Damian across twelve feet of overheated conference room. He was nodding at something one of the other men had said, his public face perfectly calibrated — interested, engaged, mildly amused. He didn't know Brandt was watching him. He didn't know Brandt was counting the doors he'd opened and the questions he'd asked and assembling them into a pattern that pointed at exactly what Damian was actually doing.

*...I'll tell Gessler to pull Desmond's access logs from the procurement office. If the pattern holds, we'll know by the weekend.*

Anya moved.

---

She came at the group from the side, arriving with the breezy momentum of someone who'd just spotted an acquaintance across a crowded room. Which, technically, she had.

"Mr. Desmond, hello again — I didn't know you'd be at this one." She extended her hand and gave him the attaché smile, the one that was warm without being memorable. "We met at the cultural exchange last week? Anna Kessler, Westalian mission."

Damian's surface thoughts registered recognition and something more complicated beneath it — a quick pulse of the half-familiarity that had been dogging him since the exchange event. *Her again. That laugh. Something about...*

He took her hand. "Of course. Ms. Kessler. The cultural attaché." His public voice was smooth, diplomatic, and his grip was brief and correct. But his thoughts added: *Why is she at an economic forum? Cultural attachés don't usually show up for tariff reform panels.*

Good. He was suspicious of her. That was fine. That was manageable. What wasn't manageable was Brandt, who was thinking:

*...Westalian diplomatic staff. Why is she approaching Desmond? Log that.*

Anya turned to Brandt with the full force of her professional friendliness. "I don't think we've met. Anna Kessler — I'm with the cultural liaison office at the Westalian mission. And you are...?"

"Brandt." He didn't offer a first name. His handshake was perfunctory, and his thoughts were already filing her: *Westalian. Cultural attaché. Approached Desmond specifically. Could be routine. Could be a contact protocol.*

She needed to break his focus on Damian. Not by being subtle — subtlety would just give him more data to analyze. She needed to make herself the more interesting object in the room.

"I actually came over because I overheard the tail end of your conversation about the cross-border trade metrics," Anya said, which was a lie; she'd heard nothing except Brandt's internal surveillance report on Damian. "Our office has been looking at the discrepancy between Ostanian and Westalian figures on the Velden corridor trade volume. Do you work on that at all?"

She'd chosen the word Velden deliberately. Brandt's own thoughts had flagged the Velden procurement audit as sensitive — by introducing it as a mundane trade topic, she was muddying the signal. If Velden came up in casual cross-border conversation, it was less significant that Damian had mentioned it.

Brandt's expression didn't change, but his thoughts flickered: *She mentioned Velden. Coincidence? The trade corridor stats are public, even if the procurement audit isn't. Different Velden issue.*

"The trade metrics are handled by the Commerce Ministry," Brandt said. His voice was flat, the kind of careful neutrality that intelligence-adjacent functionaries used when they weren't sure if they were being worked. "My office deals with policy coordination."

"Oh, of course, sorry. It's just that the bilateral figures have been a bit of a headache for us." Anya let herself fumble slightly — a half-step too eager, the enthusiastic junior diplomat who doesn't quite know where the boundaries are. "We keep getting asked to reconcile the numbers and nobody can agree on the baseline methodology. Do you know if there's been any standardization effort on the Ostanian side?"

The question was boring enough to be real. That was the trick — not to be interesting, but to be tediously plausible. Brandt's suspicion was built for pattern recognition, and what she was giving him was noise. A young Westalian attaché cornering people at forums to ask about trade methodology was annoying, not operational.

*...probably just doing her job. Westalian cultural staff are always wandering into the wrong meetings. Not a contact protocol. Too clumsy.*

The word *clumsy* stung, but it meant the performance was working.

Damian, meanwhile, was watching her with an expression of polite engagement and a mind that was working much faster. *She's deflecting him. That question about Velden — she introduced it to dilute the significance. She did that on purpose. How does she know about Velden?*

Anya kept her attention on Brandt, forcing the conversation deeper into the weeds of bilateral trade statistics while the operative's focus gradually loosened from Damian and settled into the irritated patience of a man being trapped in a conversation he hadn't asked for. She asked about import quotas. She asked about harmonization protocols. She asked about a statistical methodology paper she'd supposedly read, which she'd invented thirty seconds ago but described with enough specificity that Brandt had to take it seriously.

*...Jesus, she's still talking. I need to get to Dreher before the break ends...*

Three minutes. That was all she needed. Three minutes of Brandt's evening report not being about Damian Desmond but about the irritating Westalian attaché who'd wasted his time with questions about trade methodology. Three minutes of diluted focus, of the suspicion losing its sharp edges as the man's thoughts turned to logistics and scheduling and the salmon that might or might not still be available at the buffet.

Brandt excused himself with a politeness that barely qualified. Anya watched him go, tracking his thoughts as he crossed the room: *...forget about the Westalian. Talk to Dreher. The Desmond thing can wait until I've got the access logs.*

Can wait. Not abandoned, not forgotten. But delayed. She'd bought Damian time, not safety. The distinction sat in her like something she'd swallowed wrong.

She turned back to Damian, and the smile she found on his face was not the diplomatic one.

"Ms. Kessler," he said. "That was quite a detailed question about trade methodology."

"I'm thorough."

*She's not a cultural attaché. She's not even close to being a cultural attaché. Nobody who works in cultural liaison knows the word 'harmonization protocol.'*

"Clearly." His voice was even but his thoughts were racing, reassembling the last five minutes from a new angle: her arrival at the exact moment Brandt's questions were getting pointed, the Velden reference that muddied intelligence water she shouldn't have known was muddy, the performance of ditzy enthusiasm that had been precisely calibrated to bore an intelligence-adjacent functionary into disengagement.

"I should let you get back to—" she started.

"There's a service corridor behind the east stairwell," Damian said quietly, cutting her off. His public face hadn't changed. He was still smiling, still holding his posture, still looking for all the world like a diplomat wrapping up a pleasant conversation. But his voice had dropped and his thoughts had gone very still. "Five minutes."

He walked away. Anya stood there holding her untouched sparkling water and watched him rejoin a cluster of officials near the podium, and his surface thoughts were already back to talking points and procedural framing, smooth as a door closing.

Five minutes.

She put the glass down and headed for the east stairwell.

---

The service corridor was narrow, fluorescent-lit, and smelled like cleaning solution and the metallic tang of the building's ventilation system. Pipes ran along the ceiling in grey conduit. A mop bucket sat against the far wall, half full of brown water that nobody had emptied.

Damian was leaning against the wall with his hands in his pockets. His tie was loosened — the first time she'd seen any crack in his public presentation — and his jaw was tight. When Anya came through the door, his thoughts were:

*Don't be angry. Be smart. Figure out what she is, then be angry.*

"So," he said.

"So."

A silence. The fluorescent tube above them buzzed with a faint, irregular pulse. Somewhere in the walls, a pipe clanked.

"You're not a cultural attaché," Damian said. Not a question.

"I am, actually. It's just not the only thing I am."

*Intelligence. She's intelligence. Which service? Westalian — she's posted at the mission. WISE? BND? One of the smaller shops?* His thoughts were quick and organized, running through possibilities the way someone trained in political analysis would — not panicked, but rapid, systematic.

"The man you just rescued me from," he said. "Brandt. How did you know he was a problem?"

"I'm good at reading people."

His jaw tightened further. *That's not an answer. She knew about Velden — that's not public. She knew Brandt's angle of approach before he finished asking his questions. She anticipated his reporting chain. That's not 'reading people.' That's operational intelligence.*

"He was going to report you tonight," Anya said. Because this was the part where she had to give him something real, and the clock was running, and she couldn't protect him again from outside the conversation. "Someone named Gessler. Brandt's been watching your access patterns — the procurement briefings, the naval attaché meeting, the questions you've been asking about supply depots. He was putting together a case."

Damian's face went very still. The stillness wasn't calm. It was the specific rigidity of someone absorbing bad news while maintaining composure by force of will.

*Gessler. He knows about Gessler. That means either she's been investigating the same network I have, or she's been investigating me.*

"I'm not investigating you," Anya said, and then immediately wanted to bite her own tongue off because she'd responded to the thought, not the conversation, and now she had to cover the gap. "I mean — I know how this looks. Westalian operative shows up, knows your business, stages a rescue. I'd be suspicious too."

"You should know," he said, "that your cover has been suspicion for most of this conversation."

Fair.

"I work in intelligence," she said. "I can't tell you which service. I can tell you that we've been tracking the same threat you've been tracking, and the man who just walked out of that room is part of it."

*The Renewal Front. She knows about the Renewal Front.*

His thoughts went through a rapid sequence: surprise, then calculation, then something that looked almost like relief before he locked it down. The relief was what got her. He'd been doing this alone. Running his counter-operation against his own government's hardliners, using his dead father's network, and every ally he'd found so far was either old and frightened like Grosz or too compromised to trust fully. The idea that someone else knew — that someone with resources and institutional backing knew — produced a crack in his composure that he sealed almost instantly.

Almost.

"I don't know what you're talking about," he said. Diplomatic reflex. The words were automatic and he knew it and she knew it, and his thoughts said: *Stupid. She already knows. She just derailed Brandt in real time. The denial buys nothing.*

Anya waited. She had learned this from Loid, actually — the tactical value of silence. Let the other person close the distance. Don't fill the gap.

Damian looked at the mop bucket. Looked at the pipes. Looked at the fluorescent tube that was still buzzing like it had a personal grievance against the building's electrical system. Then he looked at her.

"My father built something," he said carefully. "An infrastructure. People, relationships, channels. It's still running. Some of the people who inherited it want to use it for things I don't agree with."

"The Renewal Front."

A muscle moved in his jaw. "I don't use that name."

"They do."

Another silence. His thoughts were louder now, the disciplined order fraying at the edges as the reality of the conversation sank in: *She knows the name. She knows about Brandt's chain. She may know about Grosz, about the network, about the whole operation. If she's hostile, I'm already burned. If she's not...*

"What do you want?" he asked.

"Same thing you want. To stop it before it gets worse."

*The phrasing is too vague to be operational. She's testing whether I'll specify. She wants me to say what I'm doing so she doesn't have to tell me what she already knows.* He almost smiled at that — the grim, tight-lipped recognition of someone who'd been raised in a household where conversational maneuvering was a survival skill.

"I'm running a counter-operation," Damian said. Each word landed with the weight of a decision being made. "Against elements within the Ostanian government who are using nationalist sentiment and defense procurement infrastructure to push both nations toward a conflict that benefits a small number of people on both sides of the border."

Both sides. She'd read that in his thoughts at the state dinner — the counter-operation wasn't just anti-Ostanian. It was anti-corruption on a bilateral scale. But hearing him say it aloud, committing to it as a stated position, was different. It cost him something. She could see the cost in his thoughts: *My father would call this treason. Maybe it is.*

"I can help," she said.

"In exchange for what?"

"Shared intelligence on the Renewal Front. Your network's operational picture, my network's analytical picture. We cover more ground together than apart."

*She's making it sound simple. It's not simple. My father was undone by trusting the wrong people. My brother would say this is exactly how foreign intelligence services compromise domestic assets. You let them in, you show them the architecture, and then you belong to them.*

"Terms," Damian said. His voice had gone quieter, which she was learning meant he was getting more serious, not less. The diplomatic polish was thinning and what was underneath was younger, more stubborn, more afraid than he'd have wanted her to know.

He crossed his arms. Not a power move — she could see that in his thoughts. It was self-containment. Something to do with his hands while the rest of him decided whether to walk out of this corridor or stay.

*...Father trusted the Westalian intermediary in '08. Gave him the channel architecture. Six months later the network was compromised and three people were dead. Trust is how they get you. Trust is how they always get you.*

The memory was vivid — not his own, she realized, but inherited. A story told enough times that it lived in him like experience. Donovan Desmond's cautionary tale, passed down to his sons like an heirloom: this is what happens when you let someone inside.

"Terms," she agreed. She kept her voice steady and tried not to think about the fact that she was reading his father's ghost in his thoughts while negotiating her way in. "Shared intelligence on the Front. No questions about each other's networks — I don't need to know your sources and you don't need to know mine. And either of us can walk away. No conditions, no penalties. If this stops working, it stops."

He stared at her. His thoughts moved through the terms like a lawyer examining a contract: *Reasonable. Too reasonable. The exit clause is the tell — nobody offers an exit clause unless they're confident you won't use it. Or unless they don't mean it.*

He was right. She didn't mean it. Not fully. Walking away from him now would mean walking away from the best intelligence source on the Renewal Front she'd ever found, and she'd already committed institutional treason to protect him. The exit clause was a courtesy, not a genuine offer.

And she was reading his doubts about it in real time, which meant she was negotiating with the cards face-up while he played blind. She'd done the same thing with Sylvia two days ago — sat across from her handler and constructed a lie while reading the trust that made the lie possible. Now she sat in a service corridor with a man she was supposed to be helping and read his fears and hesitations and used them, not to exploit, but to calibrate. To say the right things at the right moments. To know when to push and when to wait.

The difference between exploiting and calibrating was a line she'd drawn herself, and she wasn't sure it existed outside her own head.

*...she seems sincere. But seeming sincere is literally what intelligence operatives do. I can't verify anything she's told me. I can only verify what she did — she saw Brandt targeting me and she intervened, and the intervention was precise and effective, and she burned her own cover story to do it. That's not nothing.*

"How did you know about Brandt?" he asked again. "The truth, this time."

And here it was. The question she couldn't answer honestly. I read his mind. I heard him building a case against you inside his own thoughts. I knew he was going to report you because I listened to him decide to do it.

"My service has been monitoring Renewal Front activity channels," she said, which was true in the broadest sense and false in every specific one. "Brandt's name came up. When I saw him working you at the forum, I recognized the approach."

Damian held her gaze for a long moment. His thoughts: *She's lying. Not about all of it — the operational picture is probably real. But the specifics don't add up. She reacted too fast, too precisely. She didn't recognize an approach pattern. She knew, in real time, what he was about to do. That's not analysis. That's something else.*

He was sharper than she'd expected. Sharper than most people she'd worked. And the thing about sharp people was that they filed their doubts. They didn't press them in the moment — they stored them for later, and later always came.

"Okay," he said. Not *I believe you.* Just *okay.* The word sat in the fluorescent corridor like a provisional thing, a handshake that both parties knew was temporary.

"Okay," she said.

*...she's intelligence. She's good. She just saved my operation, maybe my life if Gessler had escalated. And I don't trust her. But I don't have to trust her. I just have to work with her. And working with someone is not the same as trusting them, and the fact that I want to trust her is exactly the reason I shouldn't.*

He pushed off the wall, straightened his tie, and became the diplomat again with a speed that was almost physical — the posture shifting, the jaw unclenching, the eyes going from wary to pleasant. The transformation was good. It was the kind of thing you learned growing up in a house where the dinner table was a stage.

"I'll be in touch," he said. "Through regular channels. Cultural exchange calendar."

"I check it every morning."

A ghost of something — not quite a smile. "You really aren't a cultural attaché."

"I really am. It's the other thing that's the problem."

He left first. She waited the standard two minutes, leaning against the wall opposite the mop bucket, listening to the pipes clank and the fluorescent tube buzz and the distant muffled noise of the forum continuing without them.

Her hands were shaking. She noticed this the way she noticed most things about her own body during operational work — late, and with faint surprise, as if the body was a peripheral system she'd neglected to monitor. The shaking wasn't fear. It was the specific tremor of sustained performance, the adrenaline afterburn of having managed three conversations at once for twenty minutes — Brandt's thoughts, Damian's thoughts, and the words coming out of her own mouth, each track running on a different frequency, each demanding a different version of herself.

She'd done it. Alliance formed. Terms set. Damian's operation preserved, for now. Brandt's report delayed, diluted, redirected. From the outside, it looked like a clean win.

From the inside, she'd just negotiated an alliance by reading her partner's mind without his knowledge or consent. She'd heard his doubts, his fears, his grudging hope, and she'd used all of it — not cruelly, not to harm, but to optimize. To say the right thing at the right time. To know when he needed reassurance and when he needed space. She'd been inside his negotiating position the entire time, and he'd been on the other side of the table playing blind, and he'd still chosen to work with her.

She didn't know if that made the alliance stronger or if it just made her the worst kind of partner. The kind who cheats before the game starts and then pretends the result was fair.

Two days ago, she'd sat across from Sylvia and filed a false report while reading the trust that enabled it. Today, she'd stood across from Damian and negotiated an alliance while reading the doubts that shaped it. Every successful deception widened the gap between Anya and every person she dealt with. Sylvia trusted her. Damian was starting to trust her. And both forms of trust rested on information she'd stolen and lies she'd told, and the lies worked because she was good at this, and being good at this was the thing she couldn't forgive.

The corridor was empty. The mop bucket sat in its corner, the dirty water dark and still. Anya pressed the heels of her hands against her eyes until she saw colors, and then she straightened up and went back to the forum and smiled at people and held her sparkling water and did not drink it.


# Chapter 5

Damian's car smelled like leather conditioner and something underneath it — cedar, maybe, or whatever cologne he'd put on and then apparently tried to remove, because the faintest chemical sweetness of soap clung to his collar.

*She can probably smell that. Can she smell that? It doesn't matter. Focus. The operational parameters—*

Anya focused on the Gesellschaft Club's membership roster instead. Damian had pulled it from somewhere — she didn't ask where, because their terms included not questioning each other's networks, and the less she knew about how a twenty-year-old diplomat obtained a private club's internal documents, the cleaner her operational conscience stayed. Not that her operational conscience was in great shape these days.

"Bretz arrives around eight," Damian said, turning the folder's pages with the controlled precision of someone handling classified material rather than a seating chart. "He's a creature of habit. Cognac in the salon, dinner at nine, never stays past eleven because his driver has an early rotation. His wife is—"

"Margarethe. I read the file."

"Right." A beat. *She always does that. Finishes the sentence before I— never mind.* "The point is, we have approximately three hours to get close enough for a sustained conversation, and Bretz doesn't like new people."

"He likes dogs."

"He brings the dog."

"He brings the dog to the club?"

"He brings the dog everywhere. He's a colonel. People accommodate colonels."

Anya looked at the club through the windshield. It sat on the corner of Lindenstrasse like an architectural rebuke — stone facade, wrought-iron balconies, the kind of building that had survived two wars and three governments because the people inside it were always useful to whoever was in charge. Light pooled behind tall curtains from windows on the first floor. A man in a dark overcoat was being admitted through the front door with a handshake and a nod.

"I still think we should approach him separately," Anya said. "You work the room, I find Bretz, we converge when I've got a read on his—"

"No. Bretz is old guard. He respects couples. He respects men who bring their partners to social functions because it means they've got nothing to hide." Damian's voice had the measured quality of someone who'd thought this through several times. "If you approach him alone, you're a young woman asking questions. If we approach together, we're a couple making small talk."

He wasn't wrong. She didn't like that he wasn't wrong, because it meant she had to do this his way, and his way involved—

"So," Damian said. He was looking straight ahead. "The cover."

"The cover."

"We're — they'll expect us to be. Together."

"That's what 'posing as a couple' means, yes."

His jaw tightened. Not anger — something worse. His thoughts were a controlled demolition failing to stay controlled: *Don't think about her arm. She's going to take your arm when you walk in. That's normal. Couples do that. Don't — professional. This is a professional arrangement. Your hand goes on the small of her back to guide her through a doorway and that is a NORMAL SOCIAL GESTURE that NORMAL PEOPLE—*

"Damian."

He looked at her.

"You're grinding your teeth."

"I'm not." He was. He stopped. *Was I? Damn it.* "I'm reviewing the operational parameters."

"You're reviewing them very loudly."

That came out wrong. She meant his visible tension — the rigid posture, the white-knuckle grip on the steering wheel of a parked car. She did not mean that she could hear his thoughts. That would have been — she couldn't have meant that.

He exhaled. A deliberate exhalation, the kind she imagined he'd learned at some diplomatic training seminar where they taught you to breathe through international incidents. "I'm fine. Let's go over the approach one more time."

"We enter as a couple. You introduce me to people. I steer us toward Bretz. You keep him talking about his military career — he's vain about it — while I read the room." She caught herself. "Read the room. Socially. See who's friendly, who's watching."

*Read the room.* He nodded. He didn't suspect anything. He was too busy thinking about how her dress — deep green, borrowed from a WISE wardrobe she'd rather not have known existed — sat against her collarbone, and then he was furiously not thinking about it, which produced a thought somehow louder than the original.

"The name," Anya said, because one of them had to stay operational. "Bretz has been linked to defense procurement irregularities. If the Renewal Front is funding operations through military contracts, Bretz knows who's coordinating the money. We need the name of his contact in the chain."

"And your assessment — from the file — is that he'll be relaxed enough here to let something slip?"

"People let things slip when they're comfortable," she said. Which was true. She just couldn't explain the mechanism.

"Fine." Damian straightened his tie — dark blue, knotted with the care of someone who'd been taught that a tie was a form of armor. "If this goes wrong—"

"It won't go wrong."

"—we leave separately. You through the west exit, me through the front. We don't acknowledge each other."

"It's not going to go wrong."

"First rule of operations," he said. "Plan for what goes wrong."

*His father probably told him that,* Anya thought, and then wished she hadn't, because Damian's thoughts flickered — brief, involuntary — toward something dark and heavy that she wasn't going to follow. She looked out the window instead.

He gathered the folder, straightened his cuffs, and opened his door. The autumn air came in cold, carrying the smell of damp leaves and distant frying oil from a street vendor somewhere south.

"All right," he said. He walked around to open her door, which she hadn't expected. His thoughts: *This is what a boyfriend would do. I am performing the role of a boyfriend. I am not actually—*

She took his arm.

*Oh,* he thought.

They walked toward the club.

***

The Gesellschaft Club's interior matched its exterior: old money whispering through old wood. Dark panels lined the walls of the entrance hall, punctuated by oil paintings of men in uniforms who all shared the same expression of institutional self-regard. A crystal chandelier threw complicated light across a marble floor where a doorman in a coat that had its own dry-cleaning schedule was checking names against a leather-bound ledger.

Damian handled the door. His public mode engaged like a switch — the grinding tension vanished and a smooth, warm, slightly self-deprecating version of himself appeared in its place. "Desmond," he said to the doorman. "And guest."

The doorman found his name, nodded, and waved them through with the practiced disinterest of someone who had been paid not to be curious.

The reception occupied a long salon with high ceilings and too many candles. Thirty or forty people clustered around a bar and several cocktail tables, their conversation producing the specific acoustic texture of polite society — measured laughter, clinking glass, the rustle of expensive fabric. The crowd thought: a trade agreement, a bad hip, a new mistress, a parking fine, the price of heating oil, whether it was too early to leave without seeming rude, an argument about an inheritance that had been going on since June.

Anya breathed.

The noise was manageable. This wasn't a state dinner — fewer minds, less pressure, and most of these people were thinking about nothing more threatening than their investments. She tuned down the static and looked for Bretz.

She found him.

Colonel Bretz stood near a display of watercolors — harbor scenes, boats, competent but uninspired — with a glass of cognac in one hand and, at his side, the largest dog Anya had ever seen outside of a veterinary textbook.

The dog was the color of wet straw and roughly the size of a small couch. Its head came up to Bretz's hip, and Bretz was not a short man. It sat with the dignified patience of an animal that knew it was the most important creature in any room and saw no reason to prove it.

*OK,* Anya thought. *That's a very big dog.*

"That's a very big dog," Damian murmured beside her.

Bretz himself was sixty or so, with the thickened build of a man who'd been fit once and had since negotiated a truce with good meals. His face was ruddy and creased in the pattern of someone who spent a lot of time outdoors squinting at things he disapproved of. He was thinking about his sciatica. Specifically, he was thinking that the chair he'd been sitting in earlier had set the nerve on fire in his left hip, and that if his wife made him stand for another hour he was going to sit down whether she liked it or not, and that he didn't care if she thought he was embarrassing because he was a colonel and he had earned the right to sit down when his back was killing him.

He was the most human person in the room.

"Colonel Bretz," Damian said, stopping at a polite distance. "What a pleasure. Damian Desmond — we met briefly at the Eastbridge reception last spring."

Bretz turned. His thoughts registered: *Desmond. The younger one. Diplomatic corps. His father was—* and then a careful blankness, the kind of disciplined non-thought that military men produced around political names. "Desmond. Yes, I remember. You're the one who didn't eat the fish."

"The fish was ambitious," Damian said.

Bretz laughed — a single, gruff syllable. His sciatica sent a complaint up his hip and he shifted his weight. "Who's your friend?"

"My girlfriend," Damian said, and the word landed in his thoughts like a glass dropped on tile: *Girlfriend. I said girlfriend. That's the cover. I said it correctly. Why does it sound like—*

Anya smiled. "Elena," she said. "I've heard wonderful things about the club."

"It's all right," Bretz said. "The cognac's decent. The company varies." He glanced at Damian with the appraising look of an older man sizing up a younger one through the woman he'd brought. Whatever he found was satisfactory enough. He drank his cognac.

The dog chose this moment to notice Anya.

It happened in stages. First, the massive head swiveled toward her. Then the nostrils flared — an audible intake of air, like a bellows engaging. Then the dog stood up, which was a process that involved considerable vertical displacement, and walked directly to Anya with the unhurried certainty of a glacier.

It pressed its nose into her palm.

*Oh. Oh no. OK. Hello.*

"Kaiser likes you," Bretz said, and it was the first genuine warmth in his voice. "He doesn't like most people."

Kaiser was now leaning against Anya's leg with his full weight, which was substantial. She shifted her stance to keep from toppling. The dog's thoughts were simple and clear: *Good. Warm. Smells good. Stay.* And then, with uncomplicated animal conviction: *Mine now.*

"He's beautiful," Anya said, because saying *your dog has just claimed me as personal property* seemed operationally ill-advised. She scratched behind Kaiser's ears. The dog's eyes closed in slow bliss. His tail, which was approximately the size and force of a rolled magazine, began to thump against a side table.

"Careful with the tail," Bretz said. "He knocked over a Reinhardt last month. The club committee wasn't — they weren't happy about it."

"Damian!" A voice cut across the salon like a ceremonial horn. A woman in emerald silk was bearing down on them with the velocity of someone who had spotted exactly the person she'd been looking for. "Damian Desmond, I KNEW it was you!"

Damian's thoughts: *No. No no no. Who is — I don't—*

"Margarethe Bretz," Anya murmured, just before the woman arrived. Bretz's wife. She'd seen the photos in the file, but the file hadn't captured the sheer propulsive force of Margarethe Bretz's social energy. Mid-fifties, handsome, with the bearing of someone who organized things — committees, parties, other people's seating arrangements, other people's lives.

"Liebling, you didn't tell me you'd invited the young Desmond," Margarethe said to her husband, who had not invited anyone. Then she turned the full beam of her attention on Damian. "You look exactly like that actor. What's his name — the one from that film about the diplomat who falls in love with the — oh, you know the one."

"I don't think—" Damian started.

"Friedrich Engel! That's it. You're the spitting image. Has anyone told you? I'm sure people tell you all the time."

*Nobody has ever told me this. I have no idea who Friedrich Engel is.* "It's come up," Damian said, because he was, despite everything, still a diplomat.

Margarethe seized his arm with the practiced authority of someone accustomed to seizing things. "You must come meet the Hasslers. Ernst was just saying the other day that the diplomatic corps needs more young men, and you have SUCH a presence, doesn't he, Heinrich?"

"His presence is fine," Bretz said, without looking up from his cognac.

"Come, come." Margarethe was already towing Damian away from the watercolors with a force that suggested she could have had a career in maritime salvage. He threw Anya a look over his shoulder — the controlled helplessness of a man being extracted by a superior force.

*Sorry,* he thought. *I'll get back as soon as — she's very — I'll try—*

Anya watched him go. Then she looked at Bretz. Then she looked at Kaiser, who was still leaning against her leg with the structural commitment of a flying buttress.

"Your wife is... energetic," Anya said.

Bretz drank his cognac. "That's a word for it." He was thinking: *Margarethe and her celebrities. Last month it was that opera singer. The month before that she cornered some painter at the Langsdorf benefit. She'll have the boy pinned for an hour at least.* Then, with weary affection: *She's happier at these things than I am. Always has been.*

An hour. That was either a problem or an opportunity. Without Damian to steer the conversation toward military matters, Anya was working alone with a colonel who didn't know her and a dog she couldn't shake. But Bretz was relaxed here — this was his club, his cognac, his watercolors. And his wife had just removed the social obligation that might have kept his guard up.

"Do you collect?" Anya asked, nodding at the harbor scenes on the wall.

"God, no. These are terrible. Koenig — he's a member — he paints them. We all pretend they're acceptable because his wife left him last year and he needs the encouragement." Bretz shifted his weight again, his sciatica flaring. *Damn nerve. If I sit down Margarethe will tell me later I was being antisocial. If I don't sit down I'm going to be in bed for two days.* "I've got a few things at home. Nothing serious. Some landscapes. A Feldmann."

"I love Feldmann." Anya did not love Feldmann. Anya had looked up Feldmann twenty minutes ago in the car and could recall exactly one painting, which featured a cow in unconvincing light. "The pastoral work especially."

"Mm." Bretz was not interested in discussing Feldmann. He was thinking about his brother-in-law. *That bastard Gerhard borrowed another three thousand marks last Tuesday. Told Margarethe it was for the car. It's never for the car. It's for the card games at Oschmann's. Everybody knows except Margarethe, or she knows and pretends not to, which is worse.*

This was the texture of Bretz's mind. Not a military intelligence problem, not a Renewal Front contact — a man with a bad back and a wife who spent more than he'd like and a brother-in-law who gambled and an enormous dog who was, at this moment, attempting to nose his way into Anya's handbag where it hung against her hip.

She redirected Kaiser with her knee. "Down," she whispered.

Kaiser looked at her with wounded dignity. *But bag. Bag smells interesting.* He sat, but maintained nasal surveillance.

"Your dog is very determined," Anya said.

"He gets it from his mother. She was a military service dog — used to pull equipment carts at the border garrison. Kaiser thinks he's still on duty." Bretz's expression softened when he talked about the dog. He scratched Kaiser's flank absently, and the dog leaned into it. *Good dog. Only honest thing in this building.*

*Military,* his thoughts drifted. *The procurement review is Tuesday. Hartig wants the numbers before the committee even looks at the allocation. Hartig always wants the numbers early. Says it's efficient. Efficient for who—*

There. The edge of something.

Anya leaned against the wall, casual, letting Kaiser's bulk serve as cover for her proximity to Bretz. "It must be exhausting, though. The military life. My father was career military and he said the bureaucracy was worse than the enemy."

Bretz grunted. "Your father was right. I spend more time filling out procurement forms than I ever spent on a firing range." His thoughts ran: *The new defense contracts. Hartig's been pushing the Krueger-Schiff allocation through the secondary channel before the committee reviews it. Said it was standard procedure but nobody routes four hundred thousand through a secondary authorization without a reason.*

She needed to keep him in this track.

"My father always said the contract people were the real power in the military," she offered.

"They are." Bretz's face darkened. "You want to know who runs things? It's not the generals. It's the procurement officials who decide which contracts go where. And some of them — " He stopped himself. Looked at her. *Why am I talking about this with a girl I just met? She's Desmond's girlfriend. She doesn't need to hear about—*

Kaiser chose this precise moment to lunge for the handbag.

It happened fast. The massive head shot forward, jaws closing around something — her compact, it turned out — and then thirty-plus kilograms of dog was backing away from her with the clumsy triumph of an animal who had finally completed his mission. His tail hammered a cocktail table. A glass rattled. A woman nearby flinched.

"Kaiser! No!" Anya grabbed for the compact. Kaiser dodged — surprisingly nimble for his size — and trotted three paces toward the bar, tail wagging, the compact clamped between his jaws like a prize duck.

"Kaiser, drop it," Bretz said, with the resignation of a man who had been saying this sentence for seven years.

The dog did not drop it. A bartender was staring. Two women at a nearby table had turned to watch with the delighted horror of people witnessing someone else's social catastrophe.

Bretz bent to retrieve it and his sciatica fired like a flare: *Christ. My hip. My goddamn hip.* He straightened slowly, one hand on his lower back, and managed to extract the compact from Kaiser's mouth with the other. It came free with a strand of drool connecting it to the dog's lower lip like a suspension bridge.

"I apologize," Bretz said, holding the compact out to her at arm's length, slightly mortified. "He's usually — well, he's never well-behaved, but he's not usually this committed."

Anya took the compact back. It was warm and damp. She wiped it on the inside of her coat pocket without looking at it. "It's fine. He's got good taste."

Bretz laughed — a real laugh this time, not the diplomatic kind. Two syllables, rough and surprised. His guard dropped. *She's all right. Desmond's girl. Better than the usual crowd in this place. At least she doesn't pretend to like Koenig's paintings.*

Kaiser circled back and pressed himself against Anya's leg again, unrepentant. His thought was serene: *Good. She's still here.*

And then, like water following a familiar channel, Bretz's mind returned to the thing that had been sitting under the surface all evening: *Hartig. The meeting Tuesday. Four hundred thousand through the secondary channel. "Strategic national interest" — that's the phrase he uses. Same phrase every time. And the authorization came from the defense ministry's discretionary fund, which means no parliamentary oversight, which means—*

The name was there. Not a vague reference — a specific person, a specific position, a specific pattern.

*Erich Hartig. Undersecretary for Defense Procurement. He's been routing contract allocations outside the standard review process for at least six months. Says the committee is too slow, says national security can't wait for bureaucratic timelines. And maybe he's right. But nobody routes that much money that quietly unless someone told them to.*

Bretz took a long pull of his cognac. His sciatica throbbed. His thoughts continued, more tired than angry: *And I keep signing the forms because what am I going to do? Go to the Inspector General? With what? I've got a feeling, not evidence. Hartig's got authorization stamps from the ministry itself. I'm a colonel with a bad hip and six years to retirement. I'm not the man who makes the complaint. I'm the man who signs the form.*

He wasn't corrupt. He wasn't even complicit, not in the way that mattered. He was a tired man inside a system he could see was wrong but couldn't afford to challenge, doing his job because the alternative was everything he'd built for thirty years burning down around his pension. He thought about Hartig the way a clerk thinks about the executive three floors up who makes decisions that shape the clerk's life without knowing his name.

Anya stood very still while Kaiser nuzzled her knee and Bretz complained about his hip and the salon hummed with forty minds thinking about wine and weather and money, and she held the name like a key she'd found in a coat pocket — small, specific, waiting for a lock.

Across the room, Damian was trapped. Margarethe had him cornered between a potted fern and a heavyset man who was explaining something with great enthusiasm and broad hand gestures. Damian's thoughts reached her across the crowd noise: *I am going to die here. I am going to die next to this fern listening to a man describe his horse's digestive requirements and nobody will find my body until they water the plant—*

Anya bit the inside of her cheek.

"You all right?" Bretz asked.

"Fine. Sorry — your dog is standing on my foot."

Kaiser was standing on her foot. His full weight bore down on her left instep through the thin sole of a borrowed formal shoe. She shifted. He shifted with her, maintaining contact like a satellite tracking its ground station.

They talked for another fifteen minutes. Bretz told her about the club's new menu, which he disapproved of. He told her about the charity dinner his wife had committed him to next month. He told her about his brother-in-law's latest request for money, which he was going to refuse this time, which he had said the last four times. His thoughts confirmed what his words offered: he was a man in the last stretch of a long career, managing a body that was failing him and a family that was costing him and a conscience that occasionally kept him up at night but not long enough to make him do anything about it.

He was not a villain. He was not even particularly useful to whoever was using those procurement channels, except as the man who signed the forms and didn't ask why. He bore the specific human weight of someone who had chosen compliance over confrontation at every juncture and was living with the accumulated residue of those choices, and his dog loved him, and his wife drove him crazy but he couldn't imagine dinner without her, and his back hurt.

Damian materialized at her elbow. His tie was subtly looser. He looked like someone who had survived a natural disaster and was trying to pretend it hadn't happened.

*The horse man,* his thoughts said, with the shell-shocked flatness of a veteran. *Forty-five minutes about equine digestion. Forty-five.*

Kaiser sniffed Damian once, thoroughly, then turned back to Anya. Dismissed.

"We should go," she murmured, leaning into his arm — the cover, and also the only thing keeping Kaiser from following her to the bar. "I've got what we need."

Damian's thoughts shifted immediately. The diplomatic exhaustion vanished and something sharper took its place. *She got it. Already?* "You're sure?"

"I'm sure."

He looked at her for a beat longer than operational necessity required. *How does she— every time. She was alone with him for twenty minutes and she—* He shook it off. "All right. We leave together. Normal pace."

They made their exit. Damian produced a gracious goodbye — thanking Bretz for the conversation, complimenting the club's watercolors with exactly enough sincerity to be polite and not enough to be believed, apologizing for the early departure with the seamless regret of someone who had been trained since childhood to leave rooms well. Bretz shook his hand and told him the fish was better now, if he ever came back.

Kaiser watched Anya go. He stood at Bretz's side and his massive head tracked her all the way to the door, and his thought — clear and simple and devastating in its sincerity — was: *Come back.*

Anya did not look at the dog again because she was a professional, and also because if she looked at the dog she was going to feel something about it and she couldn't afford to feel things about a colonel's dog right now when she was holding the name of a man who was funneling defense money outside parliamentary oversight.

The night air hit them on the steps. Cold — real cold, autumn with its teeth in, not the decorative chill of early October. Anya exhaled and watched her breath vanish.

"Erich Hartig," she said quietly. "Undersecretary for Defense Procurement. He's routing contract allocations through a secondary channel — four hundred thousand marks that Bretz knows about, probably more he doesn't. The authorization comes from the defense ministry's discretionary fund. No parliamentary oversight. Bretz has been signing the forms for months."

Damian stopped walking. His thoughts went very still. *Hartig. Defense procurement. The discretionary fund. That's — if the Renewal Front is using military contracts to fund its operations, this is the pipe. This is how the money moves.* He looked at her. "Bretz told you this?"

"Bretz told me he collects Feldmann landscapes and his brother-in-law owes him three thousand marks. The rest I— pieced together. From context." She met his eyes. "He doesn't know he gave us anything."

Damian's expression — the real one, underneath the diplomatic polish — was something she hadn't seen before. Not quite admiration, not quite wariness. Something between. *She scares me,* he thought, and then immediately: *Not like that. She's just — she's very good at this. Unnervingly good.* And then: *Stop thinking about how good she is. Focus.*

"Hartig reports to someone," he said. "Nobody runs a shadow procurement channel on their own authority."

"Bretz thinks so too. He doesn't know who."

"I might." Damian started walking again. His thoughts were working — connecting the Hartig name to things Anya couldn't see, intelligence from his father's old network, patterns he'd been tracking. *The defense ministry restructuring two years ago. The new discretionary authorities. If Hartig is the mechanism, then the question is who built him.* "This gives us something to trace."

"That's why we came."

They crossed the street toward his car.

***

The park was two blocks from the club. They sat on a bench under a linden tree whose leaves had gone gold and were falling in the windless air, drifting down in slow spirals that caught the light from a distant streetlamp and turned briefly bright before landing dark on the gravel. Anya's shoes were off — the borrowed heels had been punishing her feet for three hours and one hour of being used as a dog's landing pad — and her stockinged toes curled against the cold. Damian had loosened his tie and undone his top button, which was, for him, the sartorial equivalent of complete collapse.

It was quiet. The park was empty except for them and the linden and the sound of a tram somewhere east, its bell carrying across the rooftops.

"Hartig," Damian said again, turning the name over. He'd been doing this since they left the club — repeating it, connecting it to things, building the architecture of what it meant. *If Hartig controls the procurement pipe, then the Renewal Front isn't operating on ideology alone. It has infrastructure. Funded infrastructure. That changes what we're dealing with.*

"It changes the timeline too," Anya said. "If they've been running money through the discretionary fund for six months, they're not planning something. They're building something."

"Yeah." He looked at the ground between his shoes. "It's always further along than you think."

They sat with that. A leaf landed on the bench between them. Neither moved it.

"The horse man," Damian said.

Anya snorted.

"His name is Ernst Hassler. He has three horses named after Ostanian generals, two ex-wives, and — I want you to understand I'm not exaggerating — a forty-five-minute theory about the relationship between oat quality and equine temperament." A pause. His mouth twitched. "Margarethe also wanted me to come to dinner next Thursday. To meet her niece. She said her niece would love my bone structure."

"Did you say yes?"

"I said I'd check my schedule." *Which is diplomatic for I would rather eat the horse.* He almost said it aloud but caught himself. What he actually said was: "Margarethe told me Friedrich Engel is forty-seven and balding. So the resemblance is apparently spiritual rather than physical."

The laugh came out of Anya before she could manage it — not her polished social laugh or her operational that's-amusing-keep-talking laugh but the real one, the one that started somewhere below her ribs and came out slightly too loud and had a catch at the end that she hated because it sounded like a hiccup and she could never control it. She pressed her hand over her mouth but it was already out, loose in the cold air.

Damian was looking at her.

His thoughts stopped.

Not slowed — stopped. Like a record player lifting its needle. For a half-second there was nothing, just a blankness that Anya had learned to recognize as the instant before something surfaced from deep water.

Then:

*She has the same laugh.*

Not the vague familiarity from the cultural exchange event, the half-recognition at the policy forum. This was specific. A memory rising through murky water, gaining shape and color as it climbed.

*A schoolyard. The big linden tree near the east wall of the campus — the one everybody climbed, the one with the flat branch halfway up where you could sit and see the whole field. An afternoon. Autumn, like this. A girl sitting on that branch. She was — six? Seven? She'd beaten him at something. The relay race. The one where the teams were picked by the teachers and she ended up on the opposing side and she was FAST, impossibly fast for someone that small, and he'd been so sure he was going to win because he'd been training all week in the garden and—*

*She beat him. She beat him by four steps and then she climbed the tree to watch the next race and she sat up there laughing. Not at him. Not cruelly. Just this enormous, delighted, graceless laugh because she'd won and it was a beautiful afternoon and she was six years old and everything was exactly as good as it was supposed to be.*

*And her hair was—*

*Pink,* Damian thought. *Her hair was pink. And she laughed just like—*

He blinked. The memory wavered at the surface, almost whole. Anya could see it in his mind like watching someone reach for a glass on the edge of a table — the fingers stretching, brushing the rim. The name was close. Closer than it had ever been. A girl. A specific girl. Anya—

The glass didn't fall. It didn't settle back either. It just hung there, trembling, at the edge.

*That's strange,* he thought. He shook his head — a tiny movement, barely visible. *That was a long time ago. Why did I — that girl. What was her—*

The thought dissolved. Not all the way. It sank back down to where it lived, still there, still reaching, but no longer breaking the surface.

"What?" Anya said. Her voice sounded normal. She was almost sure her voice sounded normal.

"Nothing." He almost smiled. "You just reminded me of someone. For a second."

"Good someone or bad someone?"

He considered it. *Good,* he thought, and the thought carried warmth and an ache and the specific golden quality of autumn light through the branches of a tree he hadn't seen in fourteen years. What he said was: "Haven't decided yet."

Anya pulled her coat tighter. The cold had deepened while they sat here, and her feet were going numb in the damp air. She should say something operational. She should say they should go.

She didn't, for a moment. She sat on the bench with the linden dropping its leaves around them and the name Erich Hartig sitting between them like a grenade with a slow fuse, and she looked at Damian looking at the dark shapes of the trees against the city's reflected glow, and she thought about a schoolyard she hadn't thought about in years.

The relay race. She remembered it differently, of course. She remembered reading his thoughts during the race — the fierce, absolute determination to win, the fury when he realized she was ahead of him, and then, when she crossed the line first, a feeling she hadn't expected: grudging admiration, buried so deep under wounded pride that she almost missed it. She'd climbed the tree because she'd wanted to be somewhere nobody was thinking at her. And she'd laughed because she was six and it was autumn and the leaves were gold and she didn't yet know what it cost to be inside everyone's head all the time.

She didn't know, sitting on this bench, whether the warmth she felt right now was hers or an echo of his. The warmth was indistinguishable from its source. It usually was.

"Good operation," Damian said.

"Chaotic operation."

"Those are the same thing, in my experience."

"That's a terrible philosophy of operations."

"It's gotten me this far."

"You're twenty. That's not far."
