He laughed. Not a big laugh — a short exhale through his nose, the beginning of something he decided not to finish. But his thoughts loosened, and for a moment he wasn't the diplomat or the counter-operative or Donovan Desmond's son. He was just a tired person on a bench, glad to be sitting next to someone instead of sitting alone.

*I could tell him,* Anya thought. Not about the telepathy. Not about the false report. But about Eden. About the tree and the relay race and the name he was reaching for.

She didn't.

"We should go," she said. "It's cold."

"Yeah." He stood. Offered his hand to help her up — the cover, and also just the thing he did. She took it. His fingers were warm. His thought, unbidden: *Her hand is freezing.*

She pulled her shoes back on, grimacing at the cold leather. They walked to the car without talking, their breath making brief clouds that dissolved in the lamplight, and Anya carried the name of a man who was moving money for people who wanted a war, and the sound of her own laugh living inside someone else's memory of a golden afternoon, and the knowledge that the boy walking beside her was one clear moment away from remembering everything.

The fuse was shorter than it had been this morning. She could see it burning, and she couldn't put it out, and she wasn't sure anymore that she wanted to.


# Chapter 6

The stairwell smelled like garlic and something that had crossed over. Sugar, butter, and heat pushed past the point of partnership into open hostility — a scorched sweetness that stuck to the walls and would probably still be there tomorrow. Her mother was cooking.

Anya let herself in with the spare key she'd carried since she was nine. The apartment hit her the way it always did: warm and cluttered and smelling faintly of Bond's fur beneath whatever Yor had done to the kitchen. Loid's shoes were lined up by the door, toes to the wall, precisely parallel. Yor's were kicked off at different angles, one on its side. Bond was asleep on the couch with his head hanging off the armrest, his white fur covering the cushions in a thin permanent layer that no amount of vacuuming had ever fully addressed.

"Anya!" Yor appeared from the kitchen doorway, flushed and holding a wooden spoon like a weapon she'd forgotten to put down. "You're early! I was going to — the stew isn't quite — well, it's almost — it's getting there, I think."

*She used too much heat again. The bottom layer is definitely burned. But the top might be — no. The top is also burned. Oh no.*

"Smells great, Mama."

"Does it? Really?" Yor's face brightened with a hope that made Anya's chest hurt. "Loid said it smelled 'interesting,' which I think means—"

"It means it smells interesting," Loid said from inside the kitchen, where he was wiping down the counter with the unhurried efficiency of a man who'd already completed his own preparations and was now managing the aftermath of someone else's. He came out drying his hands on a dish towel. "Welcome home."

He hugged her. Brief and warm, his arms tightening once before letting go. His thoughts were louder than the embrace: *She's thinner. The circles under her eyes are worse than last month. She's not sleeping enough — is she running double shifts or is this something else? I should ask. I shouldn't ask. She'll tell me if it's operational. She won't tell me if it's personal. She never does.*

Anya pulled back and smiled at him. "I'm fine, Papa."

"I didn't say anything."

"You were about to."

He gave her the look — the one that was half-amused and half-something harder, the spy's assessment running beneath the father's warmth. It lasted less than a second. Then it softened and he was just her father again, and he said, "There's rice in the cooker. And I made a small thing with the leftover chicken, in case you want options."

"The stew is fine!" Yor called from the kitchen. There was a clatter, then a smaller clatter, then a pause. "Sorry! Fine. Everything's fine."

Anya dropped her bag by the door and crossed to the couch. Bond opened one eye, registered her, and closed it again. His tail moved once against the cushion — a single thump of acknowledgment that constituted his entire greeting now that his joints were what they were. She scratched behind his ears and felt his thoughts drift through her like warm water: *girl. home. floor cold. sleep.* The simplicity of a dog's interior had always been a relief. No layers. No second meanings. Just the fact of the moment and the body that inhabited it.

She sat on the arm of the couch and let the apartment settle around her. The television was on low — a news broadcast about ministerial trade talks that nobody was watching. Loid's reading glasses sat folded on a stack of newspapers he'd already annotated with small pencil marks in the margins. They looked like idle habit. They were an indexing system he maintained by reflex, cross-referencing sources and flagging discrepancies, and Anya had known this since she was seven and had read the system in his thoughts while he taught her long division. Yor's cardigan was draped over a chair. Two of Bond's rubber toys were lined up near the bookshelf in a row too orderly for a dog to have arranged — Loid's hand, tidying without noticing.

Loid settled into his chair and picked up the newspaper. His thoughts moved beneath the newsprint like current under ice. *The border patrol deployments are increasing again. Sylvia mentioned adjusted threat levels for the southeastern corridor last week. If Anya's sector is affected — no. She'd tell me. Or she wouldn't. She's twenty. She has her own operational judgment and I have to trust it even when I don't.*

The worry wasn't new. He carried it like he carried everything — efficiently, without display, and with the specific self-awareness of a man who knew that his protectiveness was partly surveillance and couldn't make himself stop. Anya had been reading this particular strain of his thoughts for years. It never got easier.

"The news said something about the trade negotiations stalling," she said, because it was the kind of thing a daughter would say.

"They'll restart. They always do." He turned a page. *She's asking about geopolitics on a family visit. That's either casual or it's not.*

Dinner was the stew. Yor served it with a hopefulness that bordered on prayer, ladling it into bowls with extra care as if gentleness could retroactively improve the cooking. Anya ate it. The stew was thick and combative and tasted like three different recipes had argued over the same pot and nobody had won. But her mother had spent the afternoon on it, and love was the only ingredient that had survived the heat, and the backup rice was sitting discreetly on the stove for anyone who needed rescue.

"Mrs. Kaplan complained about the pipes again," Yor said, blowing on her spoon. "She said the water pressure on the fourth floor is — what was her word — 'insulting.' She said the water pressure was insulting to her personally."

"It was low last week," Loid said. "I adjusted the valve in the basement."

"She didn't seem to know that. She was telling Bond about it in the hallway for about ten minutes. I think she believes he's the building superintendent."

"Bond was in the hallway?"

"He got out when I was bringing in the groceries. He just lay down in front of her door and she started talking. I didn't want to interrupt."

Anya laughed. It came out real and easy and for a moment she wasn't carrying anything. The sound of it filled the kitchen — too loud, probably, for a joke about water pressure and an old dog, but she hadn't laughed in days and the release was involuntary.

*She should laugh more. When did she stop laughing like that? She used to — stop. She's here. Don't catalog what's wrong. She's here.*

Loid's thought pressed against her and she looked at her plate.

"How's work, Mama? Besides the pipes."

"Oh, fine, fine." Yor waved her spoon. "Busy, but — you know. They reorganized the whole records system at city hall, which was, um. An experience. I don't think Mr. Henning has recovered. He kept trying to file things in the old cabinets and they'd already been removed, so he was just standing in front of an empty wall holding folders. I felt terrible for him."

"Did you help?"

"I helped him find a chair and made tea. That seemed like the right amount."

Yor smiled, and the smile was real, and then beneath it — not deep, just passing through — the way a cloud passes across the sun and is gone before you can look up:

*— the man on his knees. Begging. His hands weren't like the briefing said. The briefing said armed, dangerous, but his hands were empty and he was saying please, I have a — and the layout was wrong too, the door was on the wall where they'd said the window would be, the whole floor plan was*

Yor picked up her spoon. "Anyway. The new system is fine. Just takes getting used to."

She was smiling again. The thought was gone, pushed back down to wherever Yor kept the things she couldn't hold and couldn't put down. She'd buried it the way she handled every dark thing — by being present and kind and not looking at the shape of what she'd just remembered.

Anya's hand had stopped over her bowl. The stew had gone cold on the spoon she was holding midair. She lowered it. She had not reached for that. She had not pressed, had not focused, had not done any of the things she did when she was working. Her mother's private horror had simply been there, close to the surface for a second, and Anya had caught it the way she caught everything — involuntarily, completely, without consent from either of them.

A man on his knees. Hands that didn't match the file. A floor plan that was wrong. Her mother was beginning to wonder whether the people the Garden told her to kill were who they said they were. It was a new thought — raw and small and unfinished — and Yor wasn't ready to follow it yet. She was tucking it away the same way she tucked away every contradiction in her life: by choosing the warm thing over the true thing and hoping the warm thing would hold.

Anya knew all of this because she had stolen it.

She reached for the bread. She could not use this knowledge. She could not mention it, reference it, act on it. She possessed operational intelligence about a covert assassination organization, pulled from her mother's unguarded mind while her mother hummed and served stew. The only decent thing she could do was pretend she'd never heard it.

"This bread's good," she said.

"The place on Kirchner Street," Loid said. "The woman with the — what do you call it — firm customer service approach."

"She's mean," Yor said.

"She's efficient."

"She's efficiently mean. She told me I was buying too much rye."

"Were you?"

"That's not the point, Loid."

---

Bond woke up.

Not the slow surfacing of an old dog working his way out of sleep — the groaning, leg-cycling, dream-chasing kind where his nose twitched at phantom smells and gravity gradually hauled him back to the couch. This was different. His head came up rigid, eyes open and locked on nothing, ears pressed flat against his skull. His whole body went taut, the loose-limbed mass of him transformed into something that looked like the animal he'd been before the grey muzzle and the stiff joints and the years.

"Bond?" Yor half-rose from her chair.

Anya was already in it. Bond's mind had cracked open like a door kicked in and the images were flooding through — not thoughts, not words, but raw visual data pouring in faster than she could organize it.

A building. She saw it through Bond's processing — a wide stone facade with columns, not decorative but structural, the heavy pale stone of old government architecture. Wide ceremonial steps leading up to the entrance. She tried to hold the image but Bond's attention was already swinging — the same building from a different angle, a terrace extending from the upper level with an iron railing, a balustrade of carved stone overlooking landscaped grounds that were wrong, torn up, the grass and ornamental trees displaced. And then people — uniformed figures, four or five of them, wearing the stiff grey-blue formal dress of Ostanian state security. They were moving fast. One was running. And behind them, behind the terrace, a blooming brightness — not daylight but fire, the specific aggressive orange that meant accelerant, that meant deliberate, rising from below the terrace and cracking the balustrade stone. The heat of it. The feeling of wrongness, which was Bond's way of processing what his precognition couldn't put into words — not a prediction but a certainty, held in the body, that this was coming and it was bad.

Bond whined. The image shredded. Another came, briefer — the building's entrance from ground level, people streaming out through the columned portico, and a concussive vibration Bond couldn't hear but felt as pressure, as disturbance, as the air being wrong.

Then it collapsed. Bond blinked twice, confused by his own alertness, and his head dropped back down to the couch arm. His tail gave one uncertain wag. He sighed.

The whole episode had lasted maybe eight seconds.

"Just a dream," Loid said, resettling in his chair. He was watching Bond with the casual attentiveness he gave to everything in his vicinity — the attention that looked like ease and was surveillance. *Precognitive episode. He hasn't had one that intense in months. Usually the signals are vague — shapes, feelings. This looked specific. His eyes were tracking something. What did he see?*

Anya was gripping the edge of the couch cushion. The images were already losing resolution in her memory the way Bond's visions always did — the specific details softening into impressions, the architectural features blurring. But she'd seen enough to hold the shape of it. A government building with a columned stone facade. A formal terrace with iron railings and carved balustrades. Men in state security dress. Fire that was not an accident. An attack on a government venue, clear enough to be actionable, and Bond's visions, when they arrived this sharp, were not wrong.

On the television, the trade negotiation coverage had shifted to an anchor summarizing upcoming diplomatic events. Anya watched the ticker scroll. She couldn't read it from the couch.

She could tell Loid. She could say: Bond just showed me a precognitive vision of a government building being attacked, with specific architectural details — a columned entrance, a terrace where fire was set deliberately — and men in SSS formal dress running.

She could say all of that, if she wanted to explain how she'd read a dog's mind. If she wanted to watch her father's face change as he absorbed the fact that his daughter had been a telepath since she was four years old and had sat at this table a thousand times and never said a word.

She could also say nothing. Hope Bond was wrong for the first time. Go back to the bread and the stew and pretend the evening was still intact.

She did neither.

"Papa." The casualness in her voice was something she'd learned from him — the flat register that delivered information without signaling urgency, the spy's trick of making an important thing sound incidental. "I heard something at work. Probably nothing, but — there's been some chatter about security concerns at government venues lately. Some kind of credible threat assessment floating around. Have you heard anything?"

Loid looked at her over the top of his newspaper.

For one second she was his daughter asking a normal question. Then something behind his eyes recalibrated and she felt the shift in his thoughts like a temperature change.

*Interesting. She led with a disclaimer — 'probably nothing' — which is a softener an amateur uses to manage the listener's reaction. She wouldn't need to manage me unless the information matters more than she's presenting. 'Chatter at work' — from which section? She didn't say. 'Credible threat assessment floating around' — that's specific language for something she claims not to have paid attention to. Either this is lazy sourcing or she's being deliberately vague about where she actually learned it.*

He set the newspaper down. "I haven't heard anything specific. Which venues are we talking about?"

"I don't know the details. Just a general thing about security posture at government events. Heightened levels or — I don't know. I wasn't really listening at the time." She was adding words now, each one a small mistake, and she could hear herself doing it — the overcorrection of someone who'd underplayed the setup and was trying to recover by being more casual, which only made the initial delivery more conspicuous. She picked up her spoon.

*She's not lying about the threat itself — she wouldn't fabricate a security concern to fill conversation. But the source. The source isn't work chatter. She learned this somewhere else, or in a way she can't account for. Her delivery shifted when I asked which venues. She doesn't want to be more specific because specificity would reveal the origin. File this.*

"I'll mention it to a few people," Loid said. "See if anything matches."

He let it go. He picked up his newspaper and turned a page and his posture eased back into the shape of a man reading the evening edition. But Anya caught the thought he had as he settled, clean and filed away like a document placed in a drawer he knew he'd open again later: *Something is off about her sourcing and she knows I caught it. Revisit.*

He would not forget. Loid Forger — Twilight — did not forget things he'd flagged, and he did not lose track of discrepancies, and the specific discrepancy he'd just identified was his daughter's credibility on one very small point, and the erosion of trust happened in exactly this way: not a collapse but a sequence of small wrong notes that accumulated until the melody was unrecognizable.

Anya ate a spoonful of stew. It had gone cold and it tasted like nothing.

Yor was telling a story about a colleague at city hall whose birthday cake had been delivered with the wrong name — "They spelled it 'Hendrick' and his name is 'Heinrich' and he got so upset he took the cake home before anyone could have a piece, which I thought was, um, a strong reaction? But also I understand, because your name is important." She was laughing as she told it, and Loid was half-smiling behind his newspaper, and Bond was asleep again, dreaming about the yard behind the building where pigeons gathered in the afternoon, and the evening had the shape of ordinary warmth and the weight of everything Anya had just done wrong sitting inside it where nobody could see.

---

The phone rang at half past eight. Not the kitchen landline but the other one — the phone in the drawer by the hallway bookshelf, the one with the specific ringtone that Anya's family politely pretended was a normal household phone and never discussed.

"Excuse me," Anya said, and stepped into the hallway. Through the doorway she could still see the kitchen: Yor washing dishes, humming something off-key, the back of her head moving slightly with the tune. Loid was drying a pot. He did not look up as she left, but his thought reached her clearly: *That phone again. She gets calls on it more than she used to. The frequency has increased since her sector assignment changed.*

The line connected and Sylvia's voice was there — flat, unadorned, the voice of someone who had spent thirty years delivering information on secure lines and had long ago removed anything from her speech that did not serve the transmission.

"This is a routine follow-up on your assessment of the Ostanian operative in the diplomatic corps sector."

"Sure." Anya leaned against the hallway wall. She could hear water running in the kitchen. Yor saying something to Loid about the dish rack being full again.

"Another agency — I will not specify which — has flagged activity in the same sector you assessed in your initial report. They are identifying communication and movement patterns consistent with a coordinated operation. Their analysis does not match your conclusions."

The stew, which had already been sitting uneasily, felt like it was reconsidering its relationship with her stomach entirely.

"What kind of patterns?"

"Movement intercepts, financial flows, communication timing. Their analytical team is reading the sector as high-activity and potentially high-threat. Your assessment characterized it as mid-level factional maneuvering with limited strategic significance."

"That was my read," Anya said. Through the doorway she watched her mother hold a plate up to the light, checking for spots, turning it carefully. The thoroughness Yor gave to a white dinner plate.

"I am not questioning your read." The absence of accusation in Sylvia's voice was its own pressure — a negative space shaped like doubt, bordered by professionalism. "I am informing you that the analytical picture is developing in a direction that your assessment did not anticipate. If your evaluation has evolved since filing, now is the appropriate time to update it."

"It hasn't changed. The sector is factional noise. Internal Ostanian positioning. It looks coordinated because factions coordinate — that's what they do."

Silence. Anya could picture Sylvia at her desk: reading glasses on the chain, coffee long cold, the fluorescent overheads of WISE station casting their flat institutional light over everything. She couldn't read thoughts through a phone line. Telepathy needed proximity, needed bodies in the same room, and the absence of access made Sylvia's pauses enormous — empty spaces Anya usually filled with stolen information and now could only sit inside.

"Noted." The word was precise and carried no particular warmth or chill. "I will include your reaffirmation in the file. If your assessment changes, contact me directly. Not through the regular reporting chain."

"Understood."

"How is your family?"

The question arrived without transition, the way Sylvia asked personal questions — as if they were simply the next item on the same operational checklist.

"They're good. My mom made stew."

"Edible?"

"Interesting."

Something that might have been amusement, or might have been Sylvia exhaling. "Good night, Anya."

The line went dead.

Anya stood in the hallway with the phone in her hand. Another agency had flagged the sector she'd deliberately downgraded. The analytical machinery of allied intelligence was doing what it was built to do — cross-referencing, comparing, catching the places where one analyst's picture didn't match another's. Her lie was inside that machinery now, a misaligned gear, and the system was starting to grind around it. Sylvia wasn't suspicious yet. Her tone had been procedural, not interrogative. But the gap between Anya's assessment and the other agency's findings would produce questions, and questions would generate reviews, and reviews would open the original report, and the original report contained a lie that Anya had told while reading the trust in Sylvia's thoughts and knowing exactly how much that trust was worth.

She put the phone back in the drawer and returned to the kitchen. Yor had finished the dishes and was wiping down the counter. Loid was reading again, or performing reading — which was, for him, functionally the same thing.

"Everything okay?" he asked without looking up.

"Fine. Work stuff."

*Work stuff. On that particular phone. At eight-thirty on a family visit. She's carrying something. Multiple things — I can see it in her posture, the way she's standing in the doorway instead of sitting down. I did the same thing at her age. I was older before it started eating me.*

"I'm going to lie down for a bit," Anya said. "Long week."

"Of course." Yor appeared, as Yor always appeared, with a thing she hadn't been asked for — a cup of tea, steaming, placed in Anya's hands with the gentle certainty of someone whose love expressed itself in warm drinks and unanswerable kindness. "Chamomile. For sleeping. If you want it. You don't have to drink it. I just thought—"

"Thanks, Mama."

She took the cup and walked down the hall to her old bedroom.

The room was smaller than she remembered, which meant she was bigger, which meant time had passed in the specific way it does when you notice a childhood space can no longer contain you. The bed was the same narrow frame from when she was six, pushed against the wall with a headboard she'd once covered in glow-in-the-dark stars. Most had fallen off over the years. Two remained, clinging to the wood grain near the top, and in the dark they still glowed their faint greenish light — a fact she verified every time she slept here, the way you check on a thing you love to make sure it's still there. Her desk was against the window. The drawers held pencil stubs, a protractor, a notebook from her last year at Eden Academy with notes in handwriting that looked like a stranger's because it was a child's.

Her headband was on the shelf. The old one — black, with the horn-shaped decorations she'd worn every day until she was fourteen and decided they were too much. She picked it up. The plastic was scuffed and slightly bent from years of daily use. She put it on. It sat differently now — not tighter, exactly, but positioned on a head that had done things the girl who'd worn it couldn't have imagined.

She sat on the bed with her tea going cold on the desk and the headband pressing against her temples and the weight of the evening settling over her like a second blanket.

Three secrets. She counted them the way you count bruises after a fall — touching each one to find the edges.

The first: her family. She had been reading her father's thoughts and her mother's thoughts and her dog's visions since before she could write her own name, and none of them had ever been asked. Tonight she had caught her father's worry about her safety, her mother's buried doubt about the Garden's honesty, and her dog's premonition of violence at a government building, and she had carried all three pieces of intelligence out of their private minds and into her own, where they sat alongside everything else she'd taken without permission over the course of twenty years.

The second: Damian. She had lied to WISE about him. Twice now — once in the debriefing room with Sylvia, once on the phone ten minutes ago. Another agency's analysis was pressing against her false assessment, and the institutional machinery was starting to notice the discrepancy, and Sylvia still trusted her, which was the specific part that made Anya's stomach clench around the stew she wished she hadn't eaten.

The third: the Garden. She knew something about her mother's employer that her mother herself was barely ready to think — that the targets might not be clean, that the briefings might not be truthful, that twenty years of violence might not have served what Yor needed it to serve. And she could not share this with anyone. Not Loid, who would act on it. Not Yor, who wasn't ready. Not WISE, who would want to know how she'd obtained intelligence about a covert assassination organization. The knowledge sat in her like something swallowed wrong — too big to digest, impossible to bring back up.

Through the wall, she heard her parents' voices. Low, murmured, the frequency of a conversation between people who had been together long enough that half the words were implied and the other half were warmth. She couldn't make out specifics at this distance. She didn't reach for them. She had taken enough from this apartment tonight.

She pulled off the headband and set it back on the shelf, next to a small ceramic cat she'd won at a school fair when she was eight. Her tea had gone lukewarm and bitter from oversteeping. She drank it anyway. It tasted like her mother's care and her own neglect, which felt like the right combination for the evening.

From the living room, faintly, Bond's dreaming thoughts reached her through the walls. Not the vision this time — just the ordinary drift of an old dog in a warm room. *Soft. Floor. Girl in the back room. Good. Sleep.*

The girl in the back room set down her tea and pulled the covers to her chest and lay looking at the two remaining glow-in-the-dark stars on the headboard. They glowed. They had always glowed. She had been looking at them since before she could read minds, which was as close to before as she could remember, and they still glowed, and she did not sleep for a long time.


# Chapter 7

The conference room on the third floor of the National Unity Party headquarters smelled like old carpet and fresh coffee, and Damian Desmond was lying.

Not badly. He'd been lying in rooms like this since he was fifteen, since his father first brought him to a donors' reception and said, "Smile at the man in the grey suit, he funds our eastern district campaigns." Donovan Desmond hadn't needed to explain what the smile should look like. Damian had understood already. You learned to lie early in this family, the way other children learned to ride bicycles.

"The Westalian delegation's posture at the trade summit was instructive," Damian said, standing at the front of a table of eight party loyalists. He kept his hands at his sides, resisting the pocket. "Their willingness to revisit border tariffs suggests they're testing our resolve. If we concede on agricultural inspection protocols, we signal flexibility. If we're perceived as flexible, we're perceived as weak."

Nods around the table. Deputy Chairman Holtz, who had known Donovan for thirty years and considered himself a kingmaker, was writing something in a leather notebook. Beside him, Petra Lang from the defense policy committee watched Damian with the particular alertness of someone waiting for him to be useful or to fail.

"I'd recommend we draft a counter-position paper before the next session," Damian continued. "Something that reframes the tariff question as a security concern rather than an economic one. The language matters."

"Your father would have just told them no," Holtz said, not looking up from his notebook.

"My father had leverage I don't have yet," Damian said. He kept his voice warm, a little rueful. The trick was to acknowledge the shadow without seeming diminished by it. "But the principle is the same. You don't negotiate from a posture of accommodation."

Holtz looked up. "You sound like him."

Damian accepted the compliment the way it was intended — as a leash, gently applied. "I had a good teacher."

None of it was what he believed. The tariff question was routine economic maneuvering, and framing it as a security concern was the kind of escalatory rhetoric that the Renewal Front depended on. Every time a trade negotiation became a sovereignty dispute, the people who wanted war got to claim the people who wanted peace were surrendering something. Damian had drafted that counter-position paper already, in his head, and it disgusted him. He would write it this afternoon and it would be good — precise, persuasive, exactly the kind of thing a hawkish young Desmond would produce — and every sentence would be a small betrayal of the thing he was actually trying to do.

The meeting went another forty minutes. Budget allocations for the eastern district campaigns. A proposed media response to the Westalian cultural exchange program, which Lang wanted to frame as soft-power infiltration. Damian contributed where expected, deflected where he could, and watched the room the way his father had taught him: not for what people said but for who looked at whom when someone else was talking. Holtz deferred to Lang on media strategy but resented her for it. Lang wanted a committee chairmanship and was building alliances Holtz hadn't noticed yet. A younger member named Fitz, who Damian had met twice, kept checking his watch — bored or anxious, hard to tell.

None of them were Renewal Front, as far as Damian could determine. They were something more ordinary and in some ways more durable: people who had organized their entire professional lives around a set of assumptions about Ostanian strength and Westalian threat, and who would lose status, funding, and purpose if those assumptions were dismantled. The Front didn't need to recruit people like Holtz and Lang. It just needed the atmosphere they maintained.

Damian smiled at Fitz on the way out. "Good meeting," he said. Fitz looked relieved that someone had spoken to him.

---

Demetrius had chosen the restaurant, which meant the Kaiserhof, which meant the table by the window overlooking the ministry gardens, which meant this was not a casual lunch. Demetrius did not do casual. He did appropriate, calculated, and precisely legible to anyone in the room who might be watching. Every meal with his brother was a performance for an audience Damian couldn't always identify.

He was already seated when Damian arrived, ten minutes early because Demetrius was always fifteen minutes early and would note the gap. His older brother wore a dark suit that fit like it had been born on his body. His hair was combed in the same style their father had worn in photographs from the ministry — parted severely, held in place by some force stronger than product, possibly will. At twenty-four, Demetrius looked forty. He had looked forty since he was eighteen. The resemblance to Donovan was the kind of thing people commented on in his presence as though it were a compliment, and Demetrius accepted it as one, because for him it was.

"You're early," Demetrius said.

"You're earlier."

"I had a call that ran short. Kessler at Vanguard Defense. They're restructuring their procurement division." He said this the way other people would mention a dentist appointment — incidentally, but with the expectation that the information was being filed. "Sit down."

Damian sat. The waiter brought menus. The Kaiserhof served the kind of Ostanian cuisine that existed to prove Ostanian cuisine could be expensive: thick soups elevated to consommes, sausages deconstructed into terrines, bread that arrived with its own explanation. Damian ordered the schnitzel because it was the simplest thing on the menu, which was also a kind of statement, though he wished it weren't.

"I heard the party meeting went well," Demetrius said, scanning the menu without appearing to read it. He would order the duck. He always ordered the duck.

"Who told you that?"

"Holtz. He called to say you sounded like Father." Demetrius set the menu down. "He says that every time you speak. I think he means it as both praise and instruction."

"I know what he means by it."

"Do you." The question was flat, not inflected upward. Demetrius looked at him across the white tablecloth with the expression that had been his default since they were children: appraisal, faintly impatient. "The tariff counter-position. You'll draft it?"

"This afternoon."

"Good. Kessler mentioned that the defense ministry is watching the trade negotiations closely. A strong Ostanian position helps their budget projections."

"Kessler at Vanguard Defense is watching trade negotiations."

"Kessler's projections align with the ministry's. Defense procurement is downstream of trade policy, Damian. This isn't complicated."

It wasn't complicated. That was the problem. The flow was so clean it looked like gravity: trade tensions justified defense spending, defense spending funded procurement contracts, procurement contracts enriched firms like Vanguard, and Vanguard's profits funded the political infrastructure that ensured trade tensions remained tense. Demetrius didn't engineer this system. He just knew where the pipes ran and made sure they didn't leak.

"How's Father?" Damian asked, because that was a thing he could ask, and because it was simpler than anything else in this conversation.

"The same. He reads the papers. He has opinions about the eastern district that I've stopped relaying because they're twenty years out of date." Demetrius paused. "He asked about you."

"What did you tell him?"

"That you're performing well. That the party leadership is pleased with your engagement."

"Are they?"

"They're satisfied. You attend, you contribute, you produce competent policy work, and you carry the name. Satisfaction is the appropriate response." He picked up his water glass. "But Holtz mentioned that your engagement has been... selective. You attend the meetings that require your presence and decline the ones that would benefit from it. The defense policy working group. The security liaison committee. Petra Lang noticed."

"I've been focused on the trade portfolio."

"The trade portfolio is a holding position and you know it. The real work in this party — the work that justifies the Desmond name's weight — is in defense and security. That's where the relationships are. That's where the money moves."

The waiter arrived. Demetrius ordered the duck. Damian's schnitzel came with a microgreen garnish that looked apologetic about being on the plate.

"I'll attend the next defense working group," Damian said.

"Attend all of them." Demetrius cut his duck with the precision of someone who considered carving a social skill. "You are either inside the room where the decisions are made or you are irrelevant to the decisions. There is no middle position."

"That sounds like Father."

"It sounds like common sense. Father just happened to articulate it well." He chewed, swallowed, set down his knife and fork at the precise angle that indicated a pause, not completion. "Damian. I understand that you have... reservations about the direction of party policy. You've always been the thoughtful one. Father said that about you once — 'Damian thinks before he speaks, which is admirable until it becomes hesitation.' I manage the infrastructure. You provide the vision. But the vision must be legible, and lately yours has been difficult to read."

"I'm being careful."

"Careful is fine. Ambiguous is not. The people who fund this party and the people who benefit from its positions need to know which side of the table you're sitting on. Kessler asked me, directly, whether your commitment to national defense is genuine. I told him yes. Don't make me a liar."

Damian looked at his brother — the sharp jaw, the controlled posture, the suit that cost more than a schoolteacher's monthly salary. Demetrius wasn't cruel. He wasn't even cynical, exactly. He was something harder to argue with: practical. He saw the Desmond political machine as infrastructure that required maintenance, not ideology that required belief. The machine produced outcomes. The outcomes benefited people who had organized their lives around those outcomes. Disrupting the machine wasn't justice; it was vandalism. Demetrius genuinely believed this. Not because he wanted the world to be this way, but because this was the world as he found it, and he considered it childish to wish for a different one.

"I'm not going to embarrass the family," Damian said.

Demetrius studied him for a moment that lasted slightly too long. "No," he said. "I don't think you will." He returned to the duck. The statement had the shape of confidence and the weight of a warning.

They talked about smaller things. A cousin's wedding. The state of the ministry gardens, which Demetrius apparently had opinions about. Their father's deteriorating opinion of modern Ostanian literature. Damian listened to his brother's voice — clipped, sure, shaped by the same household that had shaped his own — and tried to identify the moment when Demetrius had decided that maintaining the system was the same as believing in it. He couldn't find it. It was possible there hadn't been a moment. It was possible Demetrius had simply never experienced the gap.

They split the bill because Demetrius insisted on splitting bills, a gesture toward equality that was also a reminder that equality was a gesture. On the pavement outside the Kaiserhof, autumn air sharp against the warmth of the dining room, Demetrius shook his hand — formal, as if they hadn't shared a womb's worth of childhood — and said, "The defense working group meets Thursday. I'll let Lang know you're coming."

"Fine."

"And call Father. He responds better to your voice than mine."

This was the closest Demetrius came to vulnerability: acknowledging, sideways, that their father preferred the son who questioned to the son who complied. Damian had never been sure whether Demetrius resented this or simply logged it as another data point about infrastructure maintenance.

"I'll call him tonight," Damian said.

Demetrius nodded and walked toward a car that was already waiting, its engine running, its driver opening the door before Demetrius reached it. He moved through the world like the world had been briefed on his schedule. Damian stood on the pavement in his simpler suit and watched him go and thought about the word *infrastructure* and what it meant when the thing being maintained was the architecture of a conflict that killed people.

---

Grosz lived in a third-floor apartment in the Schiller district, east of the government quarter but far enough to be affordable — the kind of building where retired civil servants went to grow quiet. The stairwell smelled like boiled vegetables and floor wax. A cat watched Damian from the second-floor landing with the unblinking contempt of an animal that had seen better tenants.

He knocked twice, then once, then twice again — the old pattern from his father's network, unchanged in thirty years because the people who used it were too old to learn a new one. The door opened on a chain. One eye, watery and sharp, examined him through the gap.

"Herr Grosz. It's Damian."

"I can see who it is." The chain rattled. The door opened. "You look like your mother. Come in."

The apartment was small and meticulously kept. A sitting room with a settee and two armchairs arranged around a coffee table that held a folded newspaper, a magnifying glass, and a plate with crumbs that suggested toast, recently eaten. Bookshelves lined one wall — Ostanian political history, military memoirs, a surprising number of novels. On the mantle, framed photographs: Grosz as a younger man in a ministerial corridor, Grosz shaking hands with someone whose face Damian didn't recognize, and, in the center, Grosz standing behind Donovan Desmond at what appeared to be a state function, both of them wearing the particular expression of men who believed they were building something permanent.

Grosz himself was seventy-three, thin in the way of men who had been vigorous and were now just wiry, with liver-spotted hands and a collar buttoned to the neck despite the warmth of the apartment. He moved carefully but not slowly — the distinction mattered to him, Damian suspected. A cane leaned against the armchair but Grosz did not use it while Damian was watching.

"Sit. I'll make coffee. Or I have schnapps, if you prefer."

"Coffee, please."

"Your father would have taken the schnapps."

"My father could hold it better than I can."

Grosz almost smiled. "He couldn't. He just never let on." He disappeared into the kitchen. Water ran. A kettle clicked. Damian sat in the armchair that didn't have the cane next to it and waited.

The apartment was very still. Traffic noise filtered up from the street — a bus, someone's car horn — but inside, the quiet was the kind that accumulated in rooms where one person lived alone. Damian recognized it. His own apartment had the same quality, though he was fifty years younger and would have denied the comparison.

Grosz returned with two cups on saucers, the porcelain heavy and old-fashioned, a pattern of blue flowers that had probably been a wedding set. He sat in the opposite armchair with the deliberate care of someone protecting his knees.

"You said you had questions about the current situation." He set his cup down without drinking. "I assumed you didn't mean the tariff negotiations."

"No."

"Then ask."

Damian had rehearsed this, but rehearsal was useless with Grosz. The old man had spent three decades managing Donovan Desmond's intelligence network — not the public face of it, not the policy arm, but the operational infrastructure that gathered information, assessed threats, and occasionally acted on both. Grosz had seen every technique for extracting information that a politician's son might attempt, because he'd taught several of them to the politician himself.

"The Renewal Front," Damian said. "I've been hearing the name in channels that used to be reliable. I need to know what it is."

Grosz's expression didn't change. He picked up his coffee, took a sip, and set it back down with the saucer precisely aligned to the table's edge. "Where did you hear it?"

"That's not something I can share."

"I served your father for thirty years. I'm not asking out of curiosity."

"I know. But the people who used the name don't know I'm here, and I'd like to keep it that way."

Grosz studied him. The watery eyes were sharper than they looked — assessing, the way a retired doctor might assess a symptom he'd seen before. "You're careful. Good. Your father was careful too, until he wasn't."

"Tell me about the Front."

Grosz was quiet for a moment. The radiator ticked. Somewhere below, the cat yowled at something.

"It's not a party," he said finally. "Not a faction, not a caucus. It's a coalition. Military officers — mid-level, mostly, the ones who built their careers during the last escalation and saw their budgets cut when the peace held. Civilian nationalists from the old guard, people who consider the current detente a temporary humiliation. And a funding structure that runs through defense procurement — contracts, subcontracts, maintenance agreements, the whole apparatus of military spending that doesn't appear in political budgets because it's classified as operational infrastructure."

He said the word *infrastructure* and Damian thought of Demetrius and felt something cold settle in his chest.

"How large?" Damian asked.

"I can't give you numbers. But the funding is substantial. Defense contracts generate revenue that's difficult to trace because the contracts themselves are classified. A procurement firm inflates a maintenance estimate by fifteen percent. The excess is routed through an intermediary. The intermediary funds activities that are... off-book. Meetings, travel, communications, and the operational capacity to act when the moment comes. No one signs their name to anything. The money just moves."

"And the leadership?"

Grosz set his cup down again. His hands, Damian noticed, were not entirely steady. "The Front isn't led the way a party is led. There's no chairman, no public face. But there is a figure who organizes, who connects the military and civilian elements, who articulates the purpose." He paused. "Someone who lost enough to believe that peace is theft. That's the phrase he uses. Peace as theft. As if the absence of war took something from him that he's owed."

"You won't name him."

"I won't name him to you in this apartment, no. I don't know who might come through that door after you leave, and I'm an old man who lives alone." He looked at the photograph on the mantle — the one with Donovan. "Your father knew some of these people. Not the Front — it didn't exist in its current form during his time. But the network of military and civilian figures who believe Ostanian strength requires Westalian weakness. He worked with them when it suited him. He managed them when it didn't. The difference between your father and these people is that Donovan understood leverage as a tool. They've made it an ideology."

"My father's ideology was Ostanian dominance."

"Your father's ideology was his own authority. Ostanian dominance was the frame he used. The distinction matters, because a man who uses a frame can change it when circumstances require. A man who believes the frame is the point — that man can't stop. That man will build a provocation, a crisis, an incident, whatever it takes to make the world match the shape in his head." He paused. "That is what the Renewal Front is for. Not policy. Not governance. An incident."

The radiator ticked. Damian let the word sit in the room.

"There's something else," Grosz said. His voice dropped — not for drama, Damian thought, but because the walls were thin and this was the part that frightened him. "The funding channels aren't one-directional. I've seen — not recently, but when I was still active, before your father withdrew — I saw documentation that defense procurement firms with Westalian ownership were purchasing Ostanian military intelligence assessments. Not stealing them. *Purchasing* them, through intermediary firms that brokered the transaction."

Damian stared at him. "Westalian firms buying Ostanian military intelligence."

"Buying it. And both sides knew. The Ostanian sellers understood who was buying. The Westalian buyers understood what they were purchasing. The transaction served both: the Westalian defense establishment got intelligence that justified their own spending projections, and the Ostanian military got revenue that funded further capability, which justified further Westalian spending, which funded further purchases. A circuit."

"That's — " Damian stopped. He had been about to say *insane,* but it wasn't. It was the opposite. It was the sanest possible arrangement for people whose livelihoods depended on tension between two nations. A self-sustaining market in mutual threat. "How current is this?"

"The arrangements I knew about were from eight, ten years ago. But the infrastructure doesn't dismantle itself. If anything, the current political temperature would have made the transactions more valuable. Higher tension means higher defense budgets on both sides. Higher budgets mean more procurement. More procurement means more opportunities for the kind of brokering I described." He picked up his cup again, held it without drinking. "You assumed the Renewal Front was an Ostanian problem. A faction of nationalists pushing your country toward war. It is that. But it exists within an ecosystem that profits from the *possibility* of war, and that ecosystem has shareholders on both sides of the border. The Westalian peace establishment is real. But so is the Westalian defense establishment, and its interests are not identical."

Damian sat with this. The coffee was going cold. He didn't care. What Grosz was describing wasn't a conspiracy in the way Damian had been thinking — a plot, a group of men in a room, a plan with a timeline. It was a system. A system of aligned incentives that produced outcomes no single actor needed to orchestrate because the architecture produced them automatically. The Renewal Front was the visible edge of something structural, and the structure crossed the border he'd assumed divided the problem into two sides.

"Why are you telling me this?" Damian asked.

Grosz looked at him with something that was not quite affection and not quite pity. "Because you're Donovan's son. And because you're trying to dismantle something your father helped build, and you deserve to know what it actually is before you break yourself against it." He set the cup down. "I was loyal to your father. I'm loyal to his name. That means I'm loyal to you, even when — especially when — what you're doing puts the name at risk. But I am frightened, Damian. Not of the Front. Of what happens when the people who profit from this system realize that someone is trying to interrupt it."

"You think they'll come after me."

"I think they won't need to. They'll come after the alliances you've built. The people who trust you. The channels you've opened. They'll isolate you, and then the isolation will do the rest." He folded his hands in his lap — the liver-spotted fingers interlaced, a gesture that was both composed and protective, as if he were holding something fragile between his palms. "Your father was never alone. He had infrastructure. People, resources, leverage. You have a name and a counter-position paper and whatever allies you've found outside this room, which I will not ask about because I don't want to know."

"That's probably wise."

"It's certainly wise. I'm too old to be brave." He almost smiled again, and this time it reached his eyes, briefly, before settling back into the watchful stillness that was his default. "Be careful with the name, Damian. The Desmond name opens doors. It also tells everyone exactly where to find you."

---

The walk home took twelve minutes, east along the Friedrichstrasse and then north toward his building. The streetlights were coming on. Autumn was doing its slow, grey work on Berlint — leaves in the gutters, darkness arriving earlier each week, the kind of chill that got into your coat and sat against your ribs. Damian walked with his hands in his pockets and his collar turned up and thought about systems.

A system that profits from tension. Westalian firms purchasing Ostanian intelligence through intermediaries. Ostanian military budgets swelling on Westalian threat assessments. The Renewal Front funded through procurement contracts that nobody signed their name to. And somewhere in the middle of it, a man who lost enough to believe peace is theft, organizing a coalition of soldiers and nationalists toward an incident that would crack the détente and let the whole machine run at full capacity again.

Damian's apartment was on the fourth floor of a building that had been elegant fifty years ago and was now respectable — which was the appropriate register for a junior diplomat who happened to be Donovan Desmond's son. Modest enough to signal independence. Well-located enough to signal that the independence was a choice, not a circumstance.

He let himself in. Hung his coat. Put the kettle on out of habit and then turned it off because he'd had enough coffee and the alternative was schnapps and Grosz was right, he couldn't hold it. The apartment was quiet in the particular way that single people's apartments were quiet in the evening — not the quiet of peace but the quiet of absence, every surface waiting for a second voice that wasn't coming.

He should call his father. He should draft the counter-position paper. He should eat something. He should review the notes he'd made after the Grosz meeting, which were coded in a shorthand he'd developed himself — nothing as sophisticated as a cipher, just a personal notation that would look like shopping lists to anyone who found them.

He didn't do any of those things. Instead, he opened the closet in the hallway and pulled down the cardboard box from the top shelf. It was the only box he'd brought from the family house — everything else in this apartment had been purchased new, a deliberate severance from the curated aesthetics of the Desmond household. But this box had come with him. He'd never unpacked it. He'd never thrown it out, either.

Inside: school things. An Eden Academy report card with marks that were good but not exceptional, which had been a source of quiet disappointment his father never voiced and Damian never stopped hearing. A certificate for the inter-class spelling competition, second place. A ribbon from the athletics day — he'd run the four-hundred-metre, badly, and finished fourth, and the ribbon said PARTICIPANT in silver text, which was the institutional word for failure. A folded programme from the school festival, his name listed as part of the cleanup crew for the gymnasium. A pen that had run out of ink years ago. And, at the bottom, beneath a layer of tissue paper that someone — his mother, probably, before she got sick — had placed there to protect it, a photograph.

Class photograph. Eden Academy, third year. Four rows of students on the gymnasium steps, in uniform, faces arranged in the specific configuration of children told to hold still and smile simultaneously. The teacher, Frau Henderson, stood to the left, her posture communicating both warmth and the absolute certainty that someone was about to misbehave.

Damian found himself in the third row, second from the right. He looked furious about something. He usually had. At ten, his face had been a rotating exhibition of indignation, wounded pride, and the stubborn refusal to admit he wanted anyone's approval while desperately wanting everyone's.

He moved his gaze to the second row. He didn't decide to look there. His eyes just went.

She was standing between a boy Damian didn't remember and Becky Blackbell, who he did. Pink hair — bright, ridiculous, unmistakable even in a fading photograph. She was grinning at something off-camera, not at the photographer, which meant she'd been told to look forward and hadn't. Her uniform was slightly crooked. Her eyes were screwed up with whatever had made her laugh. She looked like trouble in the specific way that certain children look like trouble — not malicious but incapable of leaving the world as they found it.

Anya Forger. The girl who'd punched him during a dodgeball game in year one because he'd said something about her hair and she'd decided the appropriate response was violence. The girl who'd ended up on his cleanup crew at the school festival and spent the entire afternoon arguing about the correct way to stack chairs. The girl who'd been the first person at Eden Academy to treat him like a person instead of a surname, which at the time had enraged him because he'd built his entire identity around being a surname and she'd refused to notice.

He thought about the cultural attache he'd met at a state dinner. The woman with the quick smile and the laugh that caught him somewhere he hadn't expected. The way she'd tilted her head when she was listening — not politely but with a kind of total attention that made him feel like the only frequency in a crowded room.

He looked at the photograph. He looked at the girl in the second row.

He held the photograph for a long time. Longer than he meant to. The kettle was cold. The apartment was dark — he hadn't turned on the overhead light and the only illumination came from the streetlight outside the window, casting the room in the amber half-tones of a Berlint evening. The girl in the photograph grinned at whatever had been funny sixteen years ago, and Damian sat on the floor of his hallway with a box of childhood things and stared at a face he was only beginning to let himself recognize.

He put the photograph back in the box. He didn't put the box back on the shelf. He left it on the floor, open, and went to the kitchen to draft a counter-position paper about tariffs that he didn't believe in, for a party that was part of the machine he was trying to dismantle, carrying a name that opened every door and told everyone exactly where to find him.

The apartment was quiet. Outside, Berlint settled into its autumn dark, and somewhere in the system Grosz had described — the circuit of money and threat and mutual profit — the gears turned without anyone needing to touch them.


# Chapter 8

The noise started in the elevator.

Not the audible kind — the Concordia Hotel's elevators were smooth and quiet, the kind of quiet that expensive places maintained to justify the room rates. The man standing next to Anya in the elevator car was silent too, his hands folded over a leather portfolio, his expression set to the pleasant neutral that everyone in the diplomatic quarter wore after hours. But his thoughts were loud.

*— definitely left the gas on. I turned the knob before I left but did I check? I didn't check. If the building catches fire they'll find my notes about the Ackermann meeting on the kitchen counter and they'll think it's a suicide note. That's — no. I turned the knob. I'm almost sure I turned the knob.*

Anya adjusted her earring and watched the floor numbers climb. She'd been reading other people's thoughts since she was four years old. It never got less strange, but it had gotten quieter in certain ways — the surprise was gone, replaced by a low-grade fatigue she'd learned to manage the way some people managed bad knees. It was always there. She could work around it. Most days she barely noticed.

Tonight was not going to be one of those days.

The elevator opened onto the third-floor lobby of the Concordia, where the Westalian trade delegation's autumn reception was already underway behind a set of double doors. She could hear the string quartet and the glassware through the wood. She could hear everything else through the walls.

*— if he brings up the grain tariff one more time I swear I'll —*
*— nice shoes, who is she, haven't seen her at —*
*— the shrimp is late, they promised shrimp by seven and it's seven-fifteen and I'm not —*
*— didn't tell Maria I'd be out tonight, she's going to assume the worst, she always —*

Two hundred minds behind a set of doors. All going at once, all at different volumes, overlapping like radio stations bleeding into each other when you drove between cities. The gasman was right behind her, still agonizing over the knob. Anya rubbed the spot below her right ear — an old gesture, childhood, she was supposed to have stopped doing that years ago — and pushed through the doors.

The ballroom opened warm and bright, all crystal and amber light, both delegations already circulating. Westalian trade officials clustered near the bar with the loose posture of people who'd staked their territory early. Ostanian diplomatic staff had colonized the appetizer stations with the quiet efficiency of a border maneuver. A string quartet was playing something tasteful and completely ignored in the far corner. The Concordia's ballroom had two exits — one at each end — which appealed to intelligence professionals, and a well-stocked bar, which appealed to everyone else.

Anya picked up a glass of white wine she wouldn't drink and started moving.

Within the first twenty minutes she caught:

A Westalian trade minister near the terrace doors, mentally rehearsing an explanation for his wife about why he'd be home late. *— traffic, obviously, and then Kessler insisted on reviewing the export figures, these receptions always run long, if I leave at ten I can say ten-thirty because the taxi queue, and then —* The explanation was too detailed and too carefully constructed. He was building scaffolding around a lie, and the lie had nothing to do with export figures. None of her business. She kept moving.

An Ostanian general at the buffet — the one whose medals ran from his shoulder to his collar in a decorative fence — thinking about shrimp with an analytical rigor that would have been impressive in a briefing room. *— the tail came off clean. That's the mark of quality. If you have to twist, the preparation was rushed. This kitchen knows what it's doing. The cocktail sauce is adequate. Not exceptional. The horseradish ratio is off — too much lemon, not enough bite —* He selected four shrimp from the platter with the deliberation of a man making a strategic decision, held them in a brief visual inspection, and placed them on his plate in a row. His thoughts about the shrimp were more analytically rigorous than most of what Anya heard at WISE briefings.

A young Ostanian diplomat by the window, holding a champagne flute with both hands and composing a text message in her head, word by word, revising as she went. *— I think we should take a break. No. I think we need to talk about where this is going. No, that's worse. I think I need some space to figure out what I want. But I know what I want. I want to stop feeling like this every Sunday. Okay: maybe we should take some — god. I'll figure it out later.* She hadn't sipped her champagne. Her thumbnail was pressed white against the flute's stem.

A Westalian aide near the service entrance, holding a clipboard and thinking with the controlled despair of someone whose boss was about to give a speech. *— he's going to mispronounce Ackermann's title again, he did it at the Ostenheim reception and nobody corrected him, I wrote it phonetically on the note card but he won't look at the card, he never looks at the card, why do I make the cards —* The aide's jacket didn't fit. He kept tugging the sleeves down and the sleeves kept riding up, and his thoughts ran underneath the speech anxiety to a quiet, persistent worry about his mother's health that he'd been carrying all evening like a stone in his shoe.

An older Ostanian woman — some kind of deputy minister, based on the deference people showed when she passed — thinking about whether her daughter had remembered to feed the cat. *— she forgets the wet food. The dry is in the automatic feeder but the vet said he needs wet food twice a day for his kidneys, and she's twenty-eight years old and she can't remember to feed a cat —* The deputy minister was also, in a secondary track, reviewing talking points about natural gas imports. The cat took priority.

These people ran the foreign policy apparatus of two nations. Their inner lives were grocery lists and affair logistics and social anxiety and petty resentments and, occasionally, in the gaps between all of that, thoughts about actual governance. This was what telepathy in a crowded room felt like: standing in a river of other people's weather, most of it unremarkable, all of it uninvited, scanning for the handful of operationally useful fragments that justified the headache building behind her eyes.

She was here to work. The reception was an intelligence target — any gathering where both delegations occupied the same room offered proximity scanning that no surveillance equipment could replicate and no other operative in the world could perform. WISE didn't know that, of course. WISE thought she was here to observe, make contacts, and file a report on the diplomatic temperature. Which she would do. She'd translate her stolen intelligence into phrases like *my assessment* and *body language analysis suggests* and *observed behavioral indicators*, because that was how you cited a source that was someone else's private thoughts.

Two years of this. Two years of cheating and calling it fieldwork.

She drifted toward the east end of the ballroom and caught sight of Yuri Briar.

He was in a dark suit — SSS security detail for the evening, positioned where he could cover the main entrance, the corridor to the service stairwell, and most of the room. His earpiece was nearly invisible. His posture was the particular kind of alert stillness that people who'd never stood a security post would mistake for relaxation: weight balanced, hands free, eyes tracking the crowd in a steady sweep. Anya counted the interval. Every eight seconds.

She read him from across the room, his signal cutting through the noise with the clarity of a voice she knew.

*— west entrance covered. Kraus has the kitchen corridor. The Westalian delegation brought three extra staff that weren't on the advance sheet — logged it, but I don't like it. Three is an operational number. Three means someone planned for contingency.*

Sharp. He'd always been sharp. She remembered him at the Forger apartment when he was twenty, furious and adoring and so protective of Yor that he'd inspected Loid's cooking as if looking for poison in the miso soup. He'd mellowed about the cooking. A little.

*— need to call Sis. Told her dinner this week. Thursday or Friday. Can't remember which one I said. If it was Thursday I've already missed it and she'll have cooked anyway, she always makes too much food, and it'll be —* A flash of affection so bright it cut through the crowd noise like sunlight through a window. *— terrible. It'll taste terrible. I'll eat all of it and tell her it was great. That turmeric phase is not helping.*

Anya looked away before her expression could betray her. Yuri's love for his sister was so simple and so fierce that catching it always felt like stepping on something delicate. She shouldn't have been reading it. She was always reading it.

Then his attention shifted, and the warmth in his thoughts hardened into something professional.

*— there she is. The cultural attaché. Forger — Sis's family. Third function this month. Cultural attachés don't work this circuit. They cover arts events, memorial lectures, the gallery openings. They don't show up at trade receptions and mixed-delegation briefings unless they're doing something beyond culture.*

A beat. His gaze tracked across the room, and Anya realized with a cold little lurch that he was looking directly at her.

*— Sis would say I'm overthinking. But three events, three different kinds. A cultural attaché doesn't circulate like that. Not unless she's got a reason nobody filed with my office.*

Anya turned smoothly toward the nearest conversation cluster and inserted herself into a discussion about bilateral arts funding that three Westalian attachés were conducting with the enthusiasm of people fulfilling a professional obligation. She was careful not to look at Yuri. She was careful to look like someone who had strong opinions about ceramics residency grants.

"The Ostenheim program is actually oversubscribed this year," one of the attachés said, and Anya nodded like this was the most interesting thing she'd heard all evening.

She asked an intelligent question about studio access and extracted herself after four minutes. She moved toward the bar, where a cluster of Westalian staff had gathered, and passed within range of Strauss.

Strauss worked the economic intelligence desk at WISE. He was nursing a gin and tonic, watching the room with half-attention, thinking in the slightly cynical register of someone who'd been doing analytical work long enough to see the framework's seams. Anya didn't seek him out deliberately — his thoughts were just there, the way all thoughts were, crowding in uninvited.

*— the current Ostanian defense posture is consistent with escalation, but if we're honest — and we're never honest about this — the threat assessment we filed last quarter was shaped. Halverson shaped it. I helped. We didn't invent the threat. We selected the data that made the budget request look urgent and left out the numbers that complicated it. If the tension drops, WISE's operating budget drops. Everybody at the desk knows this. Nobody says it because saying it out loud makes us the same kind of people we're supposed to be watching.*

Anya reached past him for a glass of sparkling water and said nothing. She took the water and drifted away and let his thought sit inside her like a stone she'd swallowed.

Both sides. She'd suspected it — you didn't work in intelligence without developing a sense for where institutional incentive bent the truth. But suspecting was abstract, a thing you could live alongside without looking at directly. Reading it from a colleague's unguarded thoughts was concrete. Strauss, with his gin and tonic and his decent shoes and his two kids in the Berlint school district, shaping threat assessments to justify his agency's budget. Not lying, exactly. Selecting. Framing. The same thing diplomats did, the same thing she did every time she filed a report crediting "behavioral indicators" instead of "I heard his thoughts." Everyone in this room was adjusting the picture for their own reasons, and the picture they produced — the geopolitical landscape that actual people would live and die inside — was the sum of all those adjustments, not the truth underneath.

She'd been naive to believe WISE was different from the machinery on the other side. She'd been naive for exactly as long as it had taken to read one honest thought from a man who didn't know she was listening.

Anya finished her water and kept moving.

* * *

She found Damian near the terrace doors, talking to a woman from the Ostanian Foreign Affairs Ministry about port logistics. He was listening with the genuine interest of someone who actually cared about container-handling regulations, which was both a diplomatic skill and, Anya suspected, a genuine condition. He cared about infrastructure. It was one of his more alarming qualities.

*— the tariff revision would solve the northern port bottleneck, but the ministry won't move on it because reducing friction at the border reduces the appearance of tension, and the appearance of tension is what keeps the defense allocation from —*

Even his thoughts ran through policy architecture. Anya waited for the port-logistics woman to excuse herself, which she did after two more sentences about dredging schedules.

"The shrimp's worth trying," Anya said, low enough to sound personal.

"Yeah?"

"A general out there has been analyzing it for forty minutes. His assessment is favorable."

Damian glanced toward the buffet. He was thinking about the general and also, underneath, about whether Anya had picked up anything useful from the Westalian side. His mind organized itself neatly — the social register on top, the operational calculations beneath, and below those, something warmer and less controlled that he was working hard to keep out of his active thoughts.

"Anything from the trade people?" he said. His voice was casual. His thoughts were not.

"Budget shaping. I'll explain later."

"Right." He straightened his cuffs — not nervous, deliberate, something to do with his hands while his interior reorganized. *— she does this. She walks through a room for twenty minutes and comes back with more actionable material than I get in a full evening. How does she — observation, sure, but it's more than that, there's something about the way she —* He stopped the thought, filed it somewhere she couldn't reach, and produced the diplomatic smile. "I'll take the south end. The defense liaison's here. He's been avoiding me, which means he's got something worth hearing."

"Don't antagonize him."

"I don't antagonize."

"You absolutely do."

"I create conditions for candid dialogue."

"Sure."

They separated. Two colleagues at a party, exchanging small talk about seafood — nobody watching would have caught anything. Anya moved back toward the bar and Damian crossed toward the south windows, and for a moment the room was just a room: people eating, drinking, rehearsing lies for their spouses, thinking about their shoes, performing the elaborate social labor of pretending to enjoy standing upright for three hours in formal clothing.

Through the terrace doors, the Concordia's garden was dark and cold, the last of the autumn light long gone. A few smokers stood on the terrace in their overcoats, their thoughts drifting outward into the night — a financial dispute, a half-written resignation letter, the particular melancholy of standing outside at a party where you knew almost everyone and felt close to almost no one. Anya caught these as background radiation and moved on. The headache was settling in behind her right eye now, the low, steady ache that came after an hour of sustained scanning. She'd learned to work through it. She'd never learned to like it.

She scanned the room in quadrants, the way Loid had taught her — not his teaching method but the spatial discipline she'd adapted from watching him work rooms, years ago, before she was old enough to understand what he was doing and long after she'd read it from his thoughts. Upper left, upper right, lower left, lower right. Read, assess, move. The pattern kept her from circling back to signals she'd already caught, and it kept her moving, which was important — a cultural attaché who stood still too long at a trade reception was a cultural attaché who looked like she was waiting for something.

Anya worked the next half hour with the steady patience of someone who'd been doing this since her first field assignment. She passed within range of six Ostanian officials, two defense attachés, a military aide whose thoughts were exclusively about whether he'd parked in a tow zone, and a very nervous junior diplomat who was thinking about whether his fly was down.

It was.

She couldn't tell him. She could not, under any circumstance, approach a stranger at a diplomatic reception and inform him that his fly was down based on intelligence she had obtained by reading his mind. She moved on. The junior diplomat continued to radiate anxiety about his zipper, and she had to actively steer around his signal because it was loud enough to interfere with her operational scanning, and the whole thing was a perfect miniature of her entire life: she knew the truth, she couldn't say it, and the person it concerned would never know she'd been there.

She caught fragments. The useful kind, the ones she'd compress into cover phrasing for her report:

*— Voss has been quiet this week. Too quiet. Werner doesn't go quiet unless something's ready to move, and if the timeline is —*

*— northern checkpoint reinforcements are ahead of schedule, told Ackermann the timeline holds, but if the budget review interferes with —*

*— we're not supposed to discuss the procurement contracts at functions, somebody tell Dietrich, if the Westalians overhear the contract numbers —*

Pieces. Threads. None of it formed a picture on its own, but each thought was a data point she'd cross-reference later against Damian's network. The work was patient and cumulative — intelligence as data entry, one stolen thought at a time — and the tedium was broken only by the occasional comic fragment that reminded her these powerful officials were also regular people with regular problems and regular digestive concerns.

Then Damian looked at her from across the room.

She was standing near the eastern bar, turning her water glass in her hand, when she felt his attention swing toward her. Not his diplomatic attention — the kind he pointed at everyone. Something older. Something that came from a different part of his memory. She read it as it happened:

*— there she is. By the bar. The way she holds things — close to her body, like she's protecting them. I've noticed that. When did I start noticing that?*

A hesitation. His thoughts rearranged.

*— she looks like someone. Not just familiar. She looks like — Eden Academy. The school festival. Second year. We got assigned to the same cleanup crew and she knocked over an entire bucket of soapy water on Henderson's shoes and when she laughed she —*

The memory was nearly formed. Anya could feel it surfacing through his thoughts like something rising from deep water: a girl with pink hair, younger, smaller, laughing with her whole body about the ruined shoes. The specific quality of afternoon light through Eden's courtyard trees. Her laugh. The way she tilted her head when she was about to say something terrible.

*— pink hair. She had pink hair. The attaché has pink hair. That's not — how many people have pink hair? The girl at Eden used to tilt her head exactly like — exactly like she's doing right now, she's doing it right now —*

He was one synapse away from the name. Anya could feel it sitting behind the image like a word on the tip of a tongue — *Forger* — not yet spoken in his thoughts, not yet locked to the face of the woman he'd been working with for weeks, but close. Three seconds. Maybe two.

Someone touched his arm.

The Ostanian defense liaison — the one who'd been avoiding him all evening — had apparently decided to stop avoiding him, and the man's greeting broke through Damian's concentration like a hand shattering a water's surface. His thoughts scattered into handshake protocol — the liaison's name, his file, his known positions on border security, the correct register for professional warmth. The childhood memory submerged. Sank back below the professional surface, unfinished. The name still unspoken.

Anya put her glass down on the bar because her hand was shaking. She pressed her palm flat against the bar's polished surface, the wood cool and solid under her fingers, and breathed.
