In through the nose, out through the mouth, the way Loid had taught her for tactical stress and the way Yor had taught her separately for the kind of stress that came from wanting to hit something but not being allowed to. Different teachings. Same daughter. She waited until her pulse stopped hammering against the inside of her wrist and then she picked up the glass again because a person standing at a bar without a drink looked like a person who'd just had bad news.

That was too close. Three seconds from her cover identity disintegrating inside his mind, from Damian connecting the girl at Eden Academy to the Westalian attaché to the intelligence operative who'd been working alongside him for weeks. And when he got there — when, not if, because the memory was closer each time and it was never going to stop surfacing — the questions that followed would be the kind she couldn't answer without burning everything. Why was a girl he'd known at school now working as a Westalian cultural attaché? Why had she appeared in his orbit at the state dinner? Why did she always know the right things to say and the right moments to intervene?

She needed cover. Now.

Anya found Hesse — a Westalian diplomat who ran training programs for junior staff — and inserted herself into a conversation Hesse was having with an Ostanian academic about educational exchange programs. She waited for an opening that took twenty seconds to arrive.

"The diplomatic preparatory courses are actually great for that," Anya said, when the academic mentioned networking challenges. "I went through the training exchange three years ago and I still keep running into people at these receptions who I think I recognize from the program. You spend a week in intensive sessions and everybody's face sort of blurs together afterward, you know?"

Hesse agreed. The academic agreed. Nobody cared about this anecdote. That was the point — a verbal footnote, planted in a conversation near enough to Damian's end of the room that the idea might settle into his uncertainty later. A false explanation for why the attaché looked familiar. A trail laid over the real one.

She was getting better at this. At the covering, the deflecting, the small deceptions that kept the larger ones intact. She wasn't sure that was something to feel good about.

She drifted through the crowd and spent a few minutes with a woman near the south windows who was genuinely, uncomplicatedly passionate about ceramics — a cultural program officer from the Ostanian side, actually doing the job Anya was pretending to do, thinking about glaze chemistry and firing temperatures and the specific satisfaction of pulling a bowl from a kiln and finding it had turned out right. The woman's name was Lentz. She wore a silver ring shaped like a coiled wire and her thoughts matched her words perfectly. No second layer. No concealed agenda. Just ceramics.

Anya asked about the Ostenheim residency program and received a detailed, enthusiastic answer about studio access and traditional wood-firing. It was the most restful three minutes of her evening, and she told Lentz the exhibition sounded wonderful and meant every word.

Near the east entrance, Yuri's attention tracked over Anya again. Not scanning for her, exactly — but noting her position the way he noted every position, adding it to the map he was building of the room's movements.

*— she went from the bar to the Hesse group to the ceramics woman to the south-window cluster. Four groups in twenty minutes. Cultural attachés park in one cluster and work outward. She's moving through the room. She's covering ground.*

A pause. His thoughts shifted register, and the professional sharpness softened at the edges.

*— Sis would be upset if she knew I was watching her like this. But watching is what I do. And wanting to trust someone isn't the same as having a reason.*

Anya moved away from the south windows and made herself visible at the bar for a few minutes, standing still, holding a glass of sparkling water, performing the specific kind of social stillness that said *I'm resting between conversations* and nothing more suspicious than that. It was the best she could do.

* * *

By nine o'clock the room was at peak density.

Both delegations had arrived in full. The second wave of guests had merged with the first, aides and plus-ones and late arrivals filling the spaces between clusters until the ballroom held something close to three hundred people, and the noise — her kind of noise — had become a physical pressure behind Anya's eyes.

She'd been scanning for two hours. The fatigue was specific — not sleepiness but a flattening, as if the resolution of her reception was degrading the way a radio signal degrades in a tunnel. Individual thoughts were harder to isolate. She had to focus longer to lock on, and the effort cost more each time, and the headache behind her right eye had deepened into something that pulsed when she turned her head too quickly. Her jaw was tight. She kept unclenching it and then finding it clenched again a minute later, the muscles working on their own while her attention was somewhere else. The crowd noise had layered into something atmospheric, a weather system sitting on her awareness, dense and formless, punctuated only occasionally by a thought clear enough to grab.

*— she's wearing the same dress as the ambassador's wife, this is going to be a problem —*
*— my feet, god, I knew these shoes were a mistake —*
*— the November deployment schedule still hasn't been signed because Brandt is stalling, and if he stalls past the review date —*

She kept the Brandt fragment. Discarded the shoes and the dress. A waiter passed close and his thoughts were entirely about a fight with his roommate over dishes in the sink, and the specificity of his grievance — three days, three sets of dishes, a pan with burned rice that his roommate claimed was "soaking" — was so vivid and so minor that Anya almost laughed, and the almost-laugh hurt her head.

She was near the Westalian delegation now, passing through a cluster of staffers and officials discussing energy policy in the circular, patient way that diplomatic conversations moved when nobody wanted to commit to a position. She caught their thoughts as she drifted through — energy markets, energy tariffs, someone thinking about a sick child at home and whether the fever had broken, energy projections, someone attracted to the speaker and trying not to think about it, energy subsidies.

And then:

*— the asset inside WISE has been reliable, but the handoff timeline needs to move forward before —*

The thought was clear. Surgically clear, the kind of clarity that came from concentrated focus — someone working a specific problem in their mind with real attention. It cut through the crowd noise like a signal on a clean frequency.

Anya stopped walking.

Her fingers tightened on the stem of her glass. She turned, scanning the people around her — faces, positions, who was close enough, who was thinking that —

*— need to accelerate the reporting window, the asset's coverage shifts after the review cycle and if the handoff isn't —*

Close. Someone within her range — fifteen meters, maybe less. Someone in this cluster of Westalian staffers and Ostanian counterparts and servers passing with trays and security personnel stationed at the walls. Someone who knew about an asset inside WISE. Someone who was thinking about a handoff timeline and a reporting schedule, thinking about it here, at a reception, standing within reach of the very people the intelligence was being stolen from.

Anya turned her head. Twelve people within range. Fourteen. She couldn't fix on it — the thoughts were overlapping, someone was pushing through the crowd to her left, the signal shifted.

*— the trade surplus numbers are going to —*
*— left my phone in the car, I need to —*
*— asset inside WISE — the extraction protocol —*

She moved two steps to her left. The signal didn't sharpen. She turned toward the window and caught a fragment — *handoff* — thin, dissolving — and then it was gone, swallowed by the roar of three hundred minds thinking about dinner plans and office politics and their children and the border and tomorrow's meeting agenda and whether they'd said something stupid to the wrong person earlier.

Gone. The thinker had moved, or shifted focus, or been interrupted by a conversation, and the thought had sunk back below the surface like a coin dropped into dark water. The crowd noise rushed back in to fill the gap — someone's knee pain, someone's parking meter, someone's husband, someone's budget meeting tomorrow — and the signal was buried, irretrievable, and she was standing in a room full of people and she had no idea which one of them had been thinking about betraying the agency she worked for.

She stayed where she was. She scanned the nearby faces — a Westalian analyst she recognized from the mission, an Ostanian trade official she'd passed earlier, a woman in a green dress whose thoughts were about a hotel booking, two men discussing something about fishing rights whose thoughts were actually about fishing rights. Nobody thinking about WISE. Nobody thinking about assets or handoffs or extraction protocols. The thought had been there and now it wasn't, and she couldn't chase a signal that no longer existed any more than she could chase a word someone had spoken ten minutes ago into a wind.

Anya stood in the middle of the ballroom holding her glass hard enough to feel the stem flex.

*The asset inside WISE.*

Someone in her organization was compromised. Someone inside WISE — the agency she'd trained for, built her cover around, given two years of active fieldwork to, the agency her father had given decades of his life to — was feeding intelligence. To the Renewal Front, or to someone connected to the Renewal Front, or to someone whose operational interests aligned with the forces Anya and Damian had spent weeks trying to map. She didn't know which. She didn't know who. She'd caught a fragment — clean, specific, devastating — and then lost the thinker in the crowd, and there was no way to recover the signal because she couldn't explain to anyone why she'd need to scan the room again, because she couldn't explain how she'd found it in the first place.

She put the glass down on a passing server's tray. Her hand was steady because she'd made it steady.

She found a marble column between the bar and the east windows and leaned her back against it. The stone was cool through the fabric of her dress. She closed her eyes for one second and let the noise of the room wash over her without trying to sort it.

*— hope the car's still there, meter ran out at —*
*— she said "me too" instead of "I love you too" and those aren't the same, are they? Are they the —*
*— if the eastern corridor isn't reinforced by November the whole defensive posture —*
*— I'm so tired of this, tired of smiling and being charming and pretending I care about trade routes, I just want to go home and take off these shoes and sit somewhere nobody wants anything from me —*

She opened her eyes. The room was the same. The string quartet had started something new. Near the bar, someone laughed — a big, genuine, uncomplicated laugh — and Anya wanted to find whoever it was and stand near them, because their thoughts would be warm and unconcerned and thinking about something that didn't matter, and right now she wanted very badly to be near a thought that didn't matter.

She couldn't do that. She was working. She was always working.

Here was what she knew: someone inside WISE was passing intelligence, and she'd discovered it by reading a thought she wasn't supposed to hear, using an ability she'd never told anyone she possessed, while standing at a reception she'd attended under a cover identity that was itself a deception wrapped inside a deception. She couldn't report this through channels. She couldn't explain how she knew without explaining what she was. She couldn't tell Sylvia — who trusted her, who she'd already lied to with a false assessment three weeks ago, whose trust she'd read in real time while the lie was being accepted. She couldn't go to Sylvia and say *There's a mole in WISE* because Sylvia would ask *How do you know?* and the answer was *I heard someone thinking about it*, and the conversation that followed would end Anya's career and her cover and every relationship she'd built inside the agency.

She couldn't tell Damian. Their alliance ran on the premise that she was a competent operative with a network and good instincts, not a girl who could hear people's private thoughts and had been doing it to everyone she'd ever known since she was four years old.

She couldn't tell anyone. That was the joke, if it was a joke. The most connected person in the room — the one who could hear every mind, every secret, every unguarded fear and private tenderness — was also the most alone. Because every piece of intelligence she gathered was stolen property, and the one honest thing she could say about herself was the one thing she could never, under any circumstance, say to anyone.

Anya straightened up from the column. She smoothed the front of her dress. She picked up a fresh glass of water from a tray and held it like a prop, because that was what people did at parties — they held things so their hands had a purpose.

She would need to find the mole herself. Quietly. Without telling WISE what she was looking for or why she was looking. She would investigate her own agency using tradecraft her father had taught her and an ability she'd never disclosed to him, conducting an unauthorized investigation inside an organization she'd already lied to, on the basis of information she'd obtained through a method that no one in the world knew existed.

She'd add it to the list. Below the false report. Below the concealed alliance with Damian. Below the childhood recognition that was three seconds from shattering her cover. Below the nightly, ordinary reality of being inside every person she cared about's head without once having been invited.

Across the room, Damian caught her eye and gave a small nod — the signal that meant he was wrapping up, that they'd debrief afterward. His thoughts, which she caught in the moment of eye contact, were professional and tired and, underneath, gently concerned.

*— she looks off. Something happened. I'll ask later. Not in here.*

She nodded back. The nod meant *I'm fine*, which was a lie, but she'd had a lot of practice with those tonight.

The string quartet started something brighter. The general made another pass at the shrimp platter and took four without hesitation this time, the etiquette debate apparently settled. The young diplomat by the window had deleted her mental breakup text and decided she'd try again tomorrow. Yuri Briar checked his watch — a battered analog thing with a cracked crystal that Anya knew was a gift from Yor, years ago, because she'd been in the room when Yor wrapped it — and thought about whether he'd told his sister Thursday or Friday.

Anya stood in the middle of a room full of people whose inner lives she could hear, who didn't know she was listening, who would never know, and felt the specific loneliness of knowing everything about everyone and being fully known by no one.

She finished her water and went to find her coat.


# Chapter 9

She caught the thought from across the street — sharp, focused, nothing like the ambient noise of Berlint's evening commuters. *Left side, low approach, maintain visual on target.*

Anya had been following Damian for twenty minutes, keeping a block and a half between them since he left the Foreign Affairs Ministry. A late meeting, the building mostly dark behind him. She'd told herself this was standard protocol — monitoring an operational partner in an unsecured environment. Which was partly true. The rest of the truth was that three days after catching a thought-fragment about *the asset inside WISE* at the diplomatic reception, she couldn't leave Damian's movements unchecked.

The schedule was not clean.

She stopped at a newsstand, tilting a copy of the *Berlinter Abendblatt* toward the streetlight, and opened her range.

Two minds. Both disciplined — surface thoughts running operational, not personal. The first: *Distance closing. He'll turn at the next block. Move to intercept position.* The second, further back: *Package is unaware. Clean approach.*

Package. Not target, not subject. Package. Military vocabulary, not SSS, not police.

The newsstand vendor said something about exact change. Anya dropped coins on the counter without counting them and moved.

Damian was a hundred meters ahead, walking at the unhurried pace of someone thinking about something else. She caught the edge of his thoughts — budget figures from the meeting, a line he wished he'd delivered differently to an undersecretary, the particular irritation of a man who'd sat through three hours of committee procedure. Oblivious.

The first attacker was thirty meters behind him, closing the gap. The second had crossed the street and was angling toward the mouth of a side street — Schultzgasse, narrow, poor lighting, residential doors locked by nine. If Damian turned left at the corner, the way he always did, they'd catch him in the gap between the streetlights.

She couldn't shout his name. Not her cover name for him, not his real one. Any name she used in earshot of two assailants became operational intelligence.

She ran.

The cobblestones were slick with fallen leaves — autumn in Berlint made every sidewalk a liability — and her flats had no grip. She cut across the street between a parked delivery truck and a taxi, reading both attackers' intent as she moved. The first had his hand inside his jacket. The second was reaching for something in a pocket — not a gun, smaller, a collapsible baton or a blade.

Damian turned left onto Schultzgasse.

*Now,* the first attacker thought. *Go.*

They moved in from both ends. Anya came around the corner and saw it: Damian walking between two lamp gaps, the first man accelerating behind him, the second stepping out from a recessed doorway ahead. Coordinated. Professional. They'd rehearsed this.

"Damian!"

He turned. That saved him — the first man's grab caught his shoulder instead of his neck, and Damian stumbled sideways against a building wall. The second man pulled a telescoping baton and closed fast.

Anya hit the second man from the side, driving her elbow into his kidney the way Yor had shown her — find the floating rib, commit your weight, follow through. He grunted and bent, and she grabbed his baton arm with both hands and twisted. Yor would've taken the arm out of the socket. Anya wasn't Yor. The man wrenched free and swung the baton backhanded, and it connected with her left side — ribs — a white shock that made her vision pinch at the edges and stole the breath from her chest.

She read his next thought — *step back, reset, take the throat* — and ducked under the follow-up swing, putting her shoulder into his diaphragm and driving forward. They went down together onto the wet cobblestones. He outweighed her by forty kilos at least. He was already rolling, pinning her leg with his hip, one hand reaching for her neck—

Damian hit the first attacker with his briefcase, a wild overhead swing that caught the man across the ear. The man staggered. Damian hit him again. The briefcase burst open and papers scattered across the wet street in a slow white flutter, and the man caught Damian's arm and drove an elbow into his brow, splitting the skin above his left eye. Blood sheeted down his face immediately.

*Knee,* Anya thought — not a plan, just an instinct — and she brought her heel down hard on the second attacker's kneecap. She felt something give under her shoe, a crunch that transmitted up through her leg, and he screamed and rolled off her. She scrambled to her feet. Her ribs pulsed with a deep, sick ache, and she couldn't fully straighten on the left side.

"Get up," she said. "Move. *Now.*"

The first attacker had recovered. He was circling back toward them, his gait steady, professional — the kind of person who took a briefcase to the head and recalculated rather than panicked. She read his intent: *neutralize the woman first, she's the trained one, the man is secondary.* He'd identified her as the threat inside six seconds of combat. That told her something about the caliber of what they were dealing with.

Damian was on his knees, one hand pressed to his bleeding eyebrow, the other still gripping the handle of his destroyed briefcase. She grabbed his arm and hauled him upright. Behind them, the first attacker was recovering, shaking off the briefcase hit. The second was trying to stand on a knee that wouldn't hold. She caught the first man's thought — *abort, regroup, secondary extraction* — and pulled Damian the other way, toward the main street, running.

He kept pace. Blood ran into his left eye and he wiped it with his sleeve and kept running. Neither of them spoke until they'd crossed two blocks and turned onto a residential avenue where the streetlights were bright and a few late pedestrians still walked dogs and carried groceries.

Anya pressed her back against a building wall and tried to breathe. A woman walking a dachshund gave them a wide berth on the sidewalk, her thoughts a startled loop of *blood — that man is bleeding — should I call someone — no, keep walking, this isn't your business.* Her left side protested each inhale — not broken, she thought, probably not, but badly bruised. She could feel the shape of the baton's impact like a stripe painted across her ribs.

"What—" Damian started, and then had to stop because he was breathing too hard. Blood was still running from his eyebrow, tracking down the side of his face, dripping off his jaw onto his coat collar. "What the hell just happened."

She was already thinking about the precision of it. They'd known which meeting. They'd known what time he'd leave, which route he'd take. The meeting hadn't been publicly scheduled — it was a closed session with ministry staff. Someone with access to Damian's calendar, or access to the intelligence networks that tracked it.

*The asset inside WISE.*

"I need to get you somewhere safe," she said.

---

They walked west for fifteen minutes. Damian held a handkerchief to his eyebrow — he'd produced it from his coat pocket with the automatic propriety of someone whose upbringing included pressed linen for every occasion, even being ambushed on a Tuesday — and Anya kept her arm pressed to her left side and her range open, scanning every approaching mind for hostile intent. She found nothing. A woman thinking about her cat. A man composing a text message to someone who'd annoyed him at work. A teenager listening to music so loud Anya could hear the bass line bleeding from his headphones *and* catch the lyrics repeating in his thoughts. Normal Berlint. Normal evening. Except for the two of them, walking too fast, bleeding.

The WISE fallback apartment was in a residential block in the west district, far from both the diplomatic quarter and the government buildings. Anya had memorized its location during her first week in Berlint and had never used it. The building's front door opened with a six-digit code she had to enter twice because her hands were shaking. The apartment was on the third floor, no elevator, and by the time they reached it she was holding her left side with her arm and breathing through her teeth.

Inside: bare walls, a couch frame with a thin mattress where cushions should have been, a kitchen counter with a sink and nothing on it. A bathroom. A closet that held a first-aid kit, two changes of generic clothes, a prepaid phone, and a handgun she didn't look at. The heat wasn't on. The air smelled like dust and the staleness of a room no one had opened in months. A window overlooked the back of another apartment building, its curtainless glass showing the blue-grey flicker of someone else's television.

Damian sat on the edge of the mattress-couch and pressed the heel of his hand to his eyebrow. Blood seeped between his fingers. His thoughts were still cycling at combat speed — *who were they, how did they know, the Ministry calendar is internal, was I followed earlier, is she hurt worse than she's showing, her hands are shaking, she's trying to hold them still and they're shaking.*

They were. She noticed when he noticed.

Anya opened the first-aid kit. Gauze pads, antiseptic wipes, butterfly strips, ibuprofen in a foil packet, a small bottle of saline solution. She washed her hands at the kitchen sink with dish soap that had been sitting on the counter since the apartment was stocked, and brought the kit to the couch.

"Let me see."

He moved his hand. The cut was about two centimeters long, just above his left eyebrow, deep enough to still be bleeding freely but not down to bone. A clean split — the elbow had landed precisely.

She cleaned it with saline, pressing gauze to the wound while he winced. His jaw tightened but he didn't pull away. She applied two butterfly strips, pressing the edges of the cut together. Blood welled at the margin and she blotted it. His skin was warm under her fingers, even in the cold apartment. She was aware of this — the warmth, the closeness, his breath catching when the antiseptic stung — in a way she didn't want to examine.

"Your turn," he said.

"I'm fine."

"You're holding your side like something's broken."

"It's not broken." She sat on the other end of the couch and, because there was no way to check bruised ribs that didn't involve lifting her shirt, she lifted her shirt. The bruise was already forming — a dark bar across her left side, following the line of the baton strike, the red deepening toward a color that would be purple by morning. She pressed her fingers along the ribs and winced but nothing shifted under the skin. Bruised, not fractured.

Damian was looking at the bruise with an expression she didn't need telepathy to read. His thoughts confirmed it: *That's from getting to me in time. She took that hit because she was protecting me and I was—* The thought cut itself off. Self-recrimination, then. He ran his hand through his hair and looked at the floor.

"Ibuprofen," she said, and he handed her the foil packet from the kit. She swallowed three with water from the kitchen tap that ran rust-colored for the first few seconds, then clear and cold. She sat back down.

A silence settled. The apartment ticked and creaked — pipes, settling building, the small sounds that empty spaces produce at night. The television next door was audible through the wall, a sportscaster narrating something with practiced enthusiasm. Anya could hear the neighbor's thoughts, too — faint, domestic, something about a bet on the match. Normal life, on the other side of the plaster.

"How'd you know," Damian said. Not quite a question. He was looking at his hands, which had blood on them — his own, mostly. *She was already there. Before it started. She was already behind me. How is that possible unless—*

"I'm good at reading situations."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the one I've got."

He looked at her. His thoughts were running a cost-benefit: push the question and risk the alliance, or accept the non-answer and keep what they had. She watched the calculation happen, the way she always did — like reading a document someone was drafting in real time. He chose the non-answer.

"Okay," he said. And then, quieter: "Thank you. For—" He gestured vaguely at the room, the night, the bruise on her side. "Yeah."

"Yeah."

More silence. The sportscaster was getting excited about something. Anya could feel the adrenaline leaving her body like water running out of a bath — not all at once but steadily, and what it left behind was heavy and cold and unsteady. She wanted to sleep. She wanted to call Loid. She wanted to go home and eat Yor's terrible cooking and not think about the precision of an ambush that should have been impossible.

"They knew your schedule," she said.

"I noticed."

"That meeting wasn't public."

"No." *Ministry coordination only. Internal calendar. If someone had access, it had to be — but who would —* He stopped the thought. Rubbed his face. "Can we do this tomorrow?"

"Tomorrow."

He leaned his head back against the wall. The butterfly strips on his brow were already darkening with blood soaking through at the margins. He'd have a scar there. Small, but permanent. She'd given him that by being three seconds too slow.

They sat without talking for a while. The silence wasn't uncomfortable — it was the silence of two people whose bodies had just spent all their available panic and were now subsiding into the heavy, stupid calm that follows. Anya could feel a tremor starting in her hands and folded them together in her lap. The bruise on her side was stiffening, the tissue tightening as it swelled, and when she shifted on the mattress a bright line of pain ran from her ribs to her hip.

Damian was looking around the apartment — the bare walls, the naked bulb, the sink with its rust-edged faucet. *This is where spies go when something goes wrong. This is what safe means for people like her. A closet with a gun in it and a mattress with no sheets.* The thought had a quality she recognized. Not pity. Something closer to understanding.

"Eden Academy had this thing," he said.

The shift was so abrupt she almost asked him to repeat it.

"After the dodgeball tournament," he went on. His eyes were still closed, head against the wall. "The losing team had to clean the gym. Mop the floors, stack the chairs, the whole ordeal." He touched the butterfly strips absently. "My team lost because this girl punched me during the game. Not with a ball. With her actual fist. Because I said something about her hair."

"You got punched during dodgeball."

"I got punched during dodgeball. I was eight. She was — I don't remember how old. Same age, maybe. I dropped the ball I was holding and her team got us out and we had to mop for an hour." His mouth twitched. "And the thing is, I can't even remember what I said about her hair. But I remember the punch."

Anya pressed her lips together. She remembered the punch too. She remembered the exact words he'd said about her hair — something about it looking like bubblegum, delivered with the full confidence of a boy who thought he was being clever — and she remembered the satisfaction of her small fist connecting with his shoulder. She'd gotten a detention. It was worth it.

"And then the school festival cleanup," he said. "Same year, maybe? The paint crew. Someone knocked over a whole shelf of poster paints and the volunteers had to stay until nine scrubbing the floor." *She was there too. The pink-haired girl. She was on the cleanup crew and she was laughing about the paint — she had blue paint on her shoes and she was sliding around on the wet floor — and her laugh—*

He opened his eyes and looked at her.

"You remind me of someone," he said.

"You've told me that."

"I know." *Anya Forger. Her name was Anya. She had pink hair and she punched me during dodgeball and her laugh was—* The thought was clear and specific and right there, a name on the tip of his mind, not yet spoken but forming. He rubbed his eyes with the back of his hand. "Sorry. Getting hit in the head makes me nostalgic, apparently."

She laughed. Not the controlled, cover-appropriate laugh she deployed at diplomatic functions. A real laugh, too loud for the small apartment, the kind that comes from being exhausted and hurt and alive and sitting next to someone who just described the worst dodgeball game in Eden Academy history while bleeding from his eyebrow.

He laughed too. Startled by her laugh, and then caught by it — his laughter came out rough and surprised, and the butterfly strips pulled as his brow moved and he pressed gauze to his face while still laughing, which made them both laugh harder. The sportscaster next door hit a crescendo. Anya's left side screamed with every convulsion and she didn't care.

It wound down the way real laughter does — in diminishing waves, each one quieter, until they were both just breathing hard and the apartment was still around them again.

"I haven't laughed like that in a while," he said.

"Me either."

His thoughts were settling. The operational noise was fading — the fear, the tactical cycling, the who-knew-what — and underneath, something quieter and less guarded was surfacing. He was thinking about the apartment, the thin mattress, the cold. About the gauze in his hand and the woman sitting a cushion's width away from him. Not the intelligence officer. Not the alliance partner. Her. The way she'd fought — not graceful, not by-the-book, but fierce and willing to take the hit. *She got between them and me. Didn't even hesitate.*

And then, softer, almost not a thought at all: *I don't want to go home.*

---

She should have moved to the other side of the room. Should have checked the building's perimeter, or called in a report to the WISE emergency line, or started cataloguing the operational security failures that had put two military-trained killers on Damian's walking route. She knew this. She listed the things she should have done even as she didn't do any of them.

The silence between them had changed. It was not the silence of people who'd run out of things to say. It was the silence of two people who both knew what was about to happen and were deciding whether to let it.

His thoughts were open. Not deliberately — he was too tired and too hurt and too relieved to maintain the usual diplomatic surface. She was reading him the way she'd read a hundred rooms at a hundred receptions, except this wasn't a room full of strangers and the thoughts she was catching were not state secrets or diplomatic gossip. They were: *she's right there. She's close enough to touch. If I reached out —*

She should have stopped listening.

"Damian."

"Yeah."

"I'm sorry about your eyebrow."

"It's fine." He touched the butterfly strips. "I'll look rugged."

"You'll look like you got elbowed in the face."

"Ruggedly elbowed." He was almost smiling, and his thoughts had gone quiet — not empty, but still, the way attention narrows to a single point when everything else has been stripped away. *Just look at her. Just look at her and don't think about the rest of it.*

She was looking at him. The overhead light in the safe house was a single bare bulb, and it cast everything in flat, unforgiving brightness — the cut on his brow, the blood that had dried along his jaw, the dark hair falling forward because he'd been running his hands through it for an hour. He looked like exactly what he was: a twenty-year-old who'd nearly been killed and was sitting in an empty apartment with a woman he was trying not to think too much about.

He leaned forward. She didn't move away.

The kiss started carefully — his mouth on hers, tentative, a question in the pressure of it. His hand found the side of her face. His fingers were cold from the unheated room. She could taste copper where he'd wiped blood from his lip earlier, and underneath that, nothing — just him, just the warmth of his mouth and the slight roughness of his jaw and the fact that he was kissing her and she was letting him and nothing about this was operational.

She kissed him back. And the diplomatic caution, the alliance terms, the negotiated parameters of their professional arrangement — all of it became distant and abstract. His other hand settled on her hip, light, giving her the chance to pull away. She didn't pull away. She put her hand on the back of his neck and felt his pulse under her fingers, fast, and his thought was just *oh* — and then his arm tightened around her and the caution in the kiss burned away.

She was trying not to read his mind. She was actively, consciously trying to close the part of herself that had been open since she was a child — the involuntary reception that operated whether she wanted it or not, the thing that made her what she was. She tried to close it the way you try not to hear music bleeding through a wall. The way you try to ignore a voice calling your name.

She mostly failed.

What she caught came in fragments, unstructured, nothing like the organized sentences of his diplomatic thinking: *this is real — she's — her mouth — I want —* And then, below the fragments, the layer he wasn't controlling at all. Loneliness. Not the curated loneliness of a man making sympathetic conversation about his difficult life, but the structural kind — the kind that had been there so long it had become architecture. Years of it. Dinners eaten alone in the apartment with the unpacked boxes. Meetings where he performed beliefs he despised. The weight of a surname that meant something to everyone in the country except the person carrying it. And beneath the loneliness, stubborn, almost defiant: hope. That this woman who'd appeared in his life and fought for him and laughed at his terrible stories and sat with him bleeding in a cold apartment — that she was real. That this meant something.

Anya felt it all. She felt it the way she'd felt Sylvia's trust when she'd sat across from her handler and filed the false report — involuntarily, completely, and with the specific nausea of receiving something she hadn't earned and couldn't return honestly.

He kissed her harder and his hand moved from her hip to the small of her back, pulling her closer, and his thoughts dissolved. Not words anymore. Want, and warmth, and the half-formed image of her face and a physical awareness that was just his body against hers — the thin mattress shifting under their combined weight, her fingers in his hair, the ache in his brow that he was ignoring.

And then, like something breaking the surface of deep water, a thought rose through the rest of it with terrible clarity.

*Anya Forger.*

Not a question. Not a speculation. A name attached to a face attached to a memory: a girl in the second row of a class photo, eight years old, grinning at something off-camera. Pink hair pulled back with clips shaped like horns. The girl who punched him during dodgeball because he said something about her hair. The girl who laughed on the wet floor of the school festival with blue paint on her shoes.

*It's her. It's Anya Forger. It's—*

The recognition didn't complete. It hovered — achingly specific, close enough to name, but not yet committed to. His conscious mind was too overwhelmed to finish the work. Too much was happening in his hands and his chest and his mouth for the analytical part of him to lock it down. The name floated and the kiss continued and he thought *later, think about it later, right now she's here and she's—*

Anya heard every word.

She kept kissing him. She kept her hands in his hair and her mouth on his and she did not pull away, because pulling away would be the correct operational response and she was not, in this moment, capable of the correct operational response. She was capable of nothing except the knowledge that she was stealing from him. Every second of this was a theft. His most private feelings, laid open to her not by his choice but by the fact of what she was — the thing she was born with, the ability she'd never asked for, the same ability that had let her betray Sylvia and deceive Loid and read her mother's buried doubts about the Garden. And now, here, this: the unguarded tenderness of a man who was kissing her because he believed they were meeting each other equally.

They weren't. They had never been.

He pulled back eventually. Not far — his forehead against hers, both of them breathing hard. His thoughts were still warm and undefended and she could feel them like standing too close to an open fire.

"I don't know what this means for the operation," he said.

"I don't either."

"Probably nothing good."

"Probably not."

He laughed — quiet, close, just breath against her face. *I don't care. Whatever it costs. I don't care.*

She cared. She cared in a way he couldn't see and she couldn't explain and would never be able to justify. Because the feelings moving through her right now — the want, the tenderness, the specific ache of being close to someone who was close to her — she couldn't separate them from his. She'd been inside his mind for the most unguarded moment of his life, and her emotional responses were tangled with his emotional output, and she didn't know where his feelings ended and hers began.

That was the thing about telepathy that no one would ever understand, because no one knew. It didn't just give you information. It gave you feelings that weren't yours and made them indistinguishable from the ones that were. She had felt his loneliness, and now she felt lonely. She had felt his hope, and now she was hopeful. Was that connection? Or was it just contamination she'd mistaken for something real?

She thought about Loid. About the way he could compartmentalize — separate what he felt from what he did, keep the operation clean of personal debris. She'd trained under him for years, and she'd never learned that trick. Maybe you couldn't learn it when the personal debris arrived in other people's voices, uninvited, indistinguishable from your own thoughts.

She thought about Yor. About the stillness her mother carried after an assignment, the careful way she washed her hands, the gap between the sweetness and the thing underneath. Yor managed by not looking. Anya couldn't not look. That was her whole problem.

He settled back against the wall, his shoulder touching hers. The apartment was cold and neither of them moved to find a blanket or turn on a heater that probably didn't work anyway. His thoughts were drifting toward sleep — the exhaustion of adrenaline and injury finally catching up — and as they drifted, the name surfaced again. *Anya Forger.* Quiet this time. Almost fond. Not a deduction anymore, just a feeling. Like a song you used to know the words to.

She sat very still beside him and listened to his thoughts slow and thin toward sleep. The name came and went. Came and went. Each time, she waited for it to crystallize into certainty, for him to open his eyes and say *I know who you are.*

He didn't. He fell asleep with his head tilted sideways against the wall and his hand resting against hers on the mattress, the dried blood on his jaw cracking at the edges where his skin moved. Anya could hear the neighbor's television still going — the sportscaster had given way to a late-night talk show, murmured voices she couldn't make out.

She stayed awake. Her ribs ached when she breathed. His blood was on her fingers from the butterfly strips, dried now, brown at the margins of her nails. The bare bulb overhead buzzed at a frequency that had become part of the room's silence.

She had kissed him. She had read his mind while she kissed him. She had heard him think her real name and she had kept kissing him anyway. These were facts. She arranged them in order and they did not become less true.

Tomorrow she'd think about the ambush — about who had access to his schedule, about the chain of intelligence that had placed two military-trained killers on exactly the right block at exactly the right moment. Tomorrow she'd think about the mole inside WISE, and about the gaps in the operational picture that someone was exploiting, and about whether those gaps connected to a false report she'd filed in a debriefing room months ago while reading her handler's trust. Tomorrow she would be the operative again.

Tonight she sat in a cold apartment beside a man who was dreaming of a girl he used to know, and she let the name *Anya Forger* move through his sleeping thoughts like a current she couldn't stop, and she did not move, and she could not tell whether what she felt was love or its echo.


# Chapter 10

The milk was going to be a problem.

Yor held the carton at an angle under the kitchen light, reading the date stamp. Tomorrow. She put it back in the refrigerator, behind the leftover stew, where Loid was less likely to discover it. He had opinions about expiration dates that she considered disproportionate to the actual science of dairy spoilage, but she'd never found a way to say that without sounding like she was defending spoiled milk, which she wasn't. She was defending the principle that milk didn't become poison at midnight on the printed date. She was fairly sure about this.

Bond was on the living room rug, occupying approximately one-third of the available floor space with the unselfconscious entitlement of a very large, very old dog who had decided that this particular section of rug was his and always had been. His breathing was slow and even, and his legs twitched with whatever passed through a precognitive dog's sleeping mind. Yor sometimes caught Anya watching Bond with an expression that suggested she was listening to something Yor couldn't hear, but the habit was so old and so gentle that Yor had filed it years ago under the broad category of things about her daughter that she loved without understanding.

She was making a bento box. This was not strictly necessary — Loid was at the university for a consulting session, one of the occasional appointments that maintained a cover identity that had long since stopped feeling like one — and he wouldn't be back until evening. But Yor liked making them. The routine was grounding. Rice balls, three, with pickled plum. Tamagoyaki, two slices, trimmed where the browning had become something closer to charring. A thermos of mushroom miso soup from a recipe she'd clipped from a magazine in the city hall break room.

She tasted the soup. Too salty. Not catastrophically, but enough that she could hear Anya's polite silence if she ever served it — the careful way Anya said "It's good, Mama" when what she meant was *This is mostly salt*.

The bento went into the refrigerator with a note: *The soup is maybe a little salty. Sorry! Please don't feel like you have to drink it. But I think it's okay? — Y*

She was dotting the question mark when her phone buzzed.

Not a ring. A single pulse against the countertop, the kind that came from numbers that didn't exist in any directory. She set down the pen and picked up the phone.

An address on the northern outskirts. A name: Karl Dominic. A timeframe: tonight. And the six-digit code at the bottom that confirmed it was real.

She read it twice. The message would erase itself in ninety seconds. She set the phone face-down on the counter and went back to the bento box, adjusting the thermos so it wouldn't leak during refrigeration. She closed the lid. She washed the knife she'd used for the tamagoyaki, dried it, and returned it to the block.

Bond opened one eye from the rug. Closed it.

"I'm going out tonight," Yor told him. "Don't eat Papa's dinner. It's in the fridge."

She showered. In the bedroom, she dressed — fitted dark jacket, pants that didn't restrict movement at the hip, flat shoes with grip soles she'd ordered from a work-supply catalogue under the reasoning that icy sidewalks were dangerous and practical footwear was just common sense. Which was true. The shoes were also excellent for moving quietly across concrete and gravel, which was the actual reason she'd bought them.

The closet. Behind the shoe rack, behind a box of seasonal clothes she rotated every April and October with a conscientiousness that Loid found endearing and Anya found baffling. A locked case. The key lived on a chain around her neck alongside a small enamel peanut charm — a prize from an Eden Academy festival booth, won by a six-year-old Anya and presented with the solemnity of a military decoration. The charm's paint was chipping at the edges. She'd never replaced it.

Inside the case: two blades, a garrote wire, a compact medical kit, and lockpicks that had been Garden standard issue since before Anya was born. She selected the longer blade and the picks. The garrote she left. Single target, compound setting — the blade was cleaner, faster, something she could sheathe when the job was done. The garrote required sustained proximity she didn't enjoy. Not out of squeamishness. Because it was slow, and slow meant time spent close enough to hear the sound the body made, and that was a sound she preferred to keep brief.

She strapped the blade to the inside of her left forearm, under the jacket sleeve, and checked the draw. Smooth. The lockpicks went into her inner pocket.

In the hallway mirror, she looked like a woman heading out for the evening. A bit severe. She tried a small smile — the kind she gave the reception desk at city hall when she arrived for her shift. It softened things.

On her way out, she stopped at the hall table where Loid had left a shopping list in his careful handwriting: rice, laundry soap, Bond's joint medication, milk. She'd meant to remind him. He would buy a new carton, find the old one hiding behind the stew, pour it out, and give her the expression — not disapproval, exactly, more the gentle bewilderment of a man who had spent decades in intelligence operations and could not fathom his wife's relationship with dairy.

She added *milk — please check fridge first!* in her rounder, larger handwriting, and beneath it: *Back late. Don't wait up? There's stew.*

She locked the door behind her. The landing smelled of the neighbor's floor wax and the building's old radiators kicking against the October chill.

Then she went to kill Karl Dominic.

---

The compound was forty minutes north of Berlint proper, where the city's residential blocks thinned into industrial lots and gated estates belonging to people who valued privacy over proximity. Yor took the tram to the northern terminal and walked the remaining two kilometers along a service road paralleling a drainage canal. The air smelled of wet concrete, diesel from a freight depot she'd passed a kilometer back, and the faintly vegetal rot of standing water in the ditch.

The landscape changed as she walked. Berlint's streetlights gave way to intermittent industrial security lamps, and the sounds of the city — tram bells, traffic, a bar's music leaking from somewhere — faded into the low drone of freight machinery and then, past the last depot, into nothing much at all. The service road was unpaved beyond the terminal's reach: packed dirt and gravel that crunched under her soles. She passed a shuttered machine shop, a fenced lot full of construction aggregate, a drainage pump station where someone had left a folding chair and an empty thermos. The only other living thing she'd encountered in the last kilometer was a cat that crossed the road, evaluated her with the professional indifference of an animal that knew it couldn't be caught, and vanished into a culvert pipe.

She'd read the briefing on the tram — a file downloaded to her phone and programmed to delete after thirty minutes. Karl Dominic. Fifty-three. Arms broker. His supply chain had been linked to the recent military buildup along the Ostanian border: weapons transfers routed through shell companies, the kind of infrastructure that made wars possible by making weapons available. The Garden's assessment classified him as a destabilizing agent. Eliminate. Standard contract.

Standard. For twenty years, that word had been a foundation she could stand on. The Garden assigned targets. She carried out the assignment. The reasons existed at a level above her operational role, and she'd trusted them because the alternative — questioning every target, demanding justification for every life she ended — would have made the work impossible. Trust was the price of function. She'd paid it willingly.

She'd paid it for the shipping clerk, too. Three months ago. A man in the port district whose briefing profile listed arms connections that turned out, when she'd checked afterward, to be invoices for industrial machine parts. Legitimate equipment for a legitimate factory. The man had begged — not for himself, which she could manage, but for his daughter, who was waiting at home. They sometimes begged. She'd trained herself to close that channel years ago. But the invoices. The gap between the profile and the evidence. She'd reported the discrepancy to her handler. The handler had said: *Thank you, Yor. Don't worry about it. These things are reviewed.* And she'd stopped worrying.

She'd mostly stopped worrying.

She reached the compound at half past ten. The perimeter was a three-meter concrete wall topped with razor wire and motion sensors at the corners. Beyond it: the roofline of the main building, interior lights, the muted geometry of an estate that had been a manufacturing facility before Dominic repurposed it. Sealed loading docks on the east side. The main gate faced south, connected to the highway by a paved access road.

She settled into surveillance. Three men visible: one stationed at the gate, shifting his weight foot to foot with the restlessness of a man assigned a post he found boring; one near a cargo van parked in the loading area, its rear doors open; one walking the interior perimeter on a patrol route. She timed the patrol. Four minutes per circuit. Consistent rhythm. The pace of routine rather than alertness.

The north wall had a vulnerability. A motion sensor housing mounted at an angle that covered the airspace above the wall rather than the wall face itself — a factory installation, never corrected. The kind of error that accumulated in security systems maintained by people who checked boxes rather than tested coverage.

She waited for the patrol to round the south wall, went up the north wall at the sensor gap, and dropped into the compound's northeast corner behind a maintenance shed. The shed smelled of machine oil and dried grass clippings. She held still for thirty seconds. The patrol guard's boots crunched past on the other side — steady, unhurried, the steps of a man completing a task he'd completed a thousand times without incident.

The crunch faded toward the south. She moved.

The man at the cargo van was leaning against the rear bumper, smoking. His sidearm sat on his right hip, holstered, the retention snap undone. Structural carelessness — he'd never encountered trouble here, and the boredom of guard work had eroded whatever discipline he'd once maintained. Yor came around the van's blind side. The last three meters took less than a second. She put the blade through the base of his skull at the junction where the brainstem lived, and he dropped. She caught his weight against the van's bumper and lowered him to the pavement without noise. His cigarette rolled under the tire.

Inside the van: long wooden crates, unmarked, secured with military-grade packaging straps. The chemical smell of preservative oil seeped through the wood grain. Rifles. She counted twelve crates. Enough for a company.

She moved toward the main building.

The loading dock's sealed entrance had a secondary door — service access, a standard pin-tumbler lock that the picks handled in nine seconds. A corridor inside, fluorescent tubes overhead, half of them dead and the survivors flickering with a buzz that set her teeth on edge. An office door on the left, closed. Voices from deeper in the building — male, three or four of them, the measured rhythm of negotiation. The private sale. Buyers in the building.

She adjusted. Buyers meant witnesses and potentially more armed men. She needed the principal and the exit, nothing else.

Two guards in the corridor, stationed outside a conference room. The first noticed her at six meters and reached for his radio. Wrong decision. She closed the gap in a stride and drove the heel of her palm into his trachea. The sound he made was the sound of someone trying to breathe through a crushed straw. He buckled. The second guard was faster — he'd drawn his sidearm by the time she reached him — but his partner was between them, and the half-second he spent calculating a clear firing line was a half-second he didn't have. Her blade opened the inside of his forearm from wrist to elbow, deep enough to sever the flexor tendons, and the gun clattered to the floor tiles. She followed with her elbow below his ear, where the mastoid process made a target the size of a coin, and he folded.

She collected the first guard's radio. Quiet channel. No alarm.

The corridor branched. Left toward the offices. Right toward the voices. She went left. The buyers were not her contract.

Karl Dominic was alone in a corner office at the corridor's end, behind a desk buried in paperwork, speaking into a phone. He was shorter than his file photo suggested — stocky, balding, in a dress shirt with the sleeves pushed past his elbows. The office smelled of cigar smoke layered over cheap instant coffee, the kind sold in bulk at freight depot commissaries. A framed photograph on the desk: Dominic with a woman on a boat, somewhere sunny, both of them squinting at the camera with the particular resignation of people who'd been told to smile.

He looked up when the door opened. His face processed: confusion, then recognition of what she was, then fear. Three expressions in under a second, which was more than most people managed.

He dropped the phone and lunged for the desk drawer.

She was faster. She had been faster than every person she'd ever been sent to kill, and twenty years had not changed that — the speed lived below thought, below decision, in the architecture of muscle and nerve that the Garden had trained and she had maintained through thousands of hours of practice she told Loid was yoga. Her blade caught his reaching wrist before his hand found the drawer, and the cut was deep enough to part tendons. He screamed — a short, bitten-off sound, the kind that comes from someone whose body has overridden the preference for silence.

He tried to stand. She put the blade through the left side of his neck, angled for the carotid, and held it for the two seconds it took. His legs buckled. He went down against the desk, scattering papers, and slid to the floor trailing a red line down the laminate front.

The phone was still connected. A tinny voice on the other end: "Karl? Karl, what—"

Yor disconnected the call.

She cleaned the blade on the hem of Dominic's shirt and sheathed it. Her hands were steady. They were always steady during the work — the part of her body she trusted most, the part that didn't shake or hesitate or feel the weight of what it had done until afterward, when the adrenaline metabolized and the steadiness left and she was just Yor again, standing in a kitchen, cutting vegetables, and her hands would tremble for a moment over the cutting board and she'd tell herself it was the cold.

The papers on the desk.

She'd been trained not to investigate targets beyond the scope of the assignment. The Garden provided the briefing; the operative executed the contract. Investigation was a different role, different people, a different level of the organization. For twenty years, that division had been enough.

But the shipping clerk. The invoices that hadn't matched. The handler's voice: *Don't worry about it.* And the daughter, waiting at home.

Yor looked at the papers scattered across Dominic's desk and the ones that had fallen to the floor, some of them soaking up the blood pooling from his neck, and she didn't leave.

Shipping manifests. Transfer orders for military hardware — rifles, communications equipment, explosive ordnance — routed through holding companies whose names meant nothing but whose addresses she could parse: locations in Berlint's government district, near the defense ministry. The shipments were coded, but the routing told a story — military materiel flowing from international suppliers through Dominic's brokerage into channels that fed the Ostanian armed forces outside any official procurement process.

A name recurred on three separate manifests: Werner Voss. She didn't recognize it. She photographed the pages — every manifest, both sides, the routing numbers and destination codes.

One set of orders held her attention longer than the rest. Encrypted field communications equipment — military grade, the kind used for coordinating ground operations — shipped six weeks ago to a receiving address she recognized. Not the defense ministry directly but a warehouse in the adjacent district, the kind of anonymous drop point that existed to keep deliveries off official books. The quantities suggested equipping a field unit, not filling a depot. An active operation, being assembled through Karl Dominic's desk.

She checked the shipping dates against her own recent assignments. Three months ago, the Garden had sent her to eliminate a logistics coordinator in the eastern industrial district — a man whose file said he'd been diverting weapons shipments for personal profit. She'd completed the contract in his office, at his desk, not unlike the way she'd completed this one. His name didn't appear in Dominic's manifests. But the shipping routes overlapped. The same holding companies. The same coded destination addresses. The same pipeline.

She'd killed a man inside that pipeline. She might have been cleaning it up for the people who built it.

She photographed everything remaining on the desk.

Then she found the drawer.

It was the one Dominic had been reaching for. Not a weapon inside — a binder. Heavy paper, neatly organized, held together with a metal clip. Administrative documentation maintained with care: not the hurried paperwork of a desk but the careful records of a man who understood that documentation was insurance.

Subcontracting records. A ledger of completed assignments. Dates, payments received, and two columns: the party that had contracted the work, and the organization that carried it out.

The contracting party was coded. She didn't recognize the designator.

The executing organization, for nine entries spanning four years, was listed by a numerical code she knew as well as her own address. She'd seen it on her payment records for twenty years — the deposits that arrived on the fifteenth of each month, the brief confirmations after each completed assignment, the secure documents she signed when she accepted new contracts.

The Garden.

Her hands stopped being steady.

She read the entries again. Nine assignments. Four years. Targets eliminated by Garden operatives — maybe by her, maybe by people she'd trained alongside, people whose faces she saw at the biannual operational reviews the Garden held in rented banquet halls where they ate canapes and discussed logistics the way accountants discussed quarterly reports. These were their assignments. At the request of whatever entity ran Dominic's weapons pipeline.

The targets were listed by code, not by name, but the pattern was legible: former associates who'd become liabilities. Witnesses. Competitors whose continued existence created risk for the supply chain. The Garden hadn't been eliminating threats to national stability. The Garden had been removing loose ends for an arms-trafficking network that fed the very military buildup the Garden cited as justification for Dominic's own elimination.

Karl Dominic was a problem. And the Garden had spent four years solving problems for the people who created him.

She photographed every page. Her phone screen was bright in the dim office. Dominic's blood had reached her shoes — a dark tongue extending from under the desk — and she stepped back the way she'd step away from a puddle in the street. Automatic. Her body doing the practical thing while her mind tried to hold a shape it couldn't yet see the edges of.

Twenty years. The measurement kept arriving — not as an argument but as a fact that demanded reckoning. Twenty years of trust. Twenty years of the Garden saying *this person is dangerous* and Yor believing it, because believing was how she lived with what her hands did. She'd told herself a story about protection — that she killed so that people like Anya and Loid and Bond could live in a world with fewer dangerous men in it. The story had been good enough. It had been the thing she held at night when the steadiness left her hands and the faces arrived in the dark behind her closed eyes: the story that said their deaths served something, that her violence had a shape and the shape was shelter.

What if some of them hadn't been dangerous. Not all — she wasn't ready for all. But some. The shipping clerk with the machine-parts invoices and the daughter waiting at home. The nine entries in a binder in a dead man's drawer.

The thought wasn't fully formed. It was a fracture in a foundation — visible, running from somewhere she couldn't see to somewhere she couldn't reach. She could still stand on it. She could still function. She'd photographed the evidence and she would bring it home and she would —

She didn't know what she would do. That was the part that frightened her. She had always known.

She pocketed the phone, checked the corridor — clear — and moved back through the loading dock. The guards in the hallway were still unconscious. The gate guard was at his post, facing south, out of sightline. The buyers' muffled negotiation still carried through the building's interior walls, the deal proceeding as if nothing had changed inside the corner office at the end of the corridor.

She went back over the north wall at the same sensor gap, the same route — a mirror of her entry. Her left hand caught the razor wire on the descent. She felt the resistance and the tug but not the pain. The pain arrived thirty seconds later on the service road: a line of hot sting across her palm that resolved into a shallow cut running along the old scar — the one from when Anya was eight and learning to cut carrots, and the knife had slipped from her small grip and caught Yor's hand instead. Anya had cried for twenty minutes. Yor hadn't minded the cut. She'd held her bleeding palm behind her back so Anya wouldn't feel bad and said, "See? It's nothing. Let's try again."

She wrapped the cut in her jacket sleeve and kept walking.

---

She was fifty meters from the compound wall, where the service road curved toward the canal, when she saw a figure behind the industrial waste containers that lined the drainage ditch.

The figure stood.

It was Anya.

Yor stopped. Her daughter was in dark clothes — not the diplomatic wardrobe, not the outfits of a cultural attache at Westalian embassy events, but the kind of clothes Yor herself was wearing. Practical. Unreflective. The kind that existed to not be noticed. Anya's hair was pulled back under a dark knit cap. She had a small pack over one shoulder. She looked like someone who had no legitimate reason to be standing outside a secured compound on the northern outskirts of Berlint at eleven o'clock at night.

She also looked thin. She always looked thin. Yor worried about this the way she worried about the big things — not the milk or the soup, which were small worries that had solutions, but the kind of worry that lived in her chest and didn't go away because Anya was twenty and an adult and you couldn't make an adult eat.

Neither of them spoke for a moment. The canal water moved in the ditch. The compound's lights threw a pale halo against the low October clouds.

"You should eat more," Yor said. "You look thin."

Anya's mouth opened. Closed. Her eyes dropped to Yor's left hand — the jacket sleeve dark and stiff with blood, the makeshift wrapping already soaking through.

"Your hands are bleeding, Mama."

"Oh." Yor looked at her hand as if the blood were a social inconvenience, like noticing a stain on her shirt at a work dinner. "It's — it's not bad. I scraped it on a, um. A fence. It's really not deep. I just didn't have a — I'll clean it when I get home."

Anya didn't ask what fence. She didn't ask why Yor was here, what was inside the compound, what she'd done. Yor didn't ask any of the equivalent questions — why her daughter was crouched behind waste containers at an arms broker's estate, what intelligence she was following, what professional capacity took her so far past cultural attache that the cover had become its own quiet joke. These questions existed in the space between them like furniture they'd both learned to walk around without touching.

"I need to get back," Yor said, gesturing toward the road to the tram station. "It's, um. It's late."

"Yeah." Anya nodded. "I'm going the other way."

"Right. Of course."

A silence. Not empty — weighted, like a breath someone was holding. The canal gurgled. Something shifted behind the compound wall: a light switching on, a distant door.

"Be careful getting home," Yor said. "It's cold tonight. You have a proper coat? Not just that—"

"I'm fine, Mama."

"There's stew. In the fridge. From yesterday. If you want to stop by. You don't have to. But it's there."

"Okay."

"And the soup. The mushroom one. It's new. It might be — I think I used too much miso. But it's, you know. It's fine. It's an experiment."

"I'll try it."

Yor wanted to close the distance between them. She wanted to hold her daughter against her the way she had when Anya was small and the world was something Yor could physically stand between her and. But her hand was bleeding and she'd just killed five men in a dead broker's compound, and the evidence of something she couldn't yet name was on her phone pressing against her hip, and putting her bleeding palm against Anya's back would leave a mark. And it would mean standing in the open center of a question they both refused to ask.

"Goodnight, Anya."

"Night, Mama."

Yor turned and walked. She didn't look back. Looking back had always been the harder thing, and tonight every hard thing was harder.

---

Anya watched her mother's figure diminish along the service road until it became a shape against the distant freight depot lights, and then just the idea of a shape, and then gone.

She'd come here following a thread — a weapons broker on the northern outskirts whose name had appeared in three separate intelligence streams from Damian's network. The plan had been reconnaissance. Scout the security perimeter, photograph the compound layout, report back. The kind of careful preliminary work Loid had drilled into her since she was old enough to hold a camera: *You look before you touch. Always.*

She hadn't expected the compound to already be breached.

She'd known it was Yor before she'd seen her face.

Two hundred meters out, approaching along the canal, the thoughts had reached her through the thin ambient noise of the industrial outskirts — her mother's mind in a register Anya had only ever caught in fragments, quick glimpses that surfaced and vanished before she could process them. But tonight the compound's population was sparse enough and her proximity close enough that the signal came through clearly:

*Two in the corridor. Closing. Right turn. Office at the end.*

Yor's tactical mind was nothing like her domestic one. At home, her mother's thoughts moved in warm, looping patterns — had everyone eaten, was the apartment too cold, had she added too much salt, should she apologize to the neighbor for the noise. The loops circled and reassured and doubled back on themselves. In the compound, the loops were gone. What replaced them was clean and geometric, stripped of everything except function. Distances measured in steps. Threat levels ranked by proximity and weapon access. Approach angles calculated with the fluency of a woman who had been solving this kind of problem longer than Anya had been alive.

It was like hearing a different person speak. Except it wasn't. It was the same person with a wall removed — a wall Yor maintained at all times, in every room, around everyone who loved her. The wall that let the woman who burned eggs and apologized to furniture she bumped into continue to exist alongside the woman who could kill five armed men in twelve minutes without raising her pulse.

She'd stayed frozen behind the containers while the operation played out inside the compound, barely breathing, the pack clutched against her chest. Her mother's thoughts had come in clean waves — tactical processing, then briefer flashes of the private self surfacing between tasks. A quick involuntary image of the bento box in the refrigerator. A flicker of annoyance at a buzzing fluorescent tube. The detached observation that one of the corridor guards had favored his right knee and drawn too slowly because of it. Anya had read her father's operational mind many times — Loid's thoughts carried the weight of consequence, the careful balancing of options and costs. Yor's tactical mind didn't balance. It identified and acted with a directness that left no space for doubt, and the absence of doubt was the thing Anya found hardest to sit with, because she understood where it came from. The total conviction that the work was necessary. That each person removed was a threat subtracted from a world that contained the people she loved. The math was simple. The simplicity was what made it bearable, and what would make the breaking of it — the evidence Anya could feel her mother beginning to process in the office at the corridor's end — so much worse.

And then, as Yor came back over the perimeter wall and started down the service road, the geometry had come apart. The operational architecture collapsed, and underneath it, the thoughts the tactical mind had been holding back while there was work to do:

*The code. Our code. The Garden — on his records. Nine contracts. Four years.*

And beneath the words, in the layer where thought became feeling and feeling became the thing Anya had been reading in people her entire life: the slow structural crack of a woman realizing that the foundation she'd built twenty years of killing upon might not hold her weight anymore. Not the shock of new information but the nauseating click of a pattern half-glimpsed before — the shipping clerk three months ago, the invoices that didn't match, the handler's too-quick reassurance — assembling into a picture she'd been refusing to see.

Anya had received all of it. Standing behind the waste containers in the dark, close enough to hear her mother's boots grinding the gravel, she had taken in her mother's most private reckoning the way she took in everything: without permission, without announcement, without the possibility of being asked to stop.

She did this to everyone. She'd done it to Damian in a safe house two nights ago, reading the unguarded center of him while he kissed her. She'd done it to Sylvia, absorbing trust while delivering a lie. She did it to her father every time his worry leaked past his careful composure. And now she'd done it to her mother — who was walking alone down a dark road with blood drying on her hands and a crack spreading through the story she'd been telling herself for two decades — and Anya had caught every tremor of that crack because that was what she was. Not what she chose to be. What she was.

The violation wasn't romantic this time. It wasn't operational. It was the same violation it had always been, the one that didn't change shape depending on who she committed it against: structural, constant, built into the fact of her existence, committed against everyone she loved by a power she hadn't asked for and had never once been given consent to use. Damian's unguarded feelings in a safe house and Yor's private grief on a service road. The scale differed. The mechanism was identical.

She picked up her pack and started south, toward the tram station on the other side of the canal, the opposite direction from her mother.

The night was cold. Her ribs, still bruised from the ambush two days ago, sent a dull pulse through her side with every step. She could still feel the fading edge of Yor's thoughts — weakening as the distance between them widened — the tactical mind fully gone now, replaced by the domestic frequency reasserting itself like something Yor's brain did to keep functioning:

*I hope she eats the stew. She looked thin. She looked so thin.*

Anya walked. She didn't look back either.


# Chapter 11

Anya had spent three days inside WISE headquarters, and her head hurt in a way that was becoming structural.

Not the sharp spike of telepathic overload — a crowded reception, hundreds of minds pressing in at once. That was acute and temporary, like staring into bright light. This was different. Three days of reading her own colleagues one at a time, in engineered encounters she manufactured throughout the building, each requiring sustained close-range attention while pretending she was doing something ordinary. Waiting seven minutes by the third-floor elevator for a specific person to leave their desk. Timing her coffee run to coincide with someone else's. Standing near a conversation pretending to look at her phone, except the conversation was inside someone's skull and the phone was a prop and the hovering was the most invasive thing she had ever done on purpose.

She sat in her cubicle on the fourth floor with her coffee going cold and an access log spreadsheet open on her screen and rubbed her left temple with two fingers. The fluorescents buzzed. Someone's reheated fish from the break room was staging an olfactory assault on the entire floor. In the corridor outside, two analysts were arguing about sourcing methodology, and underneath their spoken words she could hear one of them thinking about a dog he'd had to put down last week and the other one worrying about a credit card bill that was overdue.

This was the thing about WISE headquarters. You could read the building all day and get nothing operational. People at spy agencies spent most of their mental energy on the same things everyone else did — errands, grievances, relationships, the small accounting of daily survival. The intelligence work happened in bursts, and between the bursts: lunch plans, parking validation, whether anyone had replaced the toilet paper on the third floor.

The thought-fragment from the diplomatic reception — *the asset inside WISE* — was what had started this. A reference to a mole, from an unidentifiable thinker in a crowd too dense to triangulate. She couldn't go back to that moment and isolate the source. She'd tried, lying in bed the night of the reception, rerunning the memory, but telepathy didn't work like a recording. It was experienced in real time and retained imperfectly, the way you couldn't reliably reconstruct which voice in a crowd had said a particular word.

So she'd started the other way. Paper first, the way Loid had taught her since she was sixteen: before you use the cheat, do the work. Access logs. Communication intercepts. Briefing attendance records cross-referenced against the timeline of specific intelligence that had reached the Renewal Front's operational pipeline. Which WISE operations had the Front anticipated, and who had been briefed on those operations beforehand? Grosz's intelligence — the picture of the Front's military-civilian structure and defense-contract funding channels that Damian had relayed to her — narrowed the question further. If the mole was passing intelligence that served the Front's specific procurement and positioning needs, the access pattern should overlap with those channels.

Eleven analysts had both the clearance and the operational access. She'd spent two weeks on the paperwork before she started reading anyone. Eleven became seven on access timing alone — two had been on postings outside Berlint during key leak windows, two more lacked compartment access to the operations the Front had anticipated.
