From seven she brought it to five, and then to three, through brief telepathic reads of colleagues whose surface thoughts were transparent and ordinary: professional tedium, private worries, the unremarkable inner weather of people whose worst secret was a parking ticket or an unfinished report.

Anna-Lise Berger she'd read in a shared elevator. Anya had timed her departure to coincide with Berger leaving the fifth-floor meeting room, pressed the button for the wrong floor so she'd have extra time, and spent forty seconds standing next to a woman she'd eaten lunch with maybe a dozen times, reading her most private thoughts without so much as asking. Berger was thinking about a posting she'd applied for in Vienna — the rejection still fresh, her resentment at the hiring committee specific and detailed, names she was mentally ranking by how much she disliked them. Underneath that: a worry about whether her mother's memory was getting worse or just her imagination, a dentist appointment she'd been postponing for three months, and a faint persistent hum of professional dissatisfaction that had nothing to do with espionage and everything to do with being forty-three and feeling overlooked.

Anya had stepped out of the elevator knowing that Anna-Lise Berger was not a spy. She also stepped out knowing things about Berger that Berger had chosen not to share with anyone — her mother's decline, her professional bitterness, the small humiliations she replayed privately. Berger had smiled at her when the doors opened. A normal smile. A colleague's smile. She didn't know that Anya had just been inside the part of her mind she showed no one.

That left two. Martin Dreher and Gerald Falk.

---

Dreher was fifty-one and worked in signals analysis on the third floor. Anya knew him the way you know office colleagues — assembled from years of incidental detail rather than deliberate attention. His coffee order was black with two sugars in the styrofoam cups that made everyone wince. He had a photo on his desk: his wife, two teenage sons, and a Golden Retriever whose expression in the picture suggested professional disappointment in the photographer. He hummed when he was concentrating, a tuneless repetitive drone that his third-floor neighbors tolerated because he was good at his job and brought in pastries from a bakery near his apartment on Fridays. He'd been at WISE for fifteen years. He was steady, competent, and the kind of person you'd describe by listing his habits rather than his qualities, because his qualities were the quiet kind that didn't announce themselves.

She found him in the third-floor break room on a Tuesday morning. He was standing by the coffee machine with the particular stillness of someone who'd walked in for a reason and gotten lost somewhere between the door and the mug rack. The mug in his hand was empty. He was looking at the motivational poster above the microwave — an eagle soaring over a mountain with the word PERSPECTIVE beneath it in bold sans-serif — but his eyes had the flat distance of someone who was seeing something else entirely.

Anya poured herself a cup of coffee. She stood close enough.

His thoughts hit her like stepping into cold water.

*—told me Tuesday and it's Tuesday and I still haven't called because what if the second panel is worse than the— three weeks maybe four before it becomes obvious. Kessler said three to four weeks before I can't— Eva sat with her mother for nine months, nine months watching her disappear, and I won't— I can't put her through—*

The fragment broke apart and reconstituted into something controlled:

*Budget meeting at two. Need the Baltic assessment. Focus.*

But the controlled thought was thin, like paper over a hole, and beneath it:

*—the referral. I need the second referral before I can start and the referral means paperwork and the paperwork means the WISE medical plan and the plan flags claims to HR and if HR flags it I'm pulled from active analysis and I can't— I've been paying cash. The cash is running— Kessler's office is at four. I'll tell Eva I'm staying late for the Wilkinson debrief. She'll believe that. She always—*

The thoughts dissolved into something that wasn't language anymore. Fear. Not the word, the thing itself — formless and enormous, the kind that lived underneath every other thought and made the surface tremble.

What Anya was reading was concealment. Active, deliberate concealment. He was hiding something from his wife. He was scheduling covert meetings. He was tracking a timeline — *three to four weeks.* He was paying cash to avoid a paper trail. His anxiety wasn't diffuse or situational; it was specific and structural, organized around a single secret he was spending most of his waking energy protecting.

Her fingers pressed into the styrofoam cup hard enough that it creaked.

"Hey, Martin."

He startled. The mug jerked and the focus came back to his face in layers, like someone surfacing from deep water. "Anya. Hi. Morning."

"You okay? You look a little..."

"Fine." Too fast. "Just a long week. You know how it is."

*Please don't look at me right now. Don't ask. I can't do this in the break room. I can't—*

"Sure," Anya said. "Get some rest."

She took her coffee back upstairs and sat with the certainty building in her chest like a stone settling into mud.

---

She was nearly sure.

Dreher had the access. Signals analysis put him squarely in the intelligence streams the Renewal Front needed — Ostanian military positioning, border activity, procurement channels. His schedule had changed recently; she checked the third-floor log-in records and found irregular hours, late departures, two-hour gaps on afternoons that corresponded to nothing in his meeting calendar. The concealment she'd read wasn't ambient personality. It was active, sustained, and consuming the majority of his mental bandwidth. He was running a double life — secret meetings, cash payments, an information timeline he was managing with the careful precision of someone trained to manage operational timelines.

It fit. It all fit. Surface thoughts of anxiety, concealment, covert meetings, a declining timeline — the pattern was what you'd expect from a compromised asset under increasing operational pressure.

She wrote a memo. Careful, stripped of anything that would reveal her source. She cited behavioral irregularities, schedule anomalies, the access profile overlap. She noted that Dreher's demeanor had shifted over the preceding three weeks in ways consistent with acute operational stress. She framed everything as analytical observation — the kind that any attentive colleague might produce — and hand-delivered it to the internal security liaison, a woman named Kurtz whose office was on the second floor and whose face had the practiced neutrality of someone whose job was to suspect the people around her.

Two analysts from counterintelligence were pulled from the Ostanian desk the next morning to run a focused investigation on Dreher. An IT specialist was reassigned to audit his terminal access logs and communication metadata. The third-floor signals team had a gap in their rotation while Dreher was placed under enhanced monitoring he didn't know about, surrounded by colleagues who smiled at him the way they always did while someone examined his entire professional life under a light he hadn't consented to.

Two days. The investigation ran for two days.

On the evening of the second day, Kurtz's report appeared in Anya's secure inbox. She opened it at her desk, read the first three lines, and walked to the fourth-floor bathroom. She locked herself in the end stall and sat on the closed toilet lid and read the rest under the hard white light that buzzed overhead.

Martin Dreher was not selling intelligence to the Renewal Front.

Martin Dreher had been diagnosed with pancreatic cancer three weeks earlier. Stage III. His oncologist — Dr. Kessler, at a private clinic on the other side of Berlint — had given him a treatment timeline that began with additional diagnostic imaging and ended with a conversation he had not yet been able to have with his wife. He'd been paying for consultations in cash to avoid triggering the WISE medical plan's automated notifications to HR, because a flagged claim would pull him from active analysis and he wasn't ready for that — wasn't ready for any of it, for the offices and the sympathy and the way people would start looking at him as someone who was leaving. The covert meetings were oncology appointments. The irregular schedule was treatment planning. The concealment was a fifty-one-year-old man trying to hold his ordinary life together for a few more weeks before he had to sit his wife down and tell her it was happening again, the thing she'd watched destroy her mother, except this time it was him.

The timeline — *three to four weeks* — was not an operational window for intelligence delivery. It was how long his doctor estimated before the symptoms would become physically apparent and the secret would keep itself no longer.

Anya sat in the stall with the folder in her lap. The ventilation pushed warm stale air through the ceiling vent. Someone walked into the bathroom, used the sink, left. In the corridor outside, two people discussed something about the Denmark file.

She had read him perfectly. Every fragment of his surface thoughts was accurate. The fear, the concealment, the secret meetings, the cash payments, the timeline — all real. All true. All pointing toward the thing he was hiding. Her telepathy hadn't given her a wrong answer. It had given her exactly what Martin Dreher was thinking about, which was his own death, and she had looked at a man drowning in the most private terror of his life and diagnosed treason.

Surface thoughts were not truth. They were not intentions, not identity, not the reason underneath the feeling. They were what a person was afraid of at any given moment, and people were afraid of what haunted them, not what defined them. A dying man's anxiety and a traitor's anxiety wore the same face. Her telepathy couldn't tell the difference because there was no difference — not on the surface, and the surface was all she had ever been able to read.

Two days of investigative resources. Two counterintelligence analysts pulled from the Ostanian desk, where they'd been tracking the intelligence channels the Renewal Front was actually using. An IT specialist diverted to audit the terminal logs of a man who was spending his lunch breaks researching survival rates. Two days in which the actual mole — whoever and wherever they were — had operated with two fewer people watching for them.

She thought of the photo on his desk. His wife. The two boys, one of whom had a football tournament on Saturday that his father had been privately bargaining with the universe to attend. The Golden Retriever with the judgmental expression. She had read the most guarded thoughts of a man whose private world was collapsing and turned them into a referral to counterintelligence, and the referral had been processed by colleagues who didn't know Dreher the way she did — who didn't know the Friday pastries or the tuneless humming or the way he said "long week" while holding his empty mug like an anchor.

She flushed the toilet she hadn't used. Washed her hands. The face in the mirror looked like hers but belonged to someone she trusted less than she had a week ago.

---

Gerald Falk was drinking tea in the far corner of the fourth-floor bullpen the next morning, reading a cable digest, when Anya found a reason to stand near him.

The reason was a printout she didn't need from the shared printer, which sat eight feet from Falk's preferred corner desk. She collected the pages slowly, aligning them, and stood in his radius.

He was the most familiar presence in the building. Twenty-three years at WISE — the kind of institutional tenure that turned a person into part of the architecture. People referenced his assessments the way they referenced standing policy, as things that had always existed and probably always would. He had thinning hair, reading glasses he wore low on his nose, and the posture of a man who'd spent the better part of three decades at desks and had made a kind of peace with what that did to the spine. He wore the same grey cardigan most days, the one with a pulled thread at the left cuff that nobody mentioned and he never fixed.

Anya had known him since she was twelve. He'd been at her Eden Academy graduation ceremony — not in any official capacity, just a colleague of Loid's who came because that's what you did for the families of people you'd worked alongside for years. Afterward, while other parents crowded the courtyard for photos, he'd found her near the refreshments table and given her a fountain pen. Silver barrel, heavier than she expected, her initials engraved in small letters on the clip. "For signing important things," he'd said, in that mild way of his, and she'd thought it was the most adult gift anyone had ever given her. She'd used it to sign her WISE intake forms two years later, sitting at a desk in this same building while her hand shook with something she'd mistaken for excitement.

"You look tired," Falk said now, not looking up from his digest. "You should eat breakfast. You always skip."

*Budget allocation for Q3 is overstated by twelve percent. Same pattern as last year. Sylvia won't challenge the escalation projections because they serve the funding cycle, same as always. Pension committee at noon — the actuarial adjustment needs review. Is it raining? Looks like it might rain.*

Anya stood near the printer, pages in hand, reading.

"I'm fine," she said. "Long week."

"Isn't there always."

*The Wilkinson assessment was sloppy. Geller sourced it from a single intercept without corroboration. Someone ought to redo the whole thing. I'll raise it at the committee meeting.*

She stayed. Thirty seconds. Forty-five. A minute. His thoughts moved through budget irritation, a colleague's substandard work, the pension committee, the weather, whether the cafeteria had changed its soup rotation on Tuesdays. There was no turbulence. No concealment. No secret currents running beneath the bureaucratic surface, no doors she could sense that were closed against her. Just a man thinking the thoughts of a man who had spent twenty-three years in an intelligence bureaucracy and had settled into its rhythms the way furniture settles into carpet — the impression so deep it was invisible.

*Anya still doesn't eat enough. Loid was the same way at her age — all work, no meals. Some things pass down whether you mean them to or not.*

The thought was warm. Avuncular. She almost flinched from it.

With Dreher, the surface had been a storm. Everything pointed inward, toward a hidden thing. The sheer volume of anxiety had made her certain, and the certainty had been wrong, and now she was standing near a man whose mind was a quiet room and couldn't determine whether quiet meant innocent or whether quiet was what disciplined concealment looked like after twenty-three years of practice.

The ambiguity was worse than confirmation would have been. Confirmation she could act on. Silence was just silence, and it told her nothing about what might or might not exist beneath it.

She walked back to her desk.

Falk's initials were on a memo she'd noticed earlier that week — a reallocation of analytical coverage from the Ostanian diplomatic sector. The memo cited her own assessment, the one she'd filed to protect Damian, as justification: since the field report had reduced the sector's threat profile, resources could be redirected to higher-priority channels. It was standard procedure. It was exactly what any senior analyst would recommend when a field operative's report deprioritized an area. The two analysts reassigned from those channels had been covering intelligence streams that, Anya knew from her work with Damian, the Renewal Front was actively using.

She'd seen the memo days ago and felt the guilt then — her false report was becoming institutional fact, redirecting real analysts from real intelligence work. Her lie had created a gap in coverage, and Falk had formalized the gap with a bureaucratically correct memo that made her dishonesty into standing policy. She looked at his initials and felt what she had felt before: the sick, specific weight of watching her own deception harden into something other people acted on. He was following available intelligence. That the intelligence was a lie she'd told was her failure, not evidence of his.

She sat at her desk in the bullpen's overhead hum and understood, with a clarity that felt more like nausea than insight, what she had been doing for the past week. She had been reading her colleagues' minds — their fears, their frustrations, their private medical crises, their small grievances and buried shames — without their knowledge or permission, to identify a person who was betraying WISE's institutional trust through an undisclosed advantage. And she was conducting this investigation as someone who had been betraying that same institutional trust since her first day: through a power she had never disclosed, accountable to no one, appointed by no one, performing surveillance that no one had sanctioned or could review. She was the organization's secret internal eye, and she had been wrong about Dreher, and she couldn't confirm Falk, and the only certainty the week had produced was that she had violated the privacy of four people who trusted her and gotten nothing usable in return.

She opened her laptop and started rereading the access logs, because the alternative to working was thinking, and thinking was not available to her right now.

---

The encrypted call came at nine that evening. One-time cipher, routed through a frequency they'd agreed on — Anya had to dig through the small notebook in her bag to find the current key because she'd mixed up the rotation order and entered the wrong sequence twice before the connection cleared. She sat on the kitchen floor of her apartment with her back against the cabinet and the phone pressed to her ear and waited through three seconds of static.

Damian's voice was careful, stripped of the diplomatic warmth that was his default register. He sounded like someone talking from a room he'd swept for devices and still didn't fully trust.

"The weather's changed faster than the forecast."

"How fast?"

"Our friend at the garden center has moved up the planting schedule. Significantly. There's a neighborhood event being organized. Something visible. Something designed to make the people across the street very unhappy."

The Renewal Front was engineering a diplomatic incident. Something public, something built to look like Westalian provocation. *Neighborhood event* was the operation. *People across the street* was Westalis. The code words strained against what they were holding, like a bag packed too full.

"When?"

A pause. She could hear him breathing. She couldn't read his thoughts over the phone — telepathy needed proximity, line of sight or close to it — and working without it felt like trying to see in a room where someone had taken half the lights. She was left with his voice, his pauses, the choices he made about which words came first. The way everyone else navigated conversation all the time.

"The forecast says this weekend. Midweek at the latest. It's..."

"Days."

"Days. Not weeks. The planting season moved."

The word sat between them in the connection static.

"I had a setback on the personnel issue," she said. She hadn't planned to tell him. But his voice on the other end of the line — careful, tired, not performing — made her want to be honest about the one thing she could be honest about without revealing everything else.

"What kind?"

"I spent two days looking at someone and I was sure. And it wasn't — what he was hiding was personal. Private. I shouldn't have been looking."

"That's two days out of a very short schedule."

"Yeah."

"Do you still have candidates?"

"One I can't rule out. One I can't confirm either. His... garden's very tidy."

"Tidy gardens aren't always what they look like."

She almost laughed. "That's really not helpful."

"I know it's not."

A silence. The kitchen faucet dripped — a problem she'd been meaning to fix for two weeks and kept not fixing, which was the kind of domestic neglect that would have made her laugh in a different month. A spy running a cross-border intelligence operation who couldn't manage a leaking tap.

"If the neighborhood event goes off before we—"

"If it goes off," Damian said, "our friend at the garden center gets enough neighborhood support to change the whole community board. Permanently. And they'll have the backing to do it."

If the diplomatic incident succeeded, the Renewal Front would have political cover for military posturing. Both governments pushed closer to a confrontation that neither population wanted but that the people who profited from tension had been engineering for months. The gardening metaphor was buckling under the weight.

"I need to identify the personnel issue first," Anya said.

"Can you? In this window?"

"I don't know." She said it flat, without the operational confidence her training told her to project. Under the code words and the cipher and the carefully maintained distance, this was what their alliance actually sounded like: two people who had been honest enough with each other to hear the gap when the honesty ran out.

"Be careful." The diplomatic smoothness dropped from his voice. What remained sounded younger, less curated. Just a man on the other end of a phone who was running out of room.

"You too."

She ended the call. The phone was warm against her palm. Outside, a tram passed somewhere below, its bell thin and distant in the autumn dark. Upstairs, her neighbor dropped something heavy and swore, a muffled thud followed by a word she didn't need telepathy to understand.

Three days reading people's private thoughts. Two of those days spent on a man whose secret was his own death. A counterintelligence investigation that had produced nothing except Anya's knowledge of Martin Dreher's pancreatic cancer and Anna-Lise Berger's resentment about Vienna and the specific depth of professional frustration that a forty-three-year-old woman carried privately. And against these: Falk's quiet thoughts about pensions and budgets and whether she was eating enough, orderly and warm and completely unreadable, and a reallocation memo bearing his initials that cited her own lie as institutional justification for moving analysts off the channels the enemy was using.

She didn't know if Falk was the mole. She didn't know if the nothing she'd read in his mind was innocence or whether twenty-three years of institutional routine could make a performance invisible even to a telepath. She knew that her telepathy — the ability she had relied on her entire life, the thing that made her valuable, the secret advantage that justified every other secret — had just spent a week producing a wrong answer, no answer, and a catalog of her colleagues' private suffering, and she couldn't tell which of those outcomes frightened her most.

Days. Not weeks.

She turned off the kitchen light. The apartment was dark and quiet and for once the silence was external — pipes, a distant radio, the neighbor settling down — all of it belonging to people whose thoughts she could not reach from here.

Her head still hurt. But it was the normal kind now, the kind that came from staring at screens and not eating and being twenty years old and tired. She'd take it.

She went to bed and lay in the dark and did not sleep for a long time.


# Chapter 12

The Blackbell estate glowed against the October dark with the restraint of someone who didn't know the word. White lanterns lined the drive at meticulous intervals. The topiary garden had been strung with lights that made it look like a magazine spread, which it would be — Becky had confirmed photographers from three publications, all carefully vetted to make the event look effortlessly elegant rather than the three weeks of controlled panic it had actually been.

Anya stepped out of the car and the thoughts hit before the cold air did.

*—if the valet scratches my paint I'm going to lose it, I just had it detailed, I'm literally watching him—*

*—she's wearing the Moreau. She's wearing the Moreau. I was told I was the only one with the Moreau—*

*—seven more handshakes. I can do seven. Maybe eight if I count the wife—*

Two hundred minds. She let them blur to a murmur and picked through it for signals: security detail — two private, one off-duty SSS officer she recognized from the diplomatic reception — exit routes, and the running calculation of how long she could stay before Becky noticed she'd left.

The answer, from a thought she'd caught three weeks ago during a phone call: *If she cancels on me one more time I'm killing her. I'm not joking. I will actually kill her.* Becky would not actually kill her. Becky did not know how to kill anyone. But the sentiment was unmistakable.

Damian came around the car. Dark suit, precise posture, the diplomatic composure that was both trained and innate. The thin scar through his eyebrow was the only thing that didn't match the packaging. He hadn't said much on the drive over. The operational background pressed on both of them — the mole still unidentified, the Renewal Front's timeline compressing, days not weeks, and the particular luxury of attending a charity gala while all of that hung in the air. But covers needed maintenance. Anya's social calendar needed to look like a real person's social calendar.

"Ready?" she said.

"No."

*The things I do for East-West cultural exchange. I'm going to end up cornered by Kirchner again, and he's going to talk about tariffs for forty minutes, and I'm going to smile and want to throw myself into the garden—*

"It'll be fine," Anya said. "Becky throws a good party."

"You keep saying that."

"The food'll be amazing."

"That's what you said about the reception."

"And was I wrong?"

He paused. "The shrimp was decent."

*She wore that color before. The state dinner. Don't— stop it. Trade policy. Think about trade policy.*

Anya walked toward the entrance and did not think about what she could hear him thinking about not thinking about.

Becky intercepted them in the foyer with the precision of a guided missile in a cocktail dress.

"You came!" She grabbed Anya by the shoulders, held her at arm's length, and subjected her to the kind of visual inspection that suggested either deep affection or quality control. "You actually came. I was going to be so mad if you bailed."

*She looks tired. She always looks tired now. Why doesn't she eat? I'm going to make her eat something if I have to physically—*

"I said I'd come," Anya said.

"You said that about the gallery opening in August and then you texted me from a parking lot at eleven saying something came up." Becky released her shoulders and turned her attention to Damian with the sudden focus of someone spotting something interesting at a market. "And you brought a guest."

"He's not—"

"Damian Desmond." She extended her hand. "Eden Academy. You cried at the eighth-grade science fair because your volcano didn't erupt."

*Oh no. She remembers the volcano. Of course she remembers the—*

"There was a structural issue with the—" Damian started.

"It didn't erupt."

"The sodium bicarbonate ratio was—"

"Damian. It didn't erupt. I was there. You cried."

His diplomatic composure held, barely. "It was a frustrating afternoon."

*Kill me. Just kill me now.*

Anya bit the inside of her cheek.

Becky smiled — the real one, not the camera version. Her dark curls were swept up in something elaborate, and she was wearing a dress that probably cost more than Anya's monthly cover salary, but underneath the production she was the same Becky who'd traded her lunch for Anya's dessert every day for a year at Eden and thought she was getting the better deal.

"I'm glad you're here," Becky said, and the thought underneath was the same shape as the words: *I'm glad. I'm glad she came.* "Both of you. Go eat, talk to people, enjoy yourselves. I have to deal with a crisis — they brought Riesling instead of Gruner Veltliner and I'm going to start a war."

She was already moving, heels sharp on the marble, thoughts scattering between logistics and the genuine pleasure of having her oldest friend in the room.

*Maybe tonight. Maybe tonight will feel like something real.*

Anya watched her go and felt the first specific pang of the evening settle behind her ribs, in the place where the bruise from the ambush had mostly healed but not entirely forgotten.

The gala's main room was the kind of ballroom that only existed in estates like this — gilt frames, a chandelier that could have doubled as a weapon, and the particular acoustics of a space designed to make conversation both possible and overheard. Tables with white cloths ran along one side. A buffet ran along the other. The bar occupied the far wall with the gravity of a small nation.

Anya worked the room the way she'd been trained: peripheral scanning, threat assessment, social mapping. But tonight was maintenance, not operations. She needed her cover identity to be a person who attended parties and talked to people and had a friend who threw galas. Normal. Unremarkable. A cultural attache with a social life.

The problem was that Eden Academy alumni were everywhere.

Becky had been pulling from the old school network for years — the class of kids who'd grown up into lawyers, diplomats, media producers, and at least one person who Anya was fairly certain had become a professional horse trainer, though she decided not to confirm this telepathically. They populated the room like a reunion nobody had officially called: clusters of people recognizing each other across tables, rediscovering childhood hierarchies and discovering they no longer applied, or discovering that they still did.

A woman in green intercepted Anya near the buffet. "Anya! From Miss Henderson's class! Do you remember me?"

*Please say yes, please say yes, I rehearsed this in the car—*

"Of course. You sat next to the window. Third row."

"Yes! Oh my God." Relief flooded the woman's thoughts like warm water. *She remembers.* "I'm Petra. Petra Vogt. I'm in publishing now."

"That's great."

"What about you? Someone said you're with the diplomatic service?"

"Cultural attache. Mostly paperwork and events."

"That sounds amazing."

It did not sound amazing. Petra's thoughts confirmed this: *Sounds boring, honestly, but she looks good. Is that dress—*

"We should catch up properly sometime," Anya said, which they both knew meant they would not.

She extracted herself and found the buffet, where the food was as extraordinary as Becky's three weeks of controlled panic deserved. She loaded a small plate and drifted toward a table near the terrace doors. Damian had been absorbed into a group near the bar — dark suits, policy talk, the particular energy of young diplomats who weren't yet important enough to be interesting but were important enough to be invited. His thoughts surfaced occasionally in the crowd noise: *—bilateral trade framework, that's safe, say that— Kirchner still parts his hair the same way, hasn't the man seen a mirror in—*

She ate something with goat cheese and allowed herself a small grin.

A passing waiter offered champagne. She took a glass and didn't drink from it — the glass was a prop, something to hold so her hands had a purpose. Across the room, a retired diplomat was telling a story about a customs mishap in Frigis that everyone had heard before and would pretend to laugh at again. An elderly woman near the terrace doors was thinking, with great vividness, about the argument she intended to have with her husband when they got home. *And I'm going to tell him exactly what I think about the way he talked to Martin, and if he says one word about my mother I'm going to—* Anya tuned away. Some people's inner lives were soap operas you didn't have a ticket to.

They were careful with each other, in public. They had been careful since the safe house — since the bruised ribs and the split eyebrow and the quiet afterward, since the line they'd crossed and then, by mutual unstated agreement, stepped back to the edge of without discussing which side they were standing on. Here, in a room full of people who knew one or both of them, careful was the only option. But careful had a weight to it, and the weight was worse in a place where everyone else was being careless and happy and slightly drunk.

Once, near the buffet, their paths crossed. Damian was reaching for a napkin. Anya was reaching for a second helping of the goat cheese thing that she'd decided she was going to eat until it was gone or she was asked to leave.

"The cheese is good," she said.

"I noticed you're eating all of it."

"It's strategically important cheese."

He almost smiled. *Don't smile. Don't— you're smiling.* "I'll leave you to your strategy."

He moved away. The almost-smile stayed in her chest for longer than it should have, and she hated that, because she could feel the operational clock ticking under the party's surface and she should have been thinking about the mole, about the accelerating timeline, about anything other than the specific way his mouth moved when he was trying not to be amused.

Then someone got out the photos.

It started with a woman named Lila — one of Becky's media contacts, also an Eden alumna — scrolling through her phone at the bar. She'd found a scanned yearbook photo: thirty children in Eden Academy uniforms, squinting at the photographer with expressions ranging from dutiful to mutinous.

"Oh my God." She held up the phone. "Look at us."

The nostalgia spread the way it always did at parties with open bars: fast, loud, and with decreasing accuracy. More phones appeared. Someone had a folder of digitized class pictures. Someone else had photos from sports day, the end-of-year ceremony, the school festival. People crowded around screens, pointing, laughing, producing the specific joyful noise of adults confronting evidence that they had once been very small and very ridiculous.

Anya watched from the edge of the cluster and felt something shift in her stomach.

A man named Erich Kessler materialized at the table with his third beer and the comfortable posture of someone who'd found his equilibrium several drinks ago. Erich ran logistics for his family's distribution company — she caught that from his surface thoughts, along with a complicated knot of feeling about his older brother's recent promotion and the awareness, carried lightly but carried everywhere, that Eden Academy had been the last time anyone treated him as remarkable for something other than his family name.

*Good to be here. Good party. I miss this. I miss when we were all just in the same building and nobody'd figured out yet who was going to matter and who wasn't.*

"Anya!" He sat down, grinning. "From Eden, right? Becky's friend?"

"That's me."

"You look exactly the same." He pointed at his own head. "The hair. I'd recognize it anywhere. Nobody else had pink hair. You were like the one spot of color in the whole school."

*Pink hair. Class photo. Second row. She sat behind me in Mr. Kraft's—*

"I've been told it's distinctive," Anya said.

"Distinctive? It's a landmark. I used to navigate the hallways by your head."

She laughed, and the laugh was real enough, and Erich was harmless enough, and for a moment it was just a party. Old classmates and bad memories and wine that Becky would insist was worth the price.

Then someone passed Erich a phone.

The photo on the screen was from the school festival cleanup day — a row of twelve-year-olds holding brooms and dustpans with the expressions of prisoners assigned to yard work. Anya was in the frame. So was Damian. They were standing next to each other with the body language of two people who would rather be standing next to anyone else in the world.

She recognized the photo. She'd been furious that day — about the assignment, the heat, the way Damian had said something about her sweeping technique that she'd found intolerable. She'd caught his thought that afternoon, fourteen years ago, clear as a bell: *She's the worst person on this committee. Why does she look like she's having fun? Nobody should be having fun.*

"Oh, this is great," Erich said. He was grinning at the screen with the delight of someone discovering buried treasure. "Hey — Desmond!"

He called it across the cluster, loud enough to turn heads. Damian looked up from his conversation.

"Erich." Smile. Measured. "Good to see you."

"Come look at this. School festival, cleanup crew. Remember this?"

Damian walked over. His thoughts shifted as he moved — *old photos, this happens at these things, smile at the photo, say something about how young we all—*

He looked at the screen.

*The festival. The brooms. The girl standing next to me who—*

His thoughts slowed. Not stopped — slowed, the way a mind slows when it's approaching something it can't take back.

Erich was still talking, beer-warm and easy, flipping between the photo and the present with the speed of someone for whom the gap between past and now was a source of amusement rather than anything sharper. He looked at the photo. He looked at Anya. He looked at the photo again.

"Wait." His face did the thing that faces do when alcohol removes the filter between recognition and speech. "Wait, wait. You and Anya Forger — you had that whole thing in school, right?" He was talking to Damian now, jabbing his beer toward the phone screen. "The rivalry, or whatever. She punched you once, didn't she? During dodgeball or something?"

The name landed in the room like a glass hitting a tile floor.

Anya Forger.

Said aloud, at a party, by a man on his third beer who was thinking about the fact that his best years were behind him and had no idea what he'd just done.

And Anya felt Damian's mind open like a lock.

It wasn't an explosion. It was worse — it was mechanical, precise, the tumblers of a pattern falling into place one after another in the mind of someone who had been building toward this for months without knowing he was building anything at all.

*Forger.*

*Pink hair.*

*The state dinner — she tilted her head the same way, exactly the same—*

An image: a class photo in a box in his apartment. A girl in the second row, grinning at something off-camera. He'd found it weeks ago and held it and stared at it and put it back in the box without understanding why his hands didn't feel steady.

*The laugh. In the park after Bretz. She had the same laugh.*

*The safe house. Her face. The way she flinched when she took the hit to her ribs.*

*The cultural attache who appeared at the state dinner. Who knew exactly the right things to know. Who intervened when the operative had me cornered. Who kissed me in a cold apartment while I was bleeding.*

Then, clear and final, like a door closing:

*She's Anya Forger.*

His expression didn't change. Twenty years of political upbringing, the Desmond discipline — you didn't show what you knew in a room full of people who were always watching. His face produced a half-smile. His hand stayed steady around his glass.

"That was a long time ago," he said, and his voice was perfect, and inside him a controlled fire was burning through everything.

*Anya Forger. Eden Academy. Westalian intelligence. She's been — since the beginning? Was the friendship at Eden — was that—*

*The alliance. The shared intelligence. The terms in the service corridor. She already knew. She knew everything before I opened my mouth.*

*The kiss.*

A silence inside the noise. A held breath in his thoughts.

*What do I do with this.*

Erich was still going. "—and she punched him, right? Like, full-on? During a game? That's legendary, honestly—"

"I think you're mixing me up with someone," Anya said.

She heard her own voice from a distance — bright, casual, the precise inflection of deflection. "Anya's a pretty common name. And I don't think I've ever punched anyone during dodgeball." She laughed. It sounded right — easy, unbothered. She'd spent years learning to produce exactly this kind of laugh, and it had never cost her this much.

"Huh." Erich squinted at her. *She does kind of look like— nah. Lots of people have pink hair now. Wait, do they?* "Yeah, must be." He shrugged and took a drink. "Common name. My cousin's called Anya, actually. Well, Anna. Close enough."

He moved on. The moment passed. The cluster of classmates shifted back to their photos and memories and the specific pleasure of discovering that the tallest kid in class was now the shortest adult.

But Damian hadn't moved on.

He stood where he'd been, glass untouched, and his thoughts moved with the careful deliberation of a man deciding what to do with something he hadn't known he'd been handed.

*Don't say it here. Not yet. Think about what this means. Think about what it changes.*

*Everything.*

His eyes found hers across the table. Half a second. Less.

Something passed through his expression that wasn't anger, wasn't accusation. Something older and stranger. The look of a person recognizing another person for the first time and the last time simultaneously.

*I see you.*

Then it was gone. He turned to answer a question about wine that someone beside him had asked, and Anya put her champagne glass down on the table because her fingers had gone numb.

The party was still happening. The string quartet had started something new. Erich was showing the phone to someone else. Two hundred people were eating and drinking and thinking their small, private, unguarded thoughts, and none of them knew that the woman by the terrace doors was standing in the wreckage of a life she'd spent two years building.

She pressed her palms flat on the white tablecloth and breathed.

He knew. And he wasn't going to say it tonight. He was going to carry it out of this room and decide, privately, what to do — and she was going to stand here reading the process of that decision and be unable to influence it, because that was what her power was. She could see everything and fix nothing.

Somewhere behind her, Erich was laughing about the dodgeball story with someone new. The name — her name, her real name — bounced around the conversation like a ball no one thought was important. *Anya Forger, yeah, she was wild, remember the time she—* Bits and fragments. Nobody connecting it. Nobody except the one person who mattered.

Damian had drifted back toward the bar. His posture was unchanged. His conversation was unchanged. He was saying something to a trade minister's aide about economic cooperation, and it was perfectly appropriate, and the woman he was talking to was charmed, and underneath all of it his mind was running the same loop: *Anya Forger. Anya Forger. What does it mean. What does it change. What do I—*

The crowd noise pressed in — thoughts layering on thoughts, the party's cheerful oblivious hum. She needed to not be in this room.

* * *

The terrace was colder than she'd expected. The party burned warm behind the glass doors, but out here the October air had the bite of a season that had decided it was done being polite. Wide flagstones, wrought-iron railing, a view of the grounds that disappeared into black about twenty feet out. A garden that probably looked impressive in daylight. Right now it was just dark, and Anya was grateful for the dark, because it didn't ask anything of her.

She gripped the railing. The cold metal helped — something solid, something that wasn't a thought or a thought about a thought. Her hands steadied.

Damian was still inside, at the edge of her range. The muffled rhythm of careful thinking, dampened by walls and distance. *What does it mean. What does it change. Think about this.*

She couldn't read what he'd decide. She could read that he was deciding, and the deciding was worse than knowing, because the outcome wasn't fixed and she had no way to tilt it except by doing the thing she'd already been doing — taking his private thoughts and using the knowledge to stay ahead of him.

The thought sat in her like something spoiled.

"You look like you're about to jump."

Becky appeared in the doorway, shoes in one hand and wine in the other. She'd taken her hair down at some point during the evening. The elaborate arrangement had surrendered to gravity and the evening's humidity, and her curls hung around her face in a way that made her look less like a media personality and more like the girl Anya had known since kindergarten.

"I'm fine."

"Mm-hmm." Becky padded barefoot across the flagstones and leaned on the railing beside her. "You're doing the thing."

"I'm not doing a thing."

"You're doing the thing where you go quiet and stare at nothing and say you're fine in a voice that means the opposite. You've been doing it since we were six. I know the thing, Anya."

*She's not fine. Something happened in there. Did someone say something? Did Damian do something? If he did something I'll—*

Anya said nothing.

They stood like that for a while. The party murmured on the other side of the glass. Below them, somewhere in the garden, an irrigation system ticked on and started its cycle, spraying arcs of water across dark grass.

"Good event," Anya offered.

"It's fine." Becky sipped her wine. "The Riesling situation has been resolved. The shellfish thing has been contained. The Brahms problem is ongoing."

"The Brahms problem?"

"The quartet keeps playing that one piece — you know the one — the slow sad one that makes everyone stare at their plates. I asked for uptempo. I specifically said uptempo."

"Did you give them a playlist?"

"I gave them a mood board."

"A mood board. For a string quartet."

"It had very clear visual cues."

Anya almost smiled. Almost. The comfort of this — the easy, particular rhythm of talking to the one person who'd known her longest — was exactly the thing that made what came next unbearable.

Becky's thoughts shifted. The surface chatter about caterers and wine and string quartets fell away, and underneath it something heavier came through. Not sudden. More like something she'd been carrying all evening, held carefully below the performance, waiting for a moment when the performance could stop.

*I should say it. She's the only one I can say it to. If I can't say it to Anya—*

"Can I tell you something?" Becky said.

"Yeah."

"Something real."

"Always."

Becky looked at her wine. She was quiet long enough that the irrigation system completed a full cycle below them and started over.

"I broke up with Marc."

"I'm sorry—"

"Don't be. He was fine. That was the whole problem." She turned the glass between her palms, a slow rotation. "We dated for five months. He came to every event. He stood where I put him. He smiled for photos. He said the right things. And in five months he never once asked me a question he didn't already know the answer to."

*He didn't want me. He wanted the press-release version. The one that gets you into rooms and onto lists.*

"He asked me about my work once," Becky said. "Early on. I got excited — I started telling him about this documentary project, a series about artists in the eastern districts, people nobody covers. And he listened for about thirty seconds and then said 'That's great, babe' and changed the subject to the party we were going to that weekend." She took a sip of wine. "That's when I knew. He didn't actually want the answer. He wanted to have asked."

The irrigation system clicked through its rotation below. Inside, through the glass, the party continued — someone laughed too loudly, a waiter navigated between clusters with a tray of something Anya couldn't see.

"And the thing is, Marc's not special. Not in a bad way — he's a perfectly fine person. But everyone's like that." Becky's voice had lost its performance quality. It was flatter, quieter, the voice she used when no one was recording and no one was watching. "Everyone who gets close. They want the Blackbell, not the Becky. They want the introductions and the guest lists and the table at the right restaurant. Nobody's ever once asked me what I do on a Tuesday when there's nothing on the calendar."

*I watch cooking shows. That's what I do. I watch cooking shows alone in my apartment and eat cereal for dinner and talk to my cat and I don't tell anyone because it doesn't match the brand.*

"People think being visible is the same as being known," Becky said. "It's not. It's the opposite. The more people look at you, the less they actually see."

She turned to Anya. Her face was open in a way that Anya had seen only a handful of times in fourteen years — unguarded, stripped of the charm and the performance and the social armor that Becky wore so well that most people forgot it was armor. Underneath, her face was younger and plainer and more tired than anyone at the gala tonight would have guessed.

"You're my best friend," Becky said. "You know that, right? You're the only person who actually listens. Not the networking version. Not the I'll-pretend-so-you-invite-me-next-time version. You hear me. The real way."

*She's the only one who sees me. The real me. The Tuesday-night-cereal me. I don't know what I'd do if I didn't have her.*

And there it was.

The thing that was worse than the recognition, worse than Damian's thoughts locking her name into place, worse than the cover dying — because this one had no operational excuse, no mission to hide behind, no greater good to justify it.

She had been inside Becky's thoughts for their entire friendship.

Since they were six years old. Since the first day Becky sat next to her at Eden Academy and thought, *I like her hair. I want to sit with her every day.* Anya had heard that thought, and she'd been hearing them ever since. Fourteen years of Becky's private inner life — the fears she spoke aloud and the ones she swallowed, the insecurities she showed and the ones she kept buried, the silent three-AM assessments of herself that she thought belonged to no one. Every confidence Becky had ever shared with Anya had been accompanied by the ones she hadn't chosen to share. And Anya had heard them all.

Becky thought Anya was a good listener. She thought Anya heard her, the real way.

She did hear her. She heard everything. She heard the things that weren't meant for anyone, the things that were supposed to belong only to Becky, and she'd been hearing them so long she could no longer tell which parts of their friendship were built on what Becky had given and which parts were built on what she'd taken.

The thought rose in her like something physical — tell her, right now, say it: *I'm a telepath. I've heard your thoughts since we were children. Every private fear, every silent thing you thought was only yours. I took it. I took all of it. And I used what I stole to be exactly the friend you needed, and I can't tell anymore whether what I feel for you is love or something I built out of fourteen years of stolen access.*

She didn't say it.

She didn't say it because Becky needed the friend she believed Anya was. Because the truth would destroy the one relationship Becky trusted — the one person she believed saw her clearly. And the destruction would be worse because the betrayal wasn't a single act. It wasn't a lie that could be confessed and forgiven. It was a condition. Every day of their friendship, every conversation, every phone call, every silence — all of it, retroactively poisoned. There was no version of the truth that left the friendship standing.

"You're mine too," Anya said. "My best friend. You know that."

It was true. And it was the most dishonest thing she'd said all night. Becky's thoughts warmed with relief and gratitude, and Anya stood in the warmth of it and felt the particular self-loathing of someone who had taken something precious from someone who loved her and could never give it back.

Becky leaned her head against Anya's shoulder. Her thoughts went quiet — not empty, but settled. The stillness of a person who had said what she needed to say and been met with the only thing she wanted.

*Thank God for her.*

Anya stared at the dark garden. The October air stung her eyes. Inside, through the glass, the party continued — lights, laughter, the string quartet finally playing something uptempo. She could hear Damian at the far edge of her range, still there, still careful, still turning it over.

*What to do. What to do with this.*

And Becky's head on her shoulder, warm and trusting and entirely undefended.

And the cold, which was useful, because it meant she didn't have to explain why her eyes were wet.


# Chapter 13

The phone rang at 6:14 in the morning.

Anya was already awake. She'd been awake for most of the night, cycling between the encrypted traffic on her WISE terminal and the anxious surface thoughts of the neighbor in 4B, who was up with a colicky infant and thinking, *please please please just sleep, I'm begging you, just sleep for one hour.* The baby's wailing came through the wall as a thin, persistent complaint. The neighbor's thoughts came through louder.

She answered the secure line on the second ring. Sylvia's voice was flat. Not angry, not shaken — flat in the particular way that meant the news was final.

"Agent Crane is dead. The border checkpoint at Kreuzfeld was compromised at approximately 0340. He was engaged by a three-person team while crossing on forged documents. The team had his route, his timing, and his cover identity. He died at the scene."

Anya sat on the edge of her bed with the phone pressed to her ear and the neighbor's desperate thoughts running through her mind — *please sleep, please just this once* — and she could not speak. The baby wailed. The neighbor's exhaustion was a gray static that filled her apartment like weather.

"His body has been recovered," Sylvia continued. "The documents were not. I have informed his wife."

Crane. David Crane. He'd given Anya his parking space three months ago when she was hauling equipment from a field op, said it with the particular casualness of someone who wanted to be helpful without making a production of it. He called everyone "chief." He was forty-one. He had a wife and no children and he'd been helping her track weapons shipments through Ostania's eastern border crossings for six weeks, running routes she'd flagged based on fragments she'd pulled from a dozen minds at a dozen different receptions. Fragments she had not reported accurately. That fact pressed against the inside of her skull — not her chest, not her throat, but the place behind her eyes where the telepathic noise lived, as if the knowledge were another person's thought she was being forced to receive.

"There is an additional development," Sylvia said. "An SSS tactical unit conducted a search-and-seizure operation at a residential address in eastern Berlint at approximately 0300. The address corresponds to a location flagged in our analytical review last week. The unit was operating on a specific warrant. They knew what to look for and where to find it."

Damian's cache. The apartment where he'd stored the intelligence files on the Renewal Front — the contact maps, the financial trails, the careful documentation of a man dismantling something from the inside. She'd been in that apartment once. The living room had a shelf of diplomatic histories organized by region, and there was a stain on the carpet near the kitchen where he'd dropped a coffee mug and never replaced the carpet because, he'd said, that would require calling his landlord and he didn't want anyone looking closely at the unit. The stain was shaped like a small country.

"The material was seized intact," Sylvia said. "Analysis is pending. The operational picture in the eastern sector is compromised."

*Compromised* was Sylvia's word for destroyed. The eastern sector was where Damian operated. The material that was seized included everything he'd spent months building — the network maps, the funding trails, the names of people who had trusted him enough to share information about the Renewal Front's structure. People who were now exposed.

"I have questions about reporting timelines for your sector that I will raise in the morning briefing," Sylvia said. "Attend at 0800."

Anya said, "Yes."

There was a pause. On the other end of the line, Sylvia was too far away to read — just a voice doing the work that voices did when someone was dead and the institution continued. But the pause itself carried something. Sylvia had done this before. She would do it again. The delivery was not cold — it was structural, the way a building's foundation was structural. The flatness was load-bearing.

"Crane was a good officer," Sylvia said. It sat in the same register as everything else she'd said, but it was the only sentence in the call that carried weight it hadn't been assigned. "We will debrief in the morning."

The line disconnected.

Anya sat. The neighbor in 4B was thinking *okay, maybe if I just hold him differently, maybe if I just —* and the baby screamed and the thought dissolved into raw, bone-tired misery that leaked through the wall and through Anya's mind and sat down next to her own.

She called Damian.

He answered immediately, which meant he already knew. His voice was controlled — the diplomatic register, the one that kept everything at arm's length — but she could hear the strain underneath it. "I'm aware," he said. "My contact in the SSS reached me twenty minutes ago. They took everything."

"Crane is dead," Anya said.

A pause. Then: "Who?"

"WISE field officer. He was running border routes I — routes I was coordinating."

"I'm sorry," Damian said, and it sounded like he meant it, which made it worse, because he didn't know the shape of what he was offering condolence for. He didn't know that the routes Crane had been running were built on intelligence Anya had filtered — that her false report, the one she'd filed sitting across from Sylvia, had redirected WISE's analytical attention away from the channels the Renewal Front was actually using, and that Falk, or whoever the mole was, had operated more freely in the blind spots her lie had opened. She couldn't draw the line directly from her report to Crane's body at a border checkpoint at 3:40 in the morning. But the line was there. The gaps she'd created in the operational picture had made space for the mole to move, and people had died in the space.

"There's something else," Damian said. "Reinhardt called me an hour ago."

Reinhardt. The name landed and Anya searched her memory — Damian had mentioned him twice, both times carefully, the way you mention someone whose trust you can't afford to lose. A mid-level official in the administrative ministry, not a Renewal Front sympathizer but not a crusader either. A man who'd fed Damian scheduling information and department memos because he'd believed, quietly, that the government's hardline elements were dangerous. Reinhardt had two daughters and coached their football team on Saturdays. He brought Damian photocopies in a gym bag and left them in the back seat of his car in the ministry garage. Anya had read his name in Damian's thoughts weeks ago, alongside a particular gratitude that Damian never expressed aloud.

"He's done," Damian said. "He told me not to call again. He thinks the SSS pulled the address from his department's records. He thinks they'll come for him next."

"Will they?"

"I don't know. Maybe. If they're working from the seized material, they'll find his name eventually. I told him I could arrange — " He stopped. "He hung up."

Anya stood in her apartment, in the gray November light that came through the kitchen window and turned everything the color of used water, and she thought about David Crane's parking space and Reinhardt's Saturday football and the file she'd handed Sylvia with the assessment she'd designed to protect the man on the other end of this phone call. She had protected Damian. She had protected his operation. She had drawn a line around the things she cared about and everything outside the line had paid for it.

*Please just sleep,* the neighbor thought. *Please.*

"We should meet," Anya said. "Not at the usual place."

"I know somewhere," Damian said.

&nbsp;

* * *

&nbsp;

The basement was underneath a printing shop in a quiet block of western Berlint, accessible through a service door next to a dumpster that smelled like wet ink and cardboard. Damian had a key. He didn't explain how. The stairs were steep concrete and the railing was cold enough that Anya released it immediately and put her hands in her coat pockets.

The room at the bottom was maybe fifteen feet by twenty. Concrete floor, concrete walls, a steel folding table and four chairs that didn't match. A single fluorescent tube on the ceiling that buzzed at a frequency she could feel in her teeth. The air was cold and damp and sat on the skin like a compress. There were water stains on the far wall in long vertical streaks, as if the wall had been weeping slowly for years.

Damian was already there, sitting in one of the chairs with his elbows on the table and his hands loosely clasped. He looked like he'd been there a while. He was wearing the same coat he'd worn the night she'd saved him from the ambush — she recognized the burn mark near the left cuff where something hot had grazed the fabric. His split eyebrow had healed into a thin pale line that pulled his expression slightly upward on the left side, a permanent look of mild skepticism that he probably didn't know about.

She sat down. The chair was metal and the cold came through her jeans immediately. The fluorescent tube buzzed.

*I should have seen this coming,* his surface thought ran, and beneath it a rolling current of anger and guilt and calculation. *The cache was too static. I kept it in one place too long. That's basic. That's a mistake a first-year would make. And Reinhardt — I put his name in that file. I wrote it down. His name is in a file that the SSS is reading right now.*

She didn't respond to his thoughts. She sat in the bad light and felt the cold metal of the chair through her clothes and let his self-recrimination wash through her alongside her own. There was a coffee stain on the folding table, old and pale, someone else's meeting. Someone else had sat here before them with their own failure, and the stain was all that was left of it.

"They knew exactly where to look," Damian said aloud. "The warrant wasn't a fishing expedition. Someone gave them the address."

"The mole," Anya said.

"Your mole."

The emphasis was not an accusation. But it wasn't nothing. The mole was inside WISE. WISE was Anya's organization. The intelligence that enabled the raid had traveled from the mole through the Renewal Front to the SSS, and the mole operated inside the same institution that Anya had lied to about Damian's significance.

"I know," she said.

His thoughts churned. *Three months of work. All of it. The contact maps — Reinhardt, Grosz's second-level names, the financial routing through the shell companies — all of it. They have all of it now.* And underneath, quieter: *Reinhardt's girls are eight and ten. Football on Saturdays. He coaches because he wanted them to have something that wasn't politics.*

"Reinhardt's going to be okay," Anya said, and immediately hated herself for saying it because she didn't know that and the words had come from reading his worry, not from any intelligence. "I mean — I think he'll be okay. His involvement was peripheral enough that the seized material might not — "

"You don't know that."

"No."

The fluorescent tube buzzed. A pipe somewhere in the wall behind them made a knocking sound — water hammer, the building's plumbing protesting some pressure change in the system above. One knock, two, three, and then silence.

"Tell me about Crane," Damian said.

"He was a field officer. Forty-one. He ran border crossings — document verification, courier routes, the physical side of intelligence movement." She paused. The words were operational and she could hear them being operational and she wanted to stop talking that way but didn't have another way to talk about someone who was dead because of work she'd directed. "He was helping me. He was running routes I'd mapped from — from my analysis."

*From the fragments.* From reading strangers' surface thoughts at receptions and dinners and briefings, assembling a picture no one had authorized her to assemble, and then filing a version of that picture that was incomplete because she'd left Damian's sector out. And the incomplete picture had left gaps. And the mole had moved in those gaps. And Crane had died in one.

Her telepathy hadn't saved him. She read minds — hundreds of them, thousands across the months of this operation — and she hadn't read the one that mattered, at the time it mattered, in the place it mattered. Her range had limits. Her attention had limits. She'd been reading a drunken classmate at a charity gala while someone, somewhere, was finalizing the intelligence that would put a three-person kill team at Crane's checkpoint.

"The thing I keep — " she started, and then stopped, because the sentence she'd been about to say was *The thing I keep thinking is that I could have prevented this*, and the sentence underneath that was *I helped cause this*, and neither of them was something she could say to Damian without explaining how. Without telling him that she'd lied to WISE about him. Without telling him that her lie had created the operational architecture in which a mole could operate undetected. Without telling him things that would make him look at her differently, and she needed him to not look at her differently, because he was the only person left.

"What?" Damian said.

"Nothing. I was — nothing."

*She's doing that thing,* his surface thought read, *where she starts and then pulls back. She does that when what she was about to say would cost her.*

He was right. He was usually right about her, in the ways that didn't require telepathy. He saw more than she wanted him to see, even without the advantage she carried and he didn't know about.

They sat in the cold for a while. The fluorescent tube buzzed. Anya's telepathy picked up faint thoughts from above — the printing shop employees arriving for the morning shift, someone thinking about a football match, someone cataloguing a paper delivery. The mundane world continuing above them while they sat in a hole in the ground with a dead colleague and a destroyed operation.

"We're burned," Damian said. "My Ostanian network is compromised. Your border routes are compromised. The mole is still inside your organization and now they know — whoever's running this — that we're enough of a threat to warrant a coordinated strike."

"Yes."

"So what do we do?"

Anya's hands were cold. She'd put them in her pockets and they were still cold. The concrete bled warmth out of everything it touched — the table, the chairs, the air itself. She thought about the question and didn't have an answer.

"I don't know," she said.

His thought, quiet: *Neither do I.*

The honesty of it — his internal admission matching what she'd said aloud — felt like the most intimate thing that had passed between them since the safe house. Not the kiss. Not the laughter or the wound care or the shared adrenaline. This. Two people sitting in a cold room without answers.

"We're still here," Anya said. "That's — I know that's not much."

"It's not nothing."

*She looks exhausted,* he thought. *Her hands are shaking — no, she's cold. We're both cold. This basement is freezing. I should —*

He took off his coat — the one with the burn mark — and held it out across the table. She stared at it.

"I'm fine," she said.

"You're shaking."

"I'm fine. I don't — " She stopped. She didn't want his coat because she didn't want comfort, and she didn't want comfort because comfort implied that this was a problem that could be made smaller by warmth. A man was dead. "I'm fine."

He set the coat on the table between them. He didn't insist. His thought was small and resigned: *Okay.*

The pipe knocked again. Once, twice. Silence.

"I should've been better," Anya said, and the words came out smaller than she intended. "At the reception — I caught a fragment. About the mole. A thought I couldn't source. And then the investigation — I was wrong about the analyst and it cost us two days and in those two days — "

"You can't calculate like that."

"I can, though."

"Anya."

He said her name — her cover name, the one he'd been calling her since the state dinner — and underneath it, in the part of his mind she was always listening to whether she wanted to or not, the thought was clear and certain: *Anya Forger.* She heard it. She'd been hearing it since the gala, the recognition hardening in his mind like concrete setting. He hadn't said it aloud. He was holding it, the way she held things — information as weight, knowledge as a thing you carried.

She didn't address it. She couldn't. Not here, not now, not sitting in this room where a dead man's logistics occupied the same air as the slow collapse of her cover. She touched the edge of the folding table. The metal was cold. Everything in this room was cold, including the conversation they were about to have about what was left.

"The Renewal Front is accelerating," Damian said, switching to operational register, the diplomatic precision reasserting itself over the rawer thing that had been showing through. "The raid and the ambush were coordinated. That means the provocation is close. Days."

"I know."

"Then we don't have time to be burned." He paused. *Rebuild from nothing.* "We have to work with what's left."

"Which is what?"

He looked at her across the folding table in the buzzing fluorescent light. His thought was simple: *Each other. That's what's left.*

He said, "We'll figure it out."

She didn't believe him. She didn't disbelieve him either. She sat with the uncertainty and the cold and the guilt and the faint thoughts leaking down from the printing shop — paper stock, a football score, someone's aching knee — and she thought about David Crane's wife, who had been told by Sylvia's flat, practiced voice at some terrible hour. She thought about the parking space and the word "chief" and the routes she'd mapped and the gaps she'd created, and the thing she could not say to the man sitting across from her, which was that she had drawn those gaps herself, in a room with a woman who trusted her, and a colleague had died in one.

&nbsp;

* * *

&nbsp;

Damian took the long way home.

He walked east through Berlint's commercial district, past shuttered storefronts and early-morning delivery trucks and the cafes rolling up their awnings for a day that didn't know yet what had happened. The November air was sharp and he'd left his coat in the basement — set it on the table for her and she hadn't taken it and he hadn't taken it back — and the wind cut through his dress shirt at every cross street. He didn't go back for it.

He walked because movement was better than sitting, and because his apartment was only ten minutes from the government district by the direct route, and he needed more than ten minutes before he did what he was about to do. A woman passed him carrying a bag of groceries and looking at her phone, and she didn't look up, and the ordinariness of her not looking up was the kind of thing that could break you if you let it.

Reinhardt had daughters. Eight and ten. Damian didn't know their names. He'd been careful not to learn their names because names created attachment and attachment created vulnerability. He'd learned their ages anyway. He'd learned about the football. He'd learned that Reinhardt brought the photocopies in a gym bag because the gym bag was the only thing he carried in and out of the ministry garage that nobody searched. These were details a person trusted you with by accident, over time, in the gaps between useful exchanges. These were the details that made it impossible to think about compromise and exposure and SSS warrants as abstractions.

His apartment was on the fourth floor of a building that had been elegant once and was now maintained just enough to justify its rent. The lobby had marble flooring with three cracked tiles that the management company had covered with a rug. The elevator groaned between floors. His unit was small by Desmond standards, which meant it was ordinary by everyone else's — a living room, a bedroom, a kitchen where the light above the sink flickered when the neighbor ran the shower. He'd chosen it because it was unremarkable. Nothing about it invited attention.

He locked the door behind him and stood in the entryway. His shoes left wet prints on the tile from the rain that had started while he was walking, a fine drizzle that he hadn't bothered to avoid. The apartment was cold. He'd left the bedroom window cracked last night and the November air had settled over everything like a thin, patient guest that had no plans to leave. The kitchen light above the sink was off, which meant the neighbor hadn't showered yet. The refrigerator hummed. The sound was the apartment's only sign of life.

The box was in the bedroom closet, on the top shelf, behind a stack of diplomatic histories he'd never finished. He'd put it there when he moved in three years ago. He'd opened it once before, months ago. Late at night, after a dinner where he'd performed beliefs he didn't hold for people who genuinely held them. He'd opened the box and found the class photo and looked at the girl in the second row and felt something he couldn't name and put the box back and gone to sleep and dreamed about a schoolyard.

He took the box down now.

It was a cardboard filing box, the kind sold at office supply stores, slightly crushed on one corner from an old move. Inside: a school blazer he'd outgrown at thirteen, folded badly. A handwritten note from his mother, the ink fading toward illegibility. A runner-up certificate from a math competition, which he'd kept because second place in a Desmond household was a specific kind of achievement — not good enough to display, too good to discard, the certificate equivalent of Damian himself. Three pens his father had given him for the entrance exams. "I expect you to return them blunted," Donovan had said, which was the closest he'd come to wishing luck. Damian had returned them blunted. His father had not mentioned it again.

And the class photo.

He sat on the edge of the bed and held the photo in both hands. Standard group portrait — three rows, the back row standing on a bench, the teacher at the side with a smile that looked mandated. The second row, seated. Third from the left: a girl with pink hair and a grin so wide it was nearly aggressive, aimed at something off-camera. She was small — the smallest in the row. Her uniform was slightly rumpled in a way that suggested she'd been doing something physical right before the photo was taken. She was six, maybe seven. She was unmistakable.

Anya Forger.

He set the photo on his knee and looked at the girl and then looked away and then looked at her again because looking away didn't change anything.

The woman at the state dinner — the cultural attache who'd appeared in his orbit with impeccable timing and an ease that felt earned rather than constructed — was Anya Forger. The woman who'd read a room full of diplomats and located him within minutes. Who'd intervened to save him from a Renewal Front operative with a social diversion so precisely targeted it could only have come from someone who understood the operative's mind with perfect clarity. Who'd formed an alliance with him based on intelligence she attributed to her network. Who'd sat in a park with him after the Bretz operation and laughed at his joke with a laugh he'd recognized without knowing why.

The classmate at Becky's gala had said the name and the room hadn't changed because the room didn't know what the name meant. But Damian had known. He'd felt it land with the specific weight of a thing he'd already been carrying — recognition that had been forming since the first night, crystal growing in solution, and the name was the nucleation point. He'd spent the rest of the gala not saying it. He'd spent the days since not saying it. He'd sat across from her in a freezing basement this morning and thought her real name and watched her not react.

She was good at not reacting. She was intelligence. Not reacting was the craft.

He tried to think about what it meant. His father had taught him, through example and absence, that emotion was a vulnerability other people exploited, and the analytical mind was the only reliable defense. His brother had taught him the same thing less nobly: maintain your position, manage your exposures. Damian had spent twenty years absorbing these lessons and he was good at them — good enough to run a covert operation against his own government, good enough to perform loyalty he didn't feel in rooms full of people who felt it genuinely.

But the analytical mind kept breaking against the same set of questions.

If she was Anya Forger, then Westalis had placed an operative near him since childhood. But that didn't hold. She'd been a child. Whatever WISE had done with her afterward, the girl in the second row of this photo had been seven years old and she'd punched him during dodgeball because he'd called her a freak and he'd deserved it. That wasn't positioning. That was being seven. No intelligence agency recruits a seven-year-old to cultivate the second son of a politician who wasn't yet important enough to cultivate. Whatever brought her back into his orbit at the state dinner, it wasn't a continuation of something that started in a schoolyard.

But it was connected. It had to be connected. Eden Academy was where the Westalian diplomatic community sent their children, and the Forgers were — what? He'd never thought about it at the time. Anya's father was a doctor, or something administrative, the kind of profession that nobody questioned. Her mother worked at city hall. They were normal. Conspicuously normal, maybe, in retrospect. The kind of normal that a skilled operative might construct.

The friendship at Eden — was that real? He could not resolve this from the outside. He could only look at the photo and remember specific things. She'd shared her lunch with him once, after his was stolen by Emile — half a peanut butter sandwich, thrust at him with the particular ferocity of a child embarrassed to be caught being kind. She'd defended him, once, fiercely and without being asked, against a teacher who'd called him his father's son in a tone that made it an indictment. She'd said something — he couldn't quite remember the words — about people and their fathers, and the teacher had looked startled, and the class had gone quiet, and Damian had felt for the first time that someone was on his side who didn't want anything from him for it.

The safe house. Her hands shaking as she cleaned the cut above his eyebrow. Her fingers were cold and precise. She'd flinched when she laughed and the bruised rib protested. The antiseptic she'd used stung and she'd said sorry and he'd said it was fine and they were both lying about different things. The kiss — he remembered it with a clarity that surprised him, because under stress his memory typically compressed events into summary. But this one was high-resolution.
